While a focus lies on energy and power supply, it also covers green solutions in industrial engineering and engineering design. Green Energy and Technology addresses researchers, advanced students, technical consultants as well as decision makers in industries and politics. Hence, the level of presentation spans from instructional to highly technical. **Indexed in Scopus**. The registered company address is: 152 Beach Road, #21-01/04 Gateway East, Singapore 189721, Singapore Preface The many vibrant facets of our daily life are sustained by an enormous energy consumption, although this might not be recognizable at ﬁrst glance. This conve- nient society where everything that is desired can be obtained anytime and any- where uses huge amount of energy in order to enable the underlying necessary rapid transport of an overwhelming amount of goods and high-speed transportation of people. Many things are controlled by convenient and easy-to-handle high-spec devices, which have so widely spread that they vanished from our minds, and proceed to work day by day while consuming energy. The degree of this energy consumption is exploding and almost keeps pace with the gross domestic product (GDP). It has been predicted that by the year 2030, the demand of electric power will correspond an amount that is 1.5 times larger than the present consumption, and we are all aware that what is supporting the present consumption are the nuclear power plants. With regard to Japan, which uses nuclear power peacefully, the nuclear power generation is an exceptional business, and it was actually the driving force of the economic growth after war. Because the nuclear power generation is a technique that emits no CO2, it has been in the focus of public attention as a clean energy that uses no fossil resources such as oil and coal and thus has constituted a business that involved the whole nation. The development and widespread implementation of a new and clean energy source that serves as a substitute for fossil resources are essential for the realization of a sustainable low-carbon society. However, the idea to solely rely on nuclear power in this matter is questioned frequently these days. The anticipated solution to overcome this issue is the renewable energy. In addition to sunlight, wind power, and hydropower, the recently popular biofuels or also geothermal energy is the so-called renewable energy resources, i.e., energy resources that will renew after use and never exhaust. In that sense, renewable energy may be also called energy that is usually left untouched. Among the amounts of energy per second provided by each of these energy resources, that is, 42  1012 kcal/s for sunlight, 88  109 kcal/s for wind power, and 0.5  109 kcal/s for hydropower, the amount of solar energy is by far the largest. The energy scale of the sunlight may be easier to grasp with the following data; the solar energy v that reaches the Earths ground surface is about 1 kW per square meter. This is equivalent to the statement that the amount of solar energy reaching the Earth within 1 hour is sufﬁcient to cope with the annual energy demand of the whole world. Even if solar cells are installed on only 1% of the Earths total surface, the electrical power required throughout the world can be generated. Moreover, this can be achieved by simply using energy that has been neglected so far. Can there be any more attractive solution to our energy problem? Furthermore, from the viewpoint of a device, the following feature of the solar cell outstanding; it contains no moving parts like those in a motor. When we get to the heart of the nuclear power generation, the thermal power generation, and also the wind power generation, they all generate power by rotating the shaft of a motor. In a solar cell, nothing moves besides the electrons in the semiconductor. Therefore, electricity can be generated without any noise. Additionally, by employing solar cells we can migrate from a large-scale centralized power generation to a small-scale distributed power gener- ation. Owing to this, the portfolio of the energy network is easier to assemble. The price per unit of electricity (e.g., dollars per kilowatt hour) generated by a solar cell depends on the energy conversion efﬁciency of the solar cell, and the realization of high conversion efﬁciencies is a mission assigned to research insti- tutes like our University. However, with respect to the realization of high conversion efﬁciencies, there exists one large hurdle: the theoretical limit for the energy conversion efﬁciency of a solar cell consisting of a single pn junction, a so-called single-junction solar cell, which cannot exceed efﬁciencies of  30%. Except for the small fraction of multi-junction solar cells designed for space applications, almost all commercially available solar cells are single junctions and therefore are subjected to this limit. Because William Shockley and Hans-Joachim Queisser presented the proof of the conversion efﬁciency limit with the calculation in their work Detailed Balance Limit of Efﬁciency of p-n Junction Solar Cells, Journal of Applied Physics Volume 32, Number 3, Pages 510519 (1961), this limit is called ShockleyQueisser limit (hereafter referred to as the SQ limit). If somebody is going to start with the study of solar cells, he or she usually gets hold of many textbooks that have already been published. It is regrettable that all these textbooks provide only the ﬁnal calculation result of the energy conversion efﬁciency, while the details of this concise and elegant work by Shockley and Queisser which is proving the solar cell limit are not elaborated. This is a result of the straightforward conclusion that the original work should be consulted directly. The various authors of these textbooks are certainly not to blame for omitting recapitulation since the original work is written well, making a repeating explanation unnecessary for most readers. When our laboratory began with the research on solar cells, we ﬁrst concentrated on studying the original work by Shockley and Queisser. Frankly speaking, the impression we had was that the understanding of the process ﬂow in their theoretical considerations exceeds by far that which can be gained through simply applying their ﬁnal equation regarding the operating point for maximum output power. With this book, we would like to make these deeper insights more accessible to those who are interested in deepening their understanding of solar cell efﬁciencies. We focus on the energy conversion vi Preface efﬁciency only and avoid covering widely extended contents as this is done in many other textbooks. First, we are going to determine the conversion efﬁciency of a solar cell satisfying the detailed balance condition (i.e., the constraint for thermal equi- librium with the surrounding implying radiation from the solar cell itself) and show how to discriminate between the factors that cause a limitation of the energy conversion efﬁciency and those that do not restrict the upper limit. Then, we discuss step by step the theoretical conversion efﬁciency limits of several types of solar cells. Additionally, in the ﬁnal Chap. 8, we explain the fundamentals of semicon- ductors. Since Chap. 8 is a summary of lecture notes for the lecture Semiconductor Physics and Electronics held for undergraduate students in the third year, the contents are rather basic but clearly emphasize the concepts that are important for semiconductors. We tried to explain the SQ limit without relying too much on knowledge about semiconductors, but as clariﬁed in Chap. 7, the ideal conditions have their own limited validity due to the characteristics of the semiconductor. To realize high conversion efﬁciencies, it is necessary to remove various restrictions by using either new materials or new solar cell designs. Therefore, the knowledge about the semiconductor fundamentals is essential, and the explanations in Chap. 8 cover also the pn junction which is required for the solar cell. Yet, even if Chap. 8 is skipped, this does not prevent the understanding of the chapters of the ﬁrst half of this book. This book thoroughly discusses the physics of the solar cell conversion efﬁciency and is unique in that respect that the results from frontier research are incorporated as well. The additional chapter on the semiconductor fundamentals aims at supporting those readers who want to proceed to the next step, that is, device physics. By arranging the insights gained by the students of this laboratory and making them accessible to people all over the world, we hope that we can assist the reader a little bit. It would be my utmost pleasure if this book can support not only scientists that are involved in solar cell research and development, but also those many young persons that would like to start studying solar cells. I would like to thank Prof. Yoshitaka Okada, Prof. Masakazu Sugiyama, and Prof. Nicholas J. Ekins-Daukes for the fruitful discussions and frequent helpful comments during our investigations, both being indispensable for the successful publication of this book. Further, I would like to thank our laboratorys students for the hours of intense discussions on the solar cell conversion efﬁciency, in particular Masanori Kawada, Akihiro Takahashi, Aiko Hasegawa, Ryuichi Hasegawa, Yusuke Bessho, Masuki Yamamoto, Naofumi Kasamatsu, Tomoyuki Kada, and Daiki Watanabe. Finally, I would like to express my gratitude to Prof. Hans-Joachim Queisser for his inspiring guidance during my stay at the Max Planck Institute (Stuttgart, Germany) many years ago. The maximum concentration factor is Xmax = 45,900 a Absorptivity: fraction of the incident radiation absorbed by the solar cell xii Symbols Chapter 1 The Solar Cell and the Electrochemical Cell Abstract We derive the conversion efﬁciencies of solar cells under various condi- tions in the chapters following Chap. 3. For our discussions on the solar cell conver- sion efﬁciency, it is extremely important to ﬁrst understand the principle of electricity generation in a solar cell. In contrast to the electrochemical cell, which is usually introduced in high-school grade chemistry, the opportunities to study the operation principle of solar cells are almost zero except for some specialized schools. Most solar cells consist of a semiconductor pn junction, which is a connection between a semiconductor n-layer with many electrons and a semiconductor p-layer containing plenty of holes in the semiconductor crystal. Even if high-school lectures on solar cells could be established, we have in mind that the explanation of how the electricity generation exactly works in such solar cells often will have to be skipped and summa- rized as knowledge belonging to the category of semiconductor engineering. On hearing the rather famous word semiconductor, one may think of realms of science completely different from the electrochemical cell. Since there are too many things that have to be learned regarding semiconductor materials, many of those who try studying the operation principle of solar cells may have difﬁculties to immediately grasp all essential relations. In this chapter, we explain how the solar cell converts the energy supplied by the Sun into electrical energy. For this, we employ the exam- ple of the well-known electrochemical cell, in particular, the voltaic pile which was most likely studied ﬁrst in high school, and compare it with the solar cell from the viewpoint of the redox reactions. In order to easily grasp the idea of how cur- rent ﬂows, the explanations especially emphasize the electron movement that occurs inside the solar cell. After explanation of the operation principle of the voltaic pile on a high-school chemistry level in Sect. 1.1, we explain the principle of electricity generation in a solar cell while outlining the parallels to the electrochemical cell. Finally, we clarify the unique qualities of the solar cell by discussing the similarities and differences between these types of cells, i.e., the electrochemical cell and the solar cell. Keywords Solar cell  Electrochemical cell  Power generation  pn junction  Springer Nature Singapore Pte Ltd. 2019 T. Kita et al., Energy Conversion Efﬁciency of Solar Cells, Green Energy and Technology, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-13-9089-0_1 1 2 1 The Solar Cell and the Electrochemical Cell Fig. 1.1 Sketch of the voltaic pile 1.1 Principle of Electricity Generation in an Electrochemical Cell The voltaic pile is representative for electrochemical cells. Since its explanation in this section is a recapitulation of that what is learned in high-school chemistry, the reader who feels conﬁdent about his/her high-school knowledge may skip this section. We shall start with the sketch of the voltaic pile, which is drawn in Fig. 1.1. Here, a zinc (Zn) plate and a copper (Cu) plate are immersed in the aqueous solution of the sulfuric acid (H2SO4) that is contained in a beaker. It is a construction where the two plates and the load (e.g., a bulb) are connected with conducting wires. Let us analyze step by step what kind of chemical reaction occurs when we prepare such a battery. First, we take some distance from the battery concept and consider the condition with disconnected wires. What type of reaction will occur when the metal plates stay immersed in the aqueous sulfuric solution? In order to analyze the reaction between a metal and the sulfuric aqueous solution, we provide a brief explanation of the ionization of metals. If a metal atom ejects an electron with a negative charge, it becomes a cation. This phenomenon is called ionization, and the reaction Zn Zn2+ + 2eis an example for the ionization of Zn. The barrier for ionization differs for every material, and the series of elements in ascending order of their ionization barriers as shown in Fig. 1.2 is called ionization 1.1 Principle of Electricity Generation in an Electrochemical Cell 3 tendency. By looking at the ionization tendency in Fig. 1.2, some readers might remember the mnemonic Kangaroo Can Make A Zoo In The Low Humid Country More Satisfactorily (Gold) from their high-school time. The ionization tendency in Fig. 1.2 expresses that electrons are more easily ejected by elements that are located more to the left. The metal with the easiest electron ejection in Fig. 1.2 is potassium (K). The metals that belong to the ﬁrst group of the periodic table of elements like potassium and natrium can easily ionize by electron ejection. In nature, elements like potassium and natrium almost never exist as pure metals. Instead, they exist as salts; i.e., they are in an ionized condition and connected with other elements like chlorine ions. On the other hand, the most right-hand side element in Fig. 1.2, which corresponds to the element with the largest barrier for electron ejection, is gold (Au). As gold is known for not rusting as well as being a chemically stable metal, we can imagine that it hardly ejects electrons and thus does not easily react with other elements. The reversed ionization tendency, which is shown below the ionization ten- dency, expresses the capability of an ionized element to receive an electron; i.e., an element can more easily receive an electron if it is located more to the right. For example, in case that two hydrogen ions (2H+) receive two electrons, the reaction 2H+ + 2eH2 takes place and the hydrogen ions form the hydrogen molecule (H2). In order to consider the reaction between the metal plates and the sulfuric acid, we systematically investigate the important elements appearing in Fig. 1.1. As indicated, the metal plates are made of zinc (Zn) and copper (Cu). Through hydration of the sulfuric acid (H2SO4), large amounts of sulfate ions (SO2 4 ) and hydrogen ions (H+) exist in the aqueous solution. Further, a small amount of hydroxide ions (OH) is present in the aqueous solution due to the self-ionization of water. Since SO2 4 and OHare not important for the present explanation, we omit them below. Fig. 1.2 Ionization tendency and its reverse 4 1 The Solar Cell and the Electrochemical Cell Within these elements, Zn and H+ form the pair of elements that results in a reaction. As can be conﬁrmed from the ionization tendency in Fig. 1.2, Zn ejects electrons easier than the hydrogen molecule H2, and the hydrogen ion H+ receives electrons easier than the zinc ion Zn2+. In such a situation, the following reaction occurs: Zn provides an electron to H+. The reaction in which a material transfers an electron to another material is called oxidation reaction. The Zn in Fig. 1.3 undergoes oxidation, because Zn was ionized by ejecting electrons. Next, let us focus on the H+. The H+ ions received the electrons from Zn and formed the hydrogen molecule H2. Such a reaction where a certain material receives an electron from another material is called reduction reaction. Therefore, we say that the H+ in Fig. 1.3 undergoes reduction. The oxidation (reaction in which an electron is given away) and the reduction (reaction in which an electron is received) are reactions that must occur simultane- ously, and thus the combined process is also called redox reaction. The reaction between Zn and H+ can be interpreted as the redox reaction between Zn and H2SO4, because H2SO4 is the source of the hydrogen ion H+. Accordingly, the abovemen- tioned reaction can be represented as the following reaction formula: Zn + H2SO4 ZnSO4 + H2 (1.1) Now, we shall consider the situation after the redox reaction, that is, after a small amount of Zn2+ has been extracted from the Zn plate into the aqueous solution. There still exist many H+ in the aqueous solution that have not undergone any reaction, as well as many zinc metal atoms that still have not been ionized. Consequently, we still have a condition with high potential for further redox reactions. However, there Fig. 1.3 Redox reaction between Zn and H+ 1.1 Principle of Electricity Generation in an Electrochemical Cell 5 exist many solved Zn2+ around the zinc plate. Since the Coulomb repulsion between the cations is signiﬁcant, the hydrogen ions H+ in the aqueous solution can hardly approach the zinc plate as shown in Fig. 1.4. Furthermore, the bubbles of H2 gas molecules that formed on the zinc plate due to the redox reaction as shown in Fig. 1.3 stick to the zinc plate. If only this zinc plate would be immersed in the aqueous sulfuric acid, the reaction speed slows down as the redox reaction proceeds because the H+ ions have difﬁculties to approach the zinc plate. To ﬁnd a solution to this issue, consider the situation in Fig. 1.1. Here, the zinc plate is connected with a conducting wire that passes through the load and connects it with the copper plate. We assume that the free electrons of the metal can unrestrictedly move within the metal plates as well as within the wire. In other words, compared to the extraction of an electron from a Zn atom on the zinc plate, it is easier (energetically more proﬁtable) for the H+ to receive an electron without delay from the copper plate, which has only a small number of cations in its vicinity (see Fig. 1.5). As a result, the Zn2+ goes into the aqueous solution by oxidation of the zinc metal atom via electron ejection in the same manner as in Fig. 1.3, but the electron that was ejected from the Zn is not transferred to the H+ on the zinc plate. Instead, the electron ﬂows into the copper plate via the wire that is connected to the zinc plate. Then, the electron is transferred to the H+ on the copper plate and causes the reduction of H+, which subsequently results in H2 (see Fig. 1.6). By enabling oxidation and reduction reactions at separate places in such a way, an electron movement can be intentionally induced. The apparatus that extracts the movement of the electron as energy by connecting a load through which the electrons pass is called electrochemical cell. Fig. 1.4 Coulomb repulsion between Zn2+ and H+ 6 1 The Solar Cell and the Electrochemical Cell Fig. 1.5 After some zinc ions have been solved in the aqueous sulfuric acid, the hydrogen ions can more easily obtain electrons from the copper plate Fig. 1.6 Reduction of H+ on the surface of the copper plate (principle of electricity generation in the voltaic pile) Regarding the electrical contacts of a battery, we distinguish between the nega- tive electrode, which is the contact where the electrons ﬂow out, and the positive electrode where the electrons ﬂow into the battery. Imagine a dry battery as shown in Fig. 1.7. The technical current is directed from the positive electrode of the dry battery to the negative electrode. Since the direction of the current is opposite to that of the electron ﬂow, the electrons move from the negative electrode of the dry battery toward the positive electrode. In case of the voltaic pile, the negative electrode is the zinc plate, which is the contact where the oxidation reaction occurs and electrons ﬂow out. The positive electrode is the copper plate since this is the contact where the electrons ﬂow in and the reduction reaction occurs. 1.2 Principle of Electricity Generation in a Solar Cell 7 Fig. 1.7 Identiﬁcation of the positive and negative electrodes of a battery 1.2 Principle of Electricity Generation in a Solar Cell In the previous section, we brieﬂy explained the redox reaction and the operating prin- ciple of the voltaic pile. In the present section, we consider the solar cells principle of electricity generation from the viewpoint of the redox reaction. For the explanations in this section, we choose the model of the monocrystalline pn junction solar cell, which is representative of solar cells. We provide a schematic of the pn junction solar cell structure in Fig. 1.8. Both the p-layer and the n-layer sides are connected with a material that serves as an electrical contact, and we assume that the electrons can be transferred without loss between this material and the semiconductor material (this connection is called Ohmic contact). Negatively charged acceptor ions exist within the p-layer together with holes that are induced by ionization of the acceptor atoms. Acceptors are charged by receiving electrons from the surrounding semiconductor host crystal. The n-layer contains positively charged donor ions, which are charged by ejecting electrons in order to form covalent bonds with the surrounding semiconductor host crystal, and the excess electrons ejected from the donors. In the vicinity of the pn junction interface at the center, the holes induced by the acceptors and the excess electrons induced by the donors will recombine and thus annihilate each other. Hence, a depletion region without holes and excess electrons extends around the junction interface. When we focus on the region outside the depletion region, we ﬁnd that the charge neutralityinthep-layerismaintainedbecausethenumberofacceptorionsandholesis equal. Similarly, the charge neutrality in the n-layer is maintained because the number of donor ions and excess electrons is equal. Since charge neutrality is established while being unpolarized, the two regions to the left and right of the depletion layer are sometimes also called neutral regions. On the other hand, although the charge neutrality is maintained in the depletion region as a whole (due to the same number of acceptor ions and donor ions), there exists a strong internal electric ﬁeld inside the depletion region. This is because the donors and acceptors are located in the p- and n-layers, respectively. 8 1 The Solar Cell and the Electrochemical Cell Fig. 1.8 Schematic of the pn junction solar cell structure The excess electrons can move without any restrictions inside the unpolarized semiconductorvolume,theelectricalcontactwithOhmicbehavior,andalsoinsidethe conducting wire. However, since the holes are actually missing valence electrons and formed within the covalent bonds of the semiconductor host crystal, they can only exist in the semiconductors covalent bonds. In other words, the holes cannot travel through the metallic parts and stay inside the semiconductor material. This situation can easily be imagined if we relate it with the hydrogen ions explained in the previous section about the voltaic pile. While the electrons were able to move freely inside the metal plates and the wire, the H+ cations could only exist in the aqueous solution. The holes and excess electrons in the p- and n-layers shown in Fig. 1.8 are carriers that originate from acceptors and donors, respectively, which are impurities used for doping. Besides the carrier generation that results from impurities, the carrier generation employing the energy of light (this is also called photoexcitation of carriers) is extremely important for the solar cells principle of electricity generation. In Fig. 1.9, we depict the process that occurs if light with sufﬁciently high energy illuminates a semiconductor atom. The criterion to decide what energy of light is sufﬁciently high is explained in detail in Chap. 3. For the sake of simplicity, only one atom is clearly depicted in Fig. 1.9, but we assume that this atom forms covalent bonds with other semiconductor atoms in its vicinity (shown as gray atoms in the top panel). Without loss of generality, the reader may consider it as a silicon (Si) atom, a typical semiconductor. The eight 1.2 Principle of Electricity Generation in a Solar Cell 9 Fig. 1.9 Photoexcitation of a carrier in case of light being incident on a semiconductor atom (e.g., Si) electrons that circulate around the atom depict the electrons that contribute to the covalent bonds. In case that light with sufﬁciently high energy is incident on the semiconductor atom, an electron from the covalent bond is ejected due to the energy of the light and becomes a free electron. The phenomenon of an electron becoming a free electron in a semiconductor in such a way, i.e., to be ejected from the covalent bond by receiving energy from external light, is called photoexcitation of the electron. The missing valence electron that is formed in the covalent bond upon generating the photoexcited electron is treated as a hole with a positive charge. Let us represent the abovementioned photoexcitation of carriers with a chemical reaction formula. In case that the semiconductor atom is silicon (Si), the chemical reaction formula of the photoexcitation can be written as shown in Eq. (1.2). Si Si+ + e - (1.2) Now, if we apply the concept of the redox reaction to Si, the photoexcitation of the electron can also be considered as a type of oxidation, because Si was ionized (Si+) by ejecting an electron. Figure 1.10 shows the situation inside the solar cell after the electron and the hole were generated by incident light. According to quantum mechanics, it can be considered that particles like electrons have no individual character. Therefore, we cannot distinguish between electrons and holes that are generated by photoexcitation and those generated via doping with impurities. In other words, this means that there is no essential difference between the hole that exists in the p-layer due to the impurity and the hole generated by photoexcitation in the area indicated with the red dotted ellipse in the neutral region of Fig. 1.10. We now proceed explaining the principle of the electricity generation in a solar cell while focusing on the movement of the electron after its generation in the 10 1 The Solar Cell and the Electrochemical Cell Fig. 1.10 Condition inside the solar cell after generation of an electronhole pair in the p-layer by photoexcitation p-layer shown in Fig. 1.10. In Fig. 1.11, we depict the condition that establishes inside the solar cell during illumination with light. With the itemized explanation below, we clarify the chronological sequence of the electron movement. The item numbers correspond to the numbers provided in Fig. 1.11. We suggest to conﬁrm the corresponding electron movement in Fig. 1.11 while reading the itemized explana- tion.  The photoexcited excess electron diffuses inside the neutral region of the p-layer without being inﬂuenced by electrical forces.  If the excess electron collides with a hole during the diffusion movement in the p- layer, the electron recombines with the hole and is annihilated; that is, the electron stops beinganexcess electronandagainbecomes avalenceelectron. This increases the solar cells temperature (see Sect. 6.7).  If the electron does not collide with any hole in the neutral region of the p-layer, it can reach the depletion region as a result of random movement. As stated above, an internal electric ﬁeld exists in the depletion region, which is induced by the asymmetric distribution of acceptor and donor ions. Since the electron is a nega- tively charged particle, it is repelled by the acceptor ions and attracted by donor ions. The electron is immediately accelerated by the internal electric ﬁeld upon reaching the depletion region and swept into the n-layer side.  In case that the electron reaches the n-layer, the electron density inside the n- layer becomes larger than that before light illumination. It is known that when the density of a particle becomes higher, the particle tries to diffuse toward a location 1.2 Principle of Electricity Generation in a Solar Cell 11 Fig. 1.11 Electron movement inside the solar cell during electricity generation with a lower density. In the present case of the n-layer, the electron can hardly diffuse toward the pn junction (i.e., the region around the interface between n- and p-layers), because the electron is pushed back by the internal electric ﬁeld in the depletion layer if it tries to move to that side. On the other hand, in case of diffusion toward the side of the electrical contact attached to the n-layer, there is no electric ﬁeld or other factors that would impede the electron movement. As a result, the electron diffuses from the n-layer into the electrode.  The electron, which was driven by diffusion, reaches the side of the p-layer by traveling through the conducting wire and passing the electrical load.  The electron that entered the p-layer recombines after collision with a hole. When we represent the recombination between electron and hole with a reaction formula for the semiconductor atom Si, as we have done with the photoexcitation of the electron in Eq. (1.2), we obtain Eq. (1.3). Si+ + e - Si (1.3) When we focus on Si, the recombination of the electron with the hole is a reaction where Si+, which contains the hole, receives the electron that came from the wire and thus becomes an uncharged Si atom. Therefore, we can treat this recombination as a reduction reaction of Si+. 12 1 The Solar Cell and the Electrochemical Cell The mechanisms explained above constitute the principle of electricity generation in a solar cell. The logic for the plus/minus assignment of the electrodes of a solar cell is exactly the same as that for the electrochemical cell; that is, the electrical contact on the n-layer side where the electrons ﬂow out is the negative electrode, and the contact of the p-layer where the electrons enter the cell is the positive electrode. 1.3 Comparison Between Electrochemical Cell and Solar Cell In Sect. 1.1, we recapitulated the electrochemical cell, and in Sect. 1.2 we provided an explanation about the solar cell with aid of the redox reaction concept. When we analyze the reactions that occur in a solar cell during electricity generation by employing the concept of the redox reaction in the voltaic pile, it will become clear that there exist many parallels. In the voltaic pile, the location where the oxidation reaction occurs is on the zinc plate, and that of the reduction reaction is on the copper plate. The reader should bear in mind that the important point here is the spatial separation of oxidation and reduction and that the reduction occurs after the electron passed the wire. In case of the solar cell, the location of the photoexcitation of electron and hole (corresponding to the oxidation reaction) is the location where light with sufﬁciently high energy strikes the semiconductor atom. The place where the electron recom- bines with the hole (corresponding to the reduction reaction) is inside the p-layer near the electrode. Moreover, regarding the recombination between electron and hole, there are two possible patterns which are the recombination of the electron before it reaches the depletion region (see Fig. 1.11; ➁) and the recombination after the electron passed the wire and entered the p-layer (see Fig. 1.11; ➅). Similar to the case of the voltaic pile, only the recombination of a hole with the electron that entered the p-layer after passing the wire contributes to the extraction of energy as electrical power. We may try to imagine the recombination of the electron before it reaches the depletion region in terms of the voltaic pile. The analogy to this recombination process would be the reduction reaction of H+ on the zinc plate, which is described in Fig. 1.3. With Fig. 1.12, we provide a diagram that compares the voltaic pile and the solar cell. We note that one distinct difference between the electrochemical cell and the solar cell is that the electrochemical cell can store electric power while the solar cell cannot. Since the electrochemical cell can store ions in the aqueous solution, electric power can be retrieved any time if a load is connected to the electrodes. In contrast, the solar cannot generate electricity unless there is a light source. However, if light itself could be stored like it is possible to store electricity with the aqueous solution of the voltaic pile, then it would be possible to use the solar cell as a dry solar battery. 1.3 Comparison Between Electrochemical Cell and Solar Cell 13 Fig. 1.12 Comparison between the pn junction solar cell and the voltaic pile Chapter 2 Photons from the Sun Abstract The solar cell is a device that converts the energy of light into electrical energy. Consequently, a prerequisite for the derivation of the conversion efﬁciency of a solar cell is to understand the nature of light energy. In the present chapter, we clarify what energy is contained in light and explain the characteristics of the light that is emitted from the sun. Keywords Photon  Sunlight  Black-body radiation 2.1 The Wavelength of Light and Its Energy Light is a type of electromagnetic wave and is known to possess both a wave-like nature and also a particle-like nature. The former nature means that light moves in space while oscillating like a wave, and the latter nature concerns the particle-like behavior that appears due to quantization of the electromagnetic ﬁeld. Therefore, it can be considered as a wave and it can be also treated as a particle. When we treat the light in terms of particles, we call these particles photons. The energy of each single particle (photon) is proportional to the frequency of the photon, and the total power of some monochromatic light is proportional to the frequency of the photons and the number of photons. Equation (2.1), which is also called Einsteins quantum theory of light, describes therelationbetweenthelightfrequency,ν,andtheenergyoflight,E.Theconstantthat relates these two physical quantities is the Planck constant, h (= 4.14  1015eV s). This equation postulates that the light, which was originally considered as a wave only, is actually built up by grains called photons. Equation (2.2) is an extremely fundamental equation that describes the relation between the frequency ν of the wave (unit: Hz), the propagation speed c of the wave (unit: m/s), and the wavelength λ (unit: m). With this equation, we can calculate how many oscillations a wave performs per second by dividing the distance that the  Springer Nature Singapore Pte Ltd. 2019 T. Since the wave considered here is light, the speed of the wave equals the speed of light, c (= 3.0  108m/s in vacuum). E = hν (2.1) ν = c λ (2.2) We can directly express the relation between energy and wavelength by inserting the general equation for waves, Eq. (2.2), into Eq. (2.1) which relates the light with a particle, resulting in Eq. (2.3). The Planck constant h and the speed of light in vacuum c are constants, and their multiplication results in about 1240  109eV m. Equation (2.4) shifts the focus on the wavelength of light and its energy through substituting the term hc in Eq. (2.3) by the constant evaluated above. hc λ = E (2.3) 1240 λ (nm) = E (eV) (2.4) From Eq. (2.4), we ﬁnd that the wavelength of a photon is inversely proportional to the energy of the photon. The graph for Eq. (2.4) is shown in Fig. 2.1. The horizontal axis is the light wavelength, and the vertical axis shows the energy that is conveyed by a single photon. The common names used to assign the different wavelength regimes are also provided in Fig. 2.1. This ﬁgure visualizes that the energy of a photon is large for short light wave- lengths, and becomes smaller for longer wavelengths. As shown in Fig. 2.1, the wavelength range of the light that can be perceived by the human eye (the visible light) is about 400800 nm (n expresses the multiplication by 109 and is read as nano; 1 nm equals 109 m),1 which corresponds to about 3.11.6 eV in terms of energy. The ultraviolet rays possess energies that are higher than that of the visible light. These rays belong to the energy region above 3.1 eV, which corresponds to wave- lengths below 400 nm, and directly affect the cells of the skin, for example, they can lead to sunburns. 1The SI preﬁxes: 1021 (Zeta Z), 1015 (Peta P), 1012 (Tera T), 109 (Giga G), 106 (Mega M), 103 (kilo k), 103 (milli m), 106 (micro µ), 109 (nano n), 1012 (pico p), 1015 (femto f), 1018 (atto a), 1021 (zepto z). 2.1 The Wavelength of Light and Its Energy 17 Fig. 2.1 The relation between the wavelength of the photon and its energy. The photon energy becomes larger for shorter wavelengths Furthermore, the infrared light as well as the far-infrared light has energies below that of visible light. These wavelength regimes are frequently raised as topics for products like cooking utensils. Although pots emitting far-infrared light are being praised for advantages like enabling delicious cooking, the far-infrared light is a very common electromagnetic wave that is emitted by any material that possesses a temperature on the order of a human being. That what relates the temperature and electromagnetic waves is a phenomenon called radiation. A more detailed explana- tion of radiation is provided in Sect. 2.3, but a brief explanation would be anything that possesses temperature necessarily emits light (electromagnetic waves), and the color of the emitted light depends on the temperature. For example, if you start a charcoal ﬁre for a barbecue, the coals with low temperature emit a dark red color, but when the temperature increases, a bright white light is observed. This phenomenon is radiation. The thermal energy of room temperature (25 C) is about 26 meV, and that for the evaporation point of water (100 C) is about 32 meV. In contrast, most parts of the sunlight we are usually exposed to consist of light between 1.6 and 3.1 eV. Hence, the energy contained in sunlight is rather high compared to the thermal energy we usually deal with. Furthermore, the light with even larger energies, such as X-rays, can directly damage the genes and are therefore very harmful to living beings. 18 2 Photons from the Sun 2.2 The Wavelengths of Sunlight Because the Sun constitutes the energy source for the solar cell, it is important to understand the characteristics of solar energy. The explanation in this section concerns the photon energies and wavelengths that are contained in sunlight. If someone draws a picture of the Sun, the Suns color tends usually to be yellow or white (with some variations depending on the individual). If sunlight, which is usually perceived as white, passes through a prism as shown in Fig. 2.2, it breaks up into seven main colors, namely red, orange, yellow, green, blue, indigo, and purple (since the color changes almost continuously, a nearly inﬁnite number of different colors exists in nature like that between red and orange and further subdivisions). This means that a large number of colors, or in other words, many photons with different wavelengths, are contained in sunlight, and the combination of these colors appears white to us. Figure 2.3 shows the wavelength distribution of the photons contained in sunlight. The horizontal axis of this ﬁgure represents the wavelength of the photons contained in sunlight, and the vertical axis shows the photon ﬂux (i.e., the number of photons that pass through the unit area per unit time). The curve 1, which is shown with the dotted line, is the initial wavelength distribution of the photons directly emitted from the Sun. This curve corresponds to the black-body radiation in case of a temperature of T = 6000 K for the sun obtained from Plancks law shown in Eq. (2.5) in the next section. The shape of curve 1 reﬂects the mechanism of the emission of electromagnetic waves from a material containing heat. Curve 2 in Fig. 2.3 shows the spectrum of sunlight that reaches the Earths ground surface. The light emitted by the Sun reaches the Earth after undergoing various interactions during the propagation in space, like the absorption or scattering by interstellar matter such as hydrogen atoms. Even after the light has reached the Fig. 2.2 Schematic of sunlight that passes through a prism 2.2 The Wavelengths of Sunlight 19 Earth, it is absorbed during the propagation through the atmosphere, due to, for example, dense oxygen and water vapor. The light that ﬁnally reaches the Earths ground surface has a spectrum like shown with curve 2. Although curve 2 describes the representative solar spectrum that is observed on the Earths ground surface under a clear sky, we have to bear in mind that the sun- light reaching the Earths ground surface continuously changes due to atmospheric conditions (for example, weather phenomena like rain). As will be explained in the following Chap. 3, the solar cell conversion efﬁciency is deﬁned as the ratio of the electric output power of the solar cell to the power of the incident light. Conse- quently, in case of a solar cell that is used on the Earths ground surface, we have to divide by the solar power reaching the ground surface. However, the denominator is ever-changing since the solar power reaching the surface constantly changes by the weather. Therefore, the solar cell conversion efﬁciency changes depending on time and location even if we illuminate solar cells with the same capabilities. For actual use, this may be acceptable, but in case of solar cell development, this is prob- lematic. Because here we have to aim at improving the conversion efﬁciency of the solar cell itself, and results like todays weather was ﬁne and therefore the solar cell conversion efﬁciency improved are of no use in research and development. To solve this issue, so-called standard sunlight has been deﬁned for the purpose of measuring the solar cell conversion efﬁciency. This approach evaluates the air mass coefﬁcient (AM), which expresses the distance travelled by the sunlight through the atmosphere, and standardizes the components in the air. Thesolar spectrumoutsidetheatmosphereis assignedtoAM0(that is, thedistance travelled through the atmosphere is zero). The AM of sunlight illuminating at an angle θ with respect to the normal of the Earths surface is given by 1/cos θ. In case of vertically incident sunlight, i.e., θ = 0, we obtain AM1 since we have cos(0) = 1. Curve 2 in Fig. 2.3 describes the sunlight in case of AM1.5. This corresponds to sunlight that reached the Earths ground surface under an incident angle of about Fig. 2.3 Wavelength distribution of photons contained in sunlight. Curve 1 is the black-body radiation spectrum for a black-body temperature of 6000 K. Curve 2 is the actual AM1.5G solar spectrum 20 2 Photons from the Sun 48 with respect to the surface normal direction. AM1.5 is the typical solar spectrum that is observed in a mild climate, and is widely used as the standard spectrum to determine the conversion efﬁciency of solar cells that are used on the Earths ground surface. There are two kinds of standards for AM1.5; AM1.5D (direct) for direct ﬂux from the Sun and AM1.5G (global) taking into account the effects of light scattering. The solar cell conversion efﬁciency that is measured using incident light with a wavelength distribution according to AM1.5G and a light source with a power of 100 mW/cm2 is called nominal efﬁciency (note that this experimentally obtained nominal efﬁciency is different from the purely theoretical concept of the nominal efﬁciency introduced in Sect. 5.1). The nominal conversion efﬁciency has been deﬁned by the International Electrotechnical Commission Technical Committee 82 on Photovoltaics, IEC TC-82, and the commonly discussed solar cell efﬁciency refers to this nominal efﬁciency. 2.3 Black-Body Radiation Some readers may know from their own experience that if, for example, coal or the ﬁlament of a bulb is heated, the color changes from red to yellow to white while the temperature increases. In such a way, any material containing heat emits electro- magnetic waves (i.e., light) by itself, and this phenomenon is called electromagnetic radiation. In particular, the radiation emitted from black bodies such as coal is called black-body radiation. A black-colored material (black body) absorbs all incident light without reﬂecting it. Because the light that can be emitted from a black body contains no light that is reﬂected by the material, we can treat all photons that are observed as photons due to radiation. Consequently, it is extremely useful to investigate electromagnetic radiation by considering a black body (See Fig. 2.4). The discovery and research of radiated light began in an era when the iron and steel industry ﬂourished, after the industrial revolution which started in the eighteenth (a) General radiation (b) Black-body radiation Fig. 2.4 The reason why a black body has to be considered is that reﬂected light can be neglected, which simpliﬁes the calculation 2.3 Black-Body Radiation 21 century and lasted until the nineteenth century. The trigger was the natural question of how the temperature in a hot blast furnace could be measured. It is obvious that you cannot just simply put a thermometer in this hot viscous liquid of melted metal. The technique for measuring the temperature in the blast furnace was subsequently obtained by measuring the light emitted from the blast furnace and revealing the relationship between the materials temperature and the light wavelength. As a result of various researches, it has been revealed that Eq. (2.5) describes the relationship between the materials temperature and the light emitted by the material. g(ν, T ) = 8πh c3 ν3 exp  hν kT  1, (2.5) Equation (2.5) is called Plancks law for the electromagnetic radiation emitted by a black body. Since its introduction should be also considered as the advent of quantum mechanics, it is an extremely important relation. Here, h is the Planck constant in units of J s, c is the speed of light in units of m/s, ν is the frequency (unit Hz), k is the Boltzmann constant (unit J/K), and T expresses the temperature of the black body in K. Regarding the origin and explanation of Plancks law for black- body radiation itself, these are provided in detail in many introductory textbooks on quantum mechanics. Therefore, the present book simply utilizes the equation and the envelope that is provided in the graph for this relation. As shown in Fig. 2.4, it is considered that the black-body radiation is independent of the characteristics of the material that constitutes the black body (except for being perfectly black, which is a rather strict condition). Therefore, the black-body radiation energy distribution is a function that depends only on the frequency ν and the temperature T. In order to better grasp the physical signiﬁcance of the black-body radiation g(ν, T ), let us analyze its unit. Because the unit of the frequency, Hz, can be also expressed as 1/s, the unit of g(ν, T ) becomes [h]  [ν]3 [c]3 = J s Hz3 (m/s)3 = J m3 Hz From this, we can verify that g(ν, T ) is the energy (J) radiated from the black body per unit frequency (1/Hz) and unit volume (1/m3). Figure 2.5 shows several graphs to describe the relation between the black-body radiation spectrum and the temperature of the material. Here, we employed the tem- peratures T = 2000, 4000, and 6000 K for the black body. For increasing black-body temperatures, the intensity of the light becomes stronger while the peak position of the radiation spectrum transits to the short- wavelength side. Since the light directly emitted from the Sun closely resembles the radiation from a black body with an absolute temperature of 6000 K, the black-body radiation spectrum for 6000 K is frequently employed to approximate the incident light in solar cell simulations. The calculations for the solar cells in this book are 22 2 Photons from the Sun Fig. 2.5 The black-body radiation spectra for different temperatures of the black-body material. As the temperature of a material increases, the intensity of the radiated light increases and the peak position shifts toward shorter wavelengths also performed by approximating the incident sunlight spectrum by the black-body radiation spectrum for 6000 K. As a supplementary exercise to become familiar with the radiation phenomenon, we shall calculate the wavelength of the intensity peak position of the electromagnetic radiation emitted by our body. Our body has a temperature of about 36 C, which is 319 K in terms of the absolute temperature. We set T in Eq. (2.5) to 319 K, and then scan the frequency ν in order to ﬁnd the maximum intensity of g(ν, T ), which is the light radiated from the material. When we perform this calculation, the maximum value is found near ν = 1.9  1013 Hz. In terms of wavelength, the intensity of the electromagnetic radiation emitted from the human body with a temperature of 36 C is strongest at a wavelength of about 16 µm (=16000 nm). The electromagnetic radiation emitted from the Sun has its maximum intensity in the wavelength range of 500800 nm. The wavelength of 16 µm is classiﬁed as light from the mid-infrared range. Since the light intensity of the radiation becomes stronger for higher temperatures, the radiation emitted by the human body is far weaker than the sunlight. By employing this relation between temperature and intensity peak position, the thermographic camera measures the weak radiation emitted from materials contain- ing heat, like the human body, and then determines the temperature. With Eq. (2.5), we introduced Plancks fundamental law for electromagnetic radi- ation. However, it is obvious that not the total radiation emitted by the Sun is actu- ally striking the Earth. The detailed balance limit which appears in Chap. 4 and the following chapters requires a concept called solid angle for its calculation. The consideration of the solid angle is essential for the evaluation of the ratio between light reaching the Earth and the total electromagnetic radiation emitted by the Sun. To solve this problem, we rewrite Plancks law for the black-body radiation in a way to include also a photon ﬂux that accounts for the solid angle. First, we deﬁne and explain the meaning of the solid angle in the next section. In the last part of this chapter, we derive the photon ﬂux that accounts for the solid angle. 2.4 Deﬁnition of the Solid Angle 23 Fig. 2.6 Deﬁnition of the solid angle. Consider a cone whose apex is in the center of a sphere with radius r and assume that it cuts out an area A from the surface of the sphere. The solid angle ω expresses the degree of the opening as seen from the apex 2.4 Deﬁnition of the Solid Angle For the deﬁnition of the solid angle, consider a sphere with radius r as shown in Fig. 2.6. Assume that a cone with apex in the center of the sphere deﬁnes a certain partial area on the surface of the sphere. When A is assigned to that area, the solid angle ω is given by Eq. (2.6). ω A r2 (sr) (2.6) This means that the solid angle is the partial surface on the unit sphere. Its unit can be expressed as steradian (sr). In simple terms, we can consider the solid angle as the extension of the planar angle into three dimensions. 2.5 The Photon Flux from a Black Body With the above, we deﬁned the solid angle, and now we shall derive the photon ﬂux of black-body radiation that accounts for the solid angle. In order to discuss the number of photons instead of the photon energy, we ﬁrst divide the light intensity g(ν, T ) of the black-body radiation [shown in Eq. (2.5)] by the photon energy hν. 24 2 Photons from the Sun g(ν, T )  1 hν = 8π c3 ν2 exp  hν kT  1 (2.7) In the explanation for Eq. (2.1) in Sect. 2.1, we already noted that hν is the energy of a single photon with frequency ν. Therefore, the right-hand side of Eq. (2.7) is the number of photons per unit frequency per unit volume. In the following, we derive the photon ﬂux. Flux is a physical property of ﬂowing objects (in this case the photons) and expresses the amount (in this case the number of photons) that is transferred through a certain area per unit time. We require two additional parameters to describe the photon ﬂux, namely the photon velocity and the propagation direction of the photons. The photon velocity is of course the speed of light, and the direction of the photon emission from a black-body sphere has a spherical symmetry. A spherical symmetry means that for any sphere with arbitrary radius, we can consider the energy distribution of the photons on the spheres surface as being constant. The value of the total solid angle is equal to the maximum A (i.e., the surface of the sphere) divided by the square of the spheres radius. Using integration, this can be expressed as shown in Eq. (2.8). dω = 4πr2 r2 = 4π (2.8) To derive the photon ﬂux, we multiply Eq. (2.7) by the speed of light, and to obtain the value per unit solid angle, we divide the result by the total solid angle 4π. This leads to Eq. (2.9). g(ν, T )  1 hν  c 4π = 2 c2 ν2 exp  hν kT  1 G(ν, T ) (2.9) When we look at the units as we have done for Eq. (2.9) describes the photon ﬂux per unit frequency that is incident from the unit solid angle on the unit area. Because Eq. (2.9) is frequently used for the evaluation of the solar cell conversion efﬁciencies in the following chapters, we deﬁne it as G(ν, T ) for convenience. Chapter 3 Graphical Solution for the Solar Cell Conversion Efﬁciency in the Completely Ideal Case Abstract The goal of this book is to derive the conversion efﬁciency under con- sideration of the equilibrium between the carrier generation and recombination that occurs in the solar cell (i.e., under consideration of the detailed balance principle). As a step toward this goal, we shall ﬁrst determine the most basic conversion efﬁciency. The ideal efﬁciency curve u  νg, Ts  , which is derived in this chapter, is the basis of all conversion efﬁciency curves that are presented in the following chapters and thus constitutes an important relation. Besides the mathematical derivation of the ideal efﬁciency curve u  νg, Ts  , the present chapter also explains a visual approach, that is, a graphical solution for the efﬁciency curve, which provides a deeper understanding of u  νg, Ts  . With this visual approach, the reader can intuitively estimate the value of the ideal solar cell conversion efﬁciency without the need of solving the equations. In this chapter, we ﬁrst provide the deﬁnition of the solar cell conversion efﬁciency, and then explain details about the efﬁciency losses induced by the semiconductor band gap, and ﬁnally, we derive the energy conversion efﬁciency of the ideal solar cell. Keywords Band gap  Thermalization loss  Transmission loss  Ideal solar cell  Conversion efﬁciency 3.1 The Conversion Efﬁciency of a Solar Cell The solar cell conversion efﬁciency is an indicator that expresses how much of the incident light can be converted into electrical energy. This conversion efﬁciency can be represented as a fraction with the power of the incident light in the denominator and the electrical power obtained from the solar cell in the numerator.  Springer Nature Singapore Pte Ltd. 2019 T. Kita et al., Energy Conversion Efﬁciency of Solar Cells, Green Energy and Technology, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-13-9089-0_3 25 26 3 Graphical Solution for the Solar Cell Conversion Efﬁciency  Conversion efﬁciency (%) = Generated electrical power(W) Incident light power(W)  100 (3.1) For example, if the power of the light incident on the solar cell is 100 W and the electrical power generated by the solar cell is 30 W, then the conversion efﬁciency is 30%. Electricity generation equal to 100 W by a solar cell under illumination with 100 W would be the most ideal case. However, a solar cell with a conversion efﬁciency of 100% is theoretically impossible. This is because several restrictions apply in the case of electricity generation using solar cells; a certain fraction of energy that cannot be used is unavoidable (Sect. 3.3 provides information on these restrictions). The reﬂectance loss, that is, the energy lost by the reﬂection of incident light at the solar cell surface, is an example of the energy that is contained in the incident light but cannot be used by the solar cell. In case of reﬂection at the surface, the amount of energy that can be absorbed by the semiconductor atoms inside the solar cell is reduced exactly by the amount of reﬂected light, and this is equivalent to a loss. Although this is a general problem, the reﬂectance loss can be reduced to a certain extent by adding a layer on the solar cell surface (the so-called anti-reﬂection layer). However, there are losses that cannot be avoided by any means. The maximum efﬁciency of an ideal solar cell that accounts for losses that are unavoidable is called the theoretical limit of the conversion efﬁciency or theoretical efﬁciency limit. We can characterize the quality of actually fabricated solar cells by using the the- oretical limit of the conversion efﬁciency as a reference value. All losses that can be suppressed by improvements, even those which can be only partially reduced (as in the case of the reﬂectance loss where an anti-reﬂection coating is implemented), are not considered in the theoretical efﬁciency limit and treated as zero loss. The theoretical efﬁciency limit is thus a value that represents the most ideal solar cell for a speciﬁc set of assumed conditions. Let us specify the unavoidable losses mentioned above. For the ideal efﬁciency curve u  νg, Ts  which is explained in this chapter, only two losses are considered: transmission losses and thermalization losses. For an in-depth explanation of these two losses, we refer the reader to Sect. 3.3. In brief, the transmission loss is a loss that arises if the incident light is not absorbed and passes through the semiconductor. In other words, not every wavelength can be absorbed by a semiconductor even if it is very thick. The thermalization loss is a loss that converts the excess energy provided by the incident light into heat. The transmission and thermalization losses are in a trade-off relation that is determined at the time of material selection, which means that if a material with small transmission losses is chosen, the thermalization losses become larger. Vice versa, if a material with small thermalization losses is chosen, the transmission losses become larger. The relation that exists between these two losses behaves like a seesaw. Due to this trade-off relation, the transmission losses 3.1 The Conversion Efﬁciency of a Solar Cell 27 and the thermalization losses cannot be suppressed at the same time i.e., the pair of these two losses represents an unavoidable loss. As a result, the ideal efﬁciency curve u  νg, Ts  is a curve that reﬂects the balance between the transmission and thermalization losses with respect to the incident solar spectrum. The theoretical limits of the conversion efﬁciencies that are derived in Chaps. 35 are those of single-junction solar cells (the solar cell with the structure that combines a p- and an n-type semiconductor layer as shown in Fig. 1.8 in Chap. 1). By employing u  νg, Ts  , the solar cell performance can already be roughly predicted at the time of selection of the semiconductor material employed in the single-junction solar cell, and is not a matter of mere chance. Considering the intense research and development of novel solar cells structures as discussed in Chap. 6, the present status and particular focus of the single-junction solar cell is that of the most basic solar cell (in terms of electrochemical cells, this would correspond to the voltaic pile). And the ideal efﬁciency curve u  νg, Ts  constitutes the basis for the derivation of the efﬁciency curve of the single-junction solar cell. Therefore, if one can grasp the idea of u  νg, Ts  , the application to many other solar cell conversion efﬁciencies becomes possible. 3.2 The Semiconductor Band Gap The band gap is one of the issues that were only brieﬂy touched during the expla- nation of the principle of electricity generation in Chap. 1. In the explanation about the photoexcitation of the electron and the hole upon light illumination of the semi- conductor atoms shown in Fig. 1.9, we emphasized that a photon with sufﬁciently high energy is required to excite the valence electron of the semiconductor atom. But the explanation of what can be considered sufﬁciently high was omitted. In the present section, we provide an explanation on the energy that can be utilized by the semiconductor. This enables us to understand the unavoidable losses (transmission and thermalization losses) that were mentioned in Sect. 3.1. For the following discussion on the energy, we employ a new representation of the pn junction that adds an energy axis (E-axis) to the spatial coordinate axes (x-axis and y-axis) used in Fig. 1.10. In Fig. 3.1, we show a three-dimensional-like representation of a pn junction. The three axes x, y, and E correspond to the direction normal to the pn junction interface in the center, the direction parallel to the interface, and the energy, respectively. The projection in the xy plane shown in Fig. 3.2 is the same view of the pn junction as that in Fig. 1.10 and similar ﬁgures. This view represents the planar coordinates that we can actually perceive with eyes. 28 3 Graphical Solution for the Solar Cell Conversion Efﬁciency  Fig. 3.1 The newly introduced three-axes scheme of the pn junction: the energy axis is indepen- dent of the spatial coordinate axes (x-axis, y-axis) The xE plane in Fig. 3.3 represents the newly introduced energy concept. The areas shown in gray indicate the energy regions where electrons can exist (also called allowed bands), and the white segment that lies between the gray areas indicates the energy region where electrons cannot exist (this is the forbidden band). The energy that an electron possesses increases along the upward direction of the E-axis. Let us investigate the energy axis shown in Fig. 3.3 a little bit more in detail. Figure 3.4 is a diagram that focuses on the energy axis in Fig. 3.3. The electrons that attract and covalently bind the neighboring semiconductor atoms have low energy and are in a stable condition. They are shown on the bottom of the ﬁgure with the yellow dots. We note that Fig. 3.4 is the band diagram of an intrinsic semiconductor that contains neither donors nor acceptors. The broken line labeled with Ef expresses the Fermi energy of the semiconductor. As shown in this diagram, the electrons in the semiconductor completely ﬁll up the allowed band below the Fermi level (this band is called valence band). The allowed band above the Fermi level, which is called the conduction band, contains no electrons under equilibrium condition. In other words, this band is empty. The black line near the bottom of the xE plane, labeled EV, describes the upper edge of the valence band, and EC expresses the lower edge of the conduction band. The difference between EV and EC, which is the width of the forbidden band, is called band-gap energy and denoted by Eg. 3.2 The Semiconductor Band Gap 29 Fig. 3.2 The view of the pn junction in the xy plane: same representation as in the diagram of the pn junction in Sect. 1.2 Fig. 3.3 The view of the pn junction in the xE plane: the horizontal axis is the energy E and the vertical axis is the x-axis of the spatial coordinate system 30 3 Graphical Solution for the Solar Cell Conversion Efﬁciency  Fig. 3.4 Under equilibrium condition, the valence electrons completely ﬁll up the conduction band Now, let us investigate how Fig. 3.4 changes if impurities that provide electrons are incorporated into the semiconductor, i.e., in case of adding donors. By adding donors, excess electrons will be released from them as already depicted in Fig. 1.8 in Sect. 1.2. Because the conduction band in Fig. 3.4 is already completely ﬁlled up with closely packed electrons, the excess electrons released from the donors cannot ﬁt into the conduction band. Under this condition, the excess electrons are placed in the upper allowed band (the conduction band), which is shown in Fig. 3.5a. On the other hand, if acceptors are added as an impurity, covalent bonds with missing electrons appear. Because such an electron deﬁcit can be treated as a hole, holes are placed in the valence band upon adding acceptors (Fig. 3.5b; open circles). Because excess electrons with smaller energy are more stable, they will ﬁrst accumulate in the lower conduction band edge state. On the other hand, the holes will ﬁrst accumulate in the upper valence band edge state since they are more stable if electrons with higher energies are absent. In the following, we explain the phenomenon of the photoexcitation of electron and hole depicted in Fig. 1.9, by using Fig. 3.6 which includes the newly introduced energy axis. When a photon with energy Ephoton larger than the semiconductor band- gap energy (Eg) is absorbed by the semiconductor crystal, a valence electron in the semiconductorreceivesthephotonsenergy.Theenergyofthevalenceelectronwhich exists in the conduction band increases exactly by the energy Ephoton. Therefore, the electron energy suddenly overcomes the forbidden band region and reaches a level in the conduction band. Consequently, the electron is released from the orbit of the covalent bond and is able to move around freely. This process constitutes the excitation of the electron. The empty location in the valence band that lost the valence electron due to the electron excitation becomes a hole (excitation of hole). With respect to the energy axis (when we observe the xE plane), the excited electron and hole exist at different coordinates. However, it is important to note that 3.2 The Semiconductor Band Gap 31 Fig. 3.5 The energy levels of electrons and holes that are released from impurities. a The excess electrons in an n-layer, b the holes in a p-layer of a semiconductor containing additional acceptors of a semiconductor containing additional donors 32 3 Graphical Solution for the Solar Cell Conversion Efﬁciency  Fig. 3.6 The energy levels of the electron and hole that are generated via photoexcitation within the xy plane (that is, the actual spatial location), both electron and hole exist at the same coordinates at the instant of excitation. 3.3 Transmission and Thermalization Losses Caused by the Band Gap In Sect. 3.2, we introduced the concept of the semiconductor band gap. As explained in Fig. 3.6, the electron in the valence band is lifted up into the conduction band by incident photons with energy Ephoton  Eg. However, the band-gap energy of a semiconductor strongly depends on the crystal structure and atomic composition. In the present section, we explain what happens in cases of Ephoton being smaller or larger than Eg. In Figs. 3.7 and 3.8, we present schematics for the transmission losses and thermalization losses, respectively. First, we shall investigate the case of transmission losses which occur if the energy of the incident light, Ephoton, is smaller than the band-gap energy of the semiconductor, Eg. Consider an incident photon with Ephoton  Eg. Even if an electron at the top of the valence band absorbs this photon and its energy increases by Ephoton, the energy level of the excited electron would still be within the forbidden band. Such transitions with a forbidden ﬁnal destination (ﬁnal state) cannot occur. Therefore, any photon with energy Ephoton that cannot be received is not absorbed by the semiconductor and passes through it. In other words, the mechanism for photoexcitation of electron and hole in the semiconductor cannot utilize energies that are smaller than the band- gap energy Eg of the semiconductor. This unusable energy is called transmission loss. Here, we emphasize that the losses arising from incomplete absorption in thin samples are explained with a slightly different physical process (See Sect. 7.1). 3.3 Transmission and Thermalization Losses Caused by the Band Gap 33 Fig. 3.7 The schematic of the transmission loss caused by a too large band-gap energy Fig. 3.8 The schematic of the thermalization loss as a result of too small band-gap energy and a fast thermal relaxation time On the other hand, if the incident photon energy Ephoton is larger than Eg, the valence electron that received the energy reaches an energy level above the bottom of the conduction band (Fig. 3.8). In this case, unoccupied states exist in the con- duction bands energy region below the energy level of the excited electron. These are allowed states for the electron. In general, the occupation of a lower energy level results in reduced total energy of the system, and thus is more stable. Hence, the excited electrons movement is directed toward the lower-lying energy level, and in the following, the electron energy reaches the bottom of the conduction band by emitting thermal energy (in form of phonons). This phenomenon is called thermal relaxation and the time required by the electron to descend from the energetic loca- tion initially reached by excitation to the bottom of the conduction band is called thermal relaxation time. 34 3 Graphical Solution for the Solar Cell Conversion Efﬁciency  The thermal relaxation time of the excited electron is on the order of 1013 s, and this is much faster than the time required by the electron to reach the n-layer by diffusion and be extracted from the electrode to the conducting wire, which are the processes for electricity generation shown in Fig. 1.11. Consequently, even if a photon with energy Ephoton  Eg has been absorbed, the energy in excess to Eg, Ephoton Eg, is unfortunately lost as heat and cannot be used as electrical energy. This is called thermalization loss. 3.4 Deﬁnition of the Ideal Solar Cell Conditions For the derivation of the ideal efﬁciency curve u  νg, Ts  , it is necessary to assume a model for the ideal solar cell. Here, we consider an ideal single-junction solar cell that fulﬁlls the following three conditions: Condition 1 All photons with energy Ephoton equal to or larger than the band-gap energy Eg generate electrons that provide an electromotive force equivalent to Eg (but, thermalization losses occur in case of Ephoton  Eg). The photons with energy below Eg generate nothing (transmission losses). According to this Condition 1, the usable energy that can be obtained from the incident photons is a constant (Eg). Furthermore, since we assume that we can extract the total energy corresponding to Eg directly as an electromotive force without further energy losses, the solar cell output voltage V under this condition can be considered equal to Eg/e. Here, the energy of the band gap has the unit electronvolt (eV), and the denominator e represents the elementary charge e = 1.602  1019 C. The charge of a single electron is e. Using the above, the voltage obtained from the ideal solar cell for different incident photon energies can be described by Eq. (3.2). Vg = 0, Ephoton  Eg Eg/e, Ephoton Eg (3.2) Condition 2 When one photon with energy Ephoton above Eg is absorbed, exactly one valence electron of a semiconductor atom is excited and extracted as current. In Eq. (2.9) in Sect. 2.5, we showed the connection between the photon frequency and the number of photons that are emitted from the Sun per unit time per unit solid angle and per unit area. The photon frequency is proportional to the energy as shown in Eq. (2.1). If the incident photon energy is larger than the semiconductor band gap, then the frequency of the incident photon, ν, is also larger than the frequency that corresponds to the band-gap energy, νg = Eg/h. Furthermore, all photons with frequency larger than νg are absorbed without fail according to Condition 1. And according to Condition 2, the number of excited electrons that can be extracted as the current is limited to the number of absorbed photons. In other words, the incident photon ﬂux equals the ﬂux of the photoexcited electrons. When we multiply the 3.4 Deﬁnition of the Ideal Solar Cell Conditions 35 electron ﬂux by the elementary charge, we obtain the current density J. Therefore, the current density generated by incident light with frequency ν can be calculated as shown in Eq. (3.3). J(ν) =  0, ν  νg eG(ν), ν νg (3.3) In the following chapters, we use the variable τs to describe the probability of generating an electronhole pair by a photon with energy above Eg. In case that the probability τs becomes unity, the incident photons generate electronhole pairs with a probability of 100%. This is equivalent to Condition 2. Condition 3 The temperature of the solar cell is assumed to be 0 K. Although we provide an explanation about the restrictions that apply to the con- version efﬁciency for a solar cell with a ﬁnite temperature T c in Chap. 4, the present chapter considers a solar cell temperature of 0 K for the derivation of the ideal efﬁ- ciency curve u  νg, Ts  . This assumption implies zero radiation from the solar cell to the outside. Inthischapter,wedonotconsideranyparametersexceptEg andT s.Inotherwords, the reﬂection at the solar cell surface, the extraction efﬁciency of the photoexcited electrons at the electrodes, and many others losses are not considered. The reader should bear in mind that the solar cell described in this chapter either absorbs or transmits the incident light, and generates electrical power by extracting all electrons that have been generated by absorption, and as such this solar cell is really very ideal. 3.5 The Three-Dimensional Visualization of the Solar Cells Output Power In the previous section, we provided the conditions that deﬁne the ideal solar cell used to derive the theoretical efﬁciency limit. Under real-world conditions, only a fraction of the light emitted from the Sun is reaching the Earth and an even smaller fraction is incident on the solar cells surface. Using the three-dimensional graph introduced in Sect. 3.5, we are able to visualize the transmission and thermalization losses (See Figs. 3.16 and 3.17). Since the extent of transmission losses and thermalization losses can immediately be estimated upon assignment of the band-gap energy Eg by using this visualization, this method should be very useful for the reader. The energy regions that could not be utilized are drawn in Figs. 3.16 and 3.17 and denoted as region 1 and region 2, respectively. The volume of region 1 repre- sents the region of photons with energies below the band-gap energy Eg, and thus expresses the transmission losses. The volume of region 2 in Fig. 3.17 represents the region of photons with energies larger than Eg, and thus expresses the losses through thermalization. The photons of this region are absorbed by the semiconductor, but the energy that can be used is limited to Eg. The energy above Eg is unfortunately converted into heat and cannot be utilized for electricity generation. This heat has also additional negative effects on the solar cell performance (See Sects. 4.1 and 6.7). The graphical representation of the transmission and thermalization losses shown in Figs. 3.16 and 3.17 should enable a better qualitative understanding of the concept of the solar cell efﬁciency. This is certainly helpful for the derivations of the different efﬁciency curves that appear in the following sections. 42 3 Graphical Solution for the Solar Cell Conversion Efﬁciency  Fig. 3.17 Visualization of the thermalization losses using the three-dimensional graph Chapter 4 Inﬂuences of Carrier Generation and Recombination on the Solar Cell Conversion Efﬁciency Abstract In the previous chapter, we determined the ideal conversion efﬁciency u(νg, Ts) of an ideal solar cell. Since we wanted to know the limiting efﬁciency in the most ideal case, we neglected effects like internal generation of radiation and luminescence. However, the relation between the voltage and the current in an actual solar cell that employs a semiconductor diode (i.e., a pn junction) is incompatible with the assumptions in Sect. 3.4 (these assumptions enabled us to set the electromotive force equal to that provided by the potential Eg/e). In the present chapter, we analyze the conversion efﬁciency of a solar cell that possesses a nonzero temperature. By including losses that arise due to the very fact of a ﬁnite solar cell temperature, the relation between the current and voltage is derived, and then, we determine the open-circuit voltage and the short-circuit current. Keywords Radiative and non-radiative generation  Radiative and non-radiative recombination  Short-circuit current  Open-circuit voltage 4.1 The Solar Cells Energy Input In case of the ideal solar cell in the previous chapter, we assumed a cell temperature of 0 K. However, a real solar cell that is located on the Earths ground surface has a ﬁnite temperature Tc. To analyze the inﬂuence of the ﬁnite solar cell temperature, consider a solar cell with an area of 1 m2. In order to maximize the incident photon ﬂux, the upper side of this planar solar cell is facing the Sun. The geometric relation between the Sun and the solar cell is shown in Fig. 4.1. Because the black-body radiation is isotropically emitted, the photon ﬂux that actually reaches the Earth is expressed with the solid angle deﬁned by Suns diameter D and the distance L between Sun and Earth. Note that this ﬁgure does not represent the actual relative size difference between D and L. Because the Sun diameter D is  Springer Nature Singapore Pte Ltd. 2019 T. Consequently, the energy reaching the Earth is minute when compared with the total energy emitted by the Sun. To express this with a number, we have to employ the solid angle.
