In the following, we consider the inﬂuence of the solar cells own temperature. The fact that the solar cell has a ﬁnite temperature Tc means that the solar cell itself also emits black-body radiation. On the other hand, the solar cell does not only emit energy. In order to maintain its own temperature Tc, the solar cell absorbs black-body radiation from the surrounding according to the energy conservation law. Therefore, the fact that a solar cell possesses a constant temperature Tc implies that it is surrounded by a black body with temperature Tc. For the next step, consider the electronhole pairs that are generated by the radia- tion of the black body with temperature Tc. Here, the radiation from the black body at temperature Tc is incident on the semiconductor, and the probability tc for generating an electronhole pair by the incident photons that have an energy larger than Eg is set to 100%, i.e., tc = 1. A solar cell in form of a planar slab is assumed. First consider only the case of incidence from one side as shown in Fig. 4.2, since the same conclu- sions can be drawn for the front and back surface. Black-body radiation comes from a hemispherical region and strikes the solar cell under various angles of incidence. Consider that the incident light is inclined by θ with respect to the normal direction of the planar solar cell. The contribution of the radiation normal to the slab can then be expressed by multiplying by cosθ. As the incident angle becomes shallower with respect to the surface, i.e., θ becomes larger, the number of photons incident per unit time is decreasing. Consequently, in case of incident sunlight that is inclined by θ with respect to the solar cells normal direction, the number of photons that can be absorbed in the planar solar cell per unit time per unit area is given by Eq. (4.5). Pﬂux  νg, Tc   cosθ (4.5) This partial ﬂux has to be integrated over the hemispherical region as shown in Eq. (4.6).  hemisphere Pﬂux  νg, Tc   cosθdω (4.6) The inﬁnitesimal solid angle d is determined in the following. It is known that the inﬁnitesimal area dA of the surface of a sphere expressed in spherical coordinates is dA = r2sinθdφdθ (4.7) 46 4 Inﬂuences of Carrier Generation and Recombination  Fig. 4.2 Black-body radiation received by the solar cell to maintain its temperature. Since we consider a planar solar cell, the photon ﬂux depends on the angle of the incident light. For incident light that is inclined by θ with respect to the normal direction of the planar solar cell, the photon ﬂux contribution is obtained via the projection by cosθ After a short consideration of the meaning of the solid angle, deﬁned in Sect. 2.4 as the fraction of the sphere surface divided by r2, the inﬁnitesimal solid angle dω can be written as follows: dω = sinθdφdθ (4.8) By employing the above, the rate of the electronhole pair generation by black- body radiation incident from the hemisphere (deﬁned by 0 θ π/2 and 0 φ  2π) at temperature Tc can be calculated straightforwardly. 2π  0 π 2  0 Pﬂux  νg, Tc   cosθsinθdθdφ = Pﬂux  νg, Tc  2π  0 dφ π 2  0 1 2sin2θdθ = Pﬂux  νg, Tc   π (4.9) Equation (4.9) takes only the front surface into account. When we analyze the number of electrons that can be extracted as current from a solar cell, we always have to keep in mind the following four parameters: the rate of electronhole pair generation by sunlight, the radiative recombination rate of the electronhole pairs (which results in photon emission), the non-radiative generation rate of the electronhole pairs, as well as their non-radiative recombination rate (which results in heat generation). Below, we explain these four parameters step by step. 1. Rate of electronhole pair generation by sunlight Upon illumination of the solar cell with light, the semiconductor in the solar cell absorbs this light which generates holes in the valence band and electrons in the conduction band. As explained in the previous section, the generation rate of elec- tronhole pairs induced by sunlight is Fs, i.e., the rate of electronhole pair generation by the black-body radiation of the Sun. 2. Radiative recombination rate of electronhole pairs The electrons and holes that have been generated by light illumination eventually recombine with each other in order to reach the thermal equilibrium. In this case, we can consider that recombination of the electron with the hole occurs faster for higher densities of electrons in the conduction band and holes in the valence band. As the recombination process in a semiconductor under moderate excitation can be considered a statistical process of collisions between electron and holes, it is evident that the rate of recombination increases for a higher electronhole pair density. Here, we assume that the radiative recombination rate per unit area, Fc, is proportional to the product of electron density n and hole density p, i.e., Fc n  p. The electron density n and hole density p under steady-state non-equilibrium conditions such as light illumination can be described by the following equations: n = niexp  Efe Ei kTc  (4.11) p = niexp  Ei Efh kTc  (4.12) For the derivation of these expressions, we refer the reader to Chap. 8. Here, Efe and Efh are the quasi-Fermi levels of the electron and the hole, respectively. Ei is the intrinsic Fermi level, ni is the intrinsic carrier density, k is the Boltzmann constant, and T c is the solar cell temperature. Using Eqs. (4.11) and (4.12) and a proportionality constant α, the radiative recom- bination rate Fc can be expressed as shown in Eq. (4.13). Fc(V ) = α  np = αn2 i  exp  Efe Efh kTc  (4.13) 48 4 Inﬂuences of Carrier Generation and Recombination  It is important to note that Fc is a function of EfeEfh. EfeEfh corresponds to the light-induced voltage V in the semiconductor which is observed upon illumination with sunlight: eV = Efe Efh (4.14) Furthermore, if we deﬁne eVc = kTc (4.15) the Fc becomes a function of V and can be expressed in rather simple terms as shown in Eq. (4.16). Fc(V ) = αn2 i  exp  V Vc  (4.16) This equation is an important analytical result in the work of Shockley and Queisser. It provides deep insight into the physics of the electricity generation in solar cells. To evaluate α, we consider the thermal equilibrium (i.e., the condition that is reached if the solar cell is kept in dark). Here, the electron and hole Fermi lev- els are equal and thus V = 0. Further, under equilibrium condition the electronhole pair generation rate Fc0 is equal to the recombination rate of the electronhole pairs for V = 0, that is, Fc(0) = Fc0. From the above, we obtain Fc(0) = αn2 i = Fc0 (4.17) and the radiative recombination rate Fc(V) can be rewritten as shown in Eq. (4.18). Fc(V ) = Fc0exp  V Vc  (4.18) 3. Non-radiative generation rate and non-radiative recombination rate of electron hole pairs Similar to the case of the radiative processes, we can suppose that the rates of the electronhole recombination belonging to the class of non-radiative processes also increase for a higher density of electrons in the conduction band and a higher density of holes in the valence band. Therefore, we assume that the non-radiative recom- bination rate per unit area, R, is proportional to the electron density times the hole density, i.e., R n  p. As a consequence, the non-radiative recombination rate R also becomes a function of V like the radiative recombination rate Fc(V ), and we obtain Eq. (4.19). R(V ) = R(0)exp  V Vc  (4.19) 4.2 The Relation Between Electrical Current and Voltage 49 Here, R(0) is the non-radiative generation rate of electronhole pairs as a result of the thermal activation of carriers via defects in the semiconductor crystal. When we summarize the above points 13, we ﬁnd that the number of electrons that can be extracted as electric current I is the sum of the electronhole pair genera- tion rate Fs (due to black-body radiation from the Sun), the non-radiative generation rate R(0), and the electronhole pair generation rate Fc0 (due to the ﬁnite solar cell temperature T c) reduced by the radiative recombination rate Fc(V ) and the non- radiative recombination rate R(V). Hence, we obtain the following equation: I/e = Fs + Fc0 Fc(V ) + R(0) R(V ) (4.20) Letusanalyzethestructureoftheaboveequation,whichisusefulforthederivation of the relation between current and voltage in the steady-state condition. I/e = Fs + Fc0 Fc(V ) + R(0) R(V ) I = e{Fs + Fc0 Fc(V ) + R(0) R(V )} I = e Fs + Fc0 Fc0exp  V Vc  + R(0) R(0)exp  V Vc  I = eFs + e{Fc0 + R(0)} 1 exp  V Vc  I = eFs + eFc0 fc 1 exp  V Vc  (4.21) Here, fc is deﬁned as the ratio of radiative electronhole pair generation to total electronhole pair generation. fc = Fc0 Fc0 + R(0) (4.22) More precisely, fc is the ratio between the net rate of electronhole pair generation by radiation only and the net generation rate of electronhole pairs via both radiative and non-radiative processes. This deﬁnition is presented in Eq. (4.23). fc = Fc0 Fc(V ) Fc0 Fc(V ) + R(0) R(V ) (4.23) Equation (4.22) can be easily derived by substituting Eqs. (4.18) and (4.19) into Eq. (4.23). In the following, we examine fc and provide an explanation about its inﬂuence on the solar cell characteristics. We start with the consideration of the net rate of electronhole pair generation by the solar cells own radiation, Fc0 Fc(V ) = Fc0 1 exp  V Vc   0 (4.24) and the net rate of electronhole pair generation via non-radiative processes, 50 4 Inﬂuences of Carrier Generation and Recombination  R(0) R(V ) = R(0) 1 exp  V Vc   0 (4.25) The voltage V in the above equations is the maximum voltage that can be obtained from the solar cell under a certain illumination according to Eq. (4.14), and thus, V  0 V holds. Furthermore, from Eq. (4.15) we ﬁnd Vc  0 V, and thus, exp(V/Vc) is larger than one. Consequently, the sign of the expression inside the curly brackets of Eqs. (4.24) and (4.25) becomes negative, which results in the inequalities on the right-hand side. Therefore, the net rate of electronhole pair generation by the solar cells own radiation is negative for both radiative and non-radiative processes (a negative generation rate means recombination). The underlying physics of these negative net generation rates is important to understand the conversion efﬁciencies of solar cells. Under thermal equilibrium, the voltage V is zero because the (quasi-)Fermi levels of electrons and holes are equal (for details, see Chap. 8). Consequently, we have Fc0 = Fc(0) as shown in Eq. (4.17). On the other hand, if the solar cell is illuminated with light, this disturbs the thermal equilibrium and the number of electronhole pairs increases due to the illumination, which means that the quasi-Fermi levels shift. A potential difference V  0 V is established as a result of the shift in the quasi-Fermi levels of both the electron and the hole. Because the electrons and holes attempt to reach the original thermal equilibrium condition, the amount of recombination increases as described by Eq. (4.18). In order to revert the system to the equilibrium condition, the net generation rate of electronhole pairs that is induced by the solar cells own radiation has to be negative. The same is true for the net rate of electronhole pair generation via non-radiative processes. Now, we focus on the second term in Eq. (4.21), eFc0 fc 1 exp  V Vc  From the above, we have 1 exp  V Vc   0 and therefore eFc0 fc 1 exp  V Vc   0 holds. Since fc is a variable between 0 and 1, the extractable current provided in Eq. (4.21), I = eFs + eFc0 fc 1 exp  V Vc  4.2 The Relation Between Electrical Current and Voltage 51 is maximized if the fc in the second term is unity. Therefore, the extractable current decreases for values of fc less than unity. Below, we consider the differences that arise between the two cases fc = 1 and fc  1. First, consider the case fc = 1. According to Eq. (4.22), this condition is reached if R(0) = 0 which implies that non-radiative generation of electronhole pairs is absent. Furthermore, R(V ) becomes zero as shown in Eq. (4.19), which means that non-radiative recombination is also absent. Consequently, the recombination losses due to non-radiative processes are suppressed in case of fc = 1, and this would enable realization of the highest conversion efﬁciency. However, this is an ideal case. On the other hand, for fc  1 the non-radiative recombination rate R(0) is nonzero. Let us consider the behavior of the solar cell performance that is observed upon changing the value of R(0). If the R(0) in fc = Fc0 Fc0 + R(0) in Eq. (4.22) increases, an increase in the non-radiative recombination rate is induced as is evident from R(V ) = R(0)exp  V Vc  in Eq. (4.19). This results in a reduction of the conversion efﬁciency. The reduction in the conversion efﬁciency is not linear in R(0); i.e., the relative difference in the maximum conversion efﬁciencies for R(0)/Fc0 = 0.01 and R(0)/Fc0 = 0.1 is more signiﬁcant than that obtained for R(0)/Fc0 = 0.1 and R(0)/Fc0 = 1. The above discussion shows that the conversion efﬁciency is higher for fc being closer to unity, because the loss through non-radiative recombination is suppressed. As such, the value of fc is important for considerations on the conversion efﬁciency. More details are provided in Chap. 5. 4.3 Short-Circuit Current and Open-Circuit Voltage Short circuit means that there is no potential barrier for the current. In terms of a simple electrical circuit with an Ohmic load, this is equivalent to zero voltage. Equation (4.21) stated that the extractable current of the solar cell is I = eFs + eFc0 fc 1 exp  V Vc  52 4 Inﬂuences of Carrier Generation and Recombination  Because the ﬁrst term in Eq. (4.21) constitutes the total current for setting V = 0 in the second term, i.e., the short-circuit condition, we deﬁne the ﬁrst term as short- circuit current and denote it by Ish as shown in Eq. (4.26). Ish = eFs (4.26) In the following, we consider the maximum current I0 that can be extracted in case of no illumination with sunlight, which implies that the rate Fs for the electronhole pair generation via sunlight is zero. Since there is no illumination with sunlight, the electronhole pairs are only generated via the solar cells own radiation (at a rate Fc0) and the non-radiative process (at a rate R(0)). Neglection of radiative and non-radiative recombination of the electronhole pairs results in Fc(V ) = 0 and R(V ) = 0. Hence, the maximum current I0 is deﬁned as I0 e{Fc0 + R(0)} (4.27) By employing this and the deﬁnition of fc (i.e., the ratio between the radiative generation rate and the total generation rate of electronhole pairs) in Eq. I = eFs + eFc0 fc 1 exp  V Vc  = Ish + I0 1 exp  V Vc  (4.29) Open circuit means that the conducting wires are not connected, which is equiv- alent to zero current. Hence, to determine the open-circuit voltage, we have to set I in Eq. (4.21) to zero. eFs + eFc0 fc 1 exp  V Vc  = 0 (4.30) By solving for V, the open-circuit voltage can be obtained as shown in Eq. Chapter 5 The Conversion Efﬁciency of a Solar Cell as Determined by the Detailed Balance Model Abstract In the previous chapter, we discussed the conditions that apply to the actual solar cell but were not considered for the ideal solar cell. In order to derive the maximum output power of the actual solar cell, we have to discuss the balance between absorption (resulting in electrical current) and emission (due to the estab- lished voltage). Therefore, the present chapter discusses the balanced condition of voltage and current by employing the currentvoltage relation determined in the previous chapter. Based on that, the conversion efﬁciency of the actual solar cell is derived. Then, we discuss the contributions of the different types of losses that occur in a solar cell with a single band gap. Keywords Detailed balance  ShockleyQueisser limit  Conversion efﬁciency  Losses in single-junction solar cell 5.1 The Nominal Efﬁciency As preparation, we ignore the balance between the carrier generation and recombina- tion that takes place at the solar cells operating point for maximum output power and consider the so-called nominal conversion efﬁciency. The operating point deﬁnes the voltage maximizing the output power of a solar cell under a certain illumination con- dition. Generally, this voltage is smaller than Vop, since no current can be extracted from a solar cell at Vop which is equivalent to zero electrical power. The calculation of the nominal conversion efﬁciency assumes that the voltage and current that can be extracted are equal to the open-circuit voltage Vop and the short-circuit current Ish, respectively. In other words, this model considers no maximum of the output power since it neglects the real currentvoltage (IV) curve, which is a common plot to characterize the solar cell behavior for various loads.  Springer Nature Singapore Pte Ltd. 2019 T. Kita et al., Energy Conversion Efﬁciency of Solar Cells, Green Energy and Technology, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-13-9089-0_5 55 56 5 The Conversion Efﬁciency of a Solar Cell as Determined  If we deﬁne the incident light power as Pinc, the nominal energy conversion efﬁciency ηnom of the solar cell becomes ηnom = VopIsh Pinc (5.1) The IV characteristic of this model consists of two straight lines as shown in Fig. 5.1. The actual IV curve, however, possesses a curvature at the corner as shown in Fig. 5.5, and therefore the actual energy conversion efﬁciency will of course differ from that given in Eq. (5.1). These details are elaborated in Sect. 5.2. Here, we proceed by assuming that such a rectangular currentvoltage characteristic can be obtained. What value can we actually expect from Eq. (5.1)? The result of the follow- ing derivation reveals that the conversion efﬁciency for the unbalanced condition, ηnom, can be expressed as the product of the ideal solar cell efﬁciency u  νg, Ts  and v  xg, xc, f  (= Vop/Vg). We can conﬁrm that the voltage V max and the current I (V max) of the maximum output power condition are smaller than the open-circuit voltage V op and the short-circuit current Ish, respectively. (5.20) as a function of the band-gap energy together with the result for the ideal solar cell. Here, we assumed a temperature of Ts = 6000 K for the Sun, Tc = 300 K for the solar cell, and f = 1.09  105 ( fc = 1, fω = 2.18  105). Further, νg is the frequency that corresponds to the band-gap energy, and G(ν) is the incoming photon ﬂux per unit area per unit time and per unit solid angle. Curve (a) in Fig. 5.9 is the ideal solar cells conversion efﬁciency, which assumes that an open-circuit voltage equivalent to the band-gap energy can be realized and that all photons from the Sun with Ephoton  Eg are absorbed and excite electrons. Therefore, the theoretical limit of the conversion efﬁciency of the ideal solar cell reaches 44.3% for a semiconductor with Eg = 1.12 eV. The curve (b) in Fig. 5.9 is the detailed balance limit of the conversion efﬁciency which adds the conditions regarding the solid angle of the Sun as seen from the Earth, the solar cell being a black body with temperature T c, and the ﬁll factor, to the ideal solar cell conditions. The conversion efﬁciencies decrease upon adding 5.2 The Detailed Balance Limit of the Conversion Efﬁciency 65 Fig. 5.9 Curve (a) shows the ideal solar cells conversion efﬁciency and curve (b) the detailed balance limit of the conversion efﬁciency further restrictions; compared to the reduction in the conversion efﬁciency observed in Fig. 5.2, the conversion efﬁciency shown in Fig. 5.9 (b) decreased once more. The additional reduction is exactly that induced by the ﬁll factor. Furthermore, from the relation between the ﬁll factor and the open-circuit voltage shown in Fig. 5.8, we ﬁnd that the value of the ﬁll factor increases for higher open-circuit voltages, i.e., the ﬁll factor is larger for semiconductors with larger Eg. Therefore, the band-gap energy where the detailed balance limit of the conversion efﬁciency reaches its maximum is larger than the band-gap energy for the maximum conversion efﬁciency of the ideal solar cell, 1.12 eV. The detailed balance limit of the conversion efﬁciency is maximized for a semiconductor with Eg = 1.34 eV where it reaches 30.5%. This value, the so-called ShockleyQueisser (SQ) limit, is a very famous number among solar cell scientists. It is the ultimate energy conversion efﬁciency of a single-junction solar cell that considers all unavoidable losses due to restrictions that apply to single- junction solar cells on the Earth. Gallium arsenide (GaAs) is a semiconductor with a band-gap energy of 1.42 eV at room temperature and possesses characteristics that are close to those required for realizing the SQ limit. Indeed, the solar cells based on GaAs have achieved the highest conversion efﬁciencies among the single-junction solar cells. To conclude this section, we discuss the inﬂuence of f on the conversion efﬁciency. In Sect. 4.3, we deﬁned f fc fω/2. Since fω 2.18105 is the geometric factor which is determined by the solid angle of the Sun, we can consider that f is changed solely via its dependence on fc. According to fc = Fc0 Fc0 + R(0), (4.22) fc approaches zero in the lower limit (R(0) ) and unity in the upper limit (R(0) = 0), i.e., 0  fc 1. The detailed balance limit of the conversion efﬁciency discussed here is for the case of fc = 1, that is, exactly that solar cell efﬁciency that 66 5 The Conversion Efﬁciency of a Solar Cell as Determined  is obtained for the case of zero non-radiative electronhole pair generation and also zero non-radiative recombination. If non-radiative recombination occurs frequently, the value of fc becomes signiﬁcantly smaller than unity and f drops below the value of 1.09  105 which was used for the detailed balance model discussed above. As a result, the value of the open-circuit voltage V op presented in Eq. (4.38) drops, and the conversion efﬁciency decreases. In other words, if the value of f becomes small, the conversion efﬁciency decreases. In Fig. 5.10, we plot the conversion efﬁciency as a function of the band-gap energy for various fc. As discussed above, the conversion efﬁciency decreases with the decrease in fc. On the other hand, the optimal band-gap energy increases with a decrease in fc. Figure 5.11 shows the fc dependence of the IV curve for a Si solar cell (Eg = 1.1 eV, T c = 300 K) with an area of 1 cm2. Closed circles indicate the maximum power point for each fc. Fig. 5.10 Detailed balance limit of the conversion efﬁciency for various f c Fig. 5.11 Theoretical IV curve of a Si solar cell for various f c. Closed circles indicate the maximum power point 5.2 The Detailed Balance Limit of the Conversion Efﬁciency 67 The open-circuit voltage V op quickly drops from 0.849 to 0.611 V when fc decreases from 1 to 104. Conversely, the short-circuit current Ish hardly depends on fc because we assumed inﬁnitely high mobility of electrons in the detailed balance model, that is, the excited electron can be extracted as current at the short-circuit condition. On the other hand, the ﬁll factor FF slightly decreases from 0.867 to 0.830 when fc decreases from 1 to 104. As the short-circuit current and ﬁll factor are insensitive to fc, the optimal band-gap energy shown in Fig. 5.10 results from the drop of the open-circuit voltage with decreasing fc. We note that f is usually determined by the crystal quality of the material used for the solar cell. 5.3 Losses in Solar Cells In Chap. 3, we assumed that the voltage corresponding to the total band-gap energy can be extracted as is, that is, we considered the most ideal solar cell. In Chap. 4, we considered the carrier generation and the recombination processes that are inher- ently connected to the ﬁnite temperature of the solar cell and discussed the fact that the open-circuit voltage of the solar cell is less than the band-gap voltage (i.e., the voltage equivalent to the band-gap energy). Then, in the present chapter, the analysis of the currentvoltage characteristics within the framework of the detailed balance theory clariﬁed that the voltage Vmax of the maximum output power condition of the solar cell is less than the open-circuit voltage Vop. In this way, the discussions extending from Chaps. 3, 4 and this chapter were concerned with the voltage reduc- tions (below values corresponding to the band-gap energy) that originate from the semiconductor characteristics. In this section, we shall consider the detailed balance between absorption and emission excluding non-radiative transitions and trace the drop of the solar cell voltage from the viewpoint of statistical thermodynamics. The approach based on statistical thermodynamics has been put into a systematic scheme for the ﬁrst time by Hirst and Ekins-Daukes in the year 2011 [1], and the present section explains their analytical approach. Inthischapter,weexpressed Vmax usingEq.(5.15),butwewerenotabletoanalyt- ically derive Vmax at that stage. Here, we analytically derive Vmax by employing the Boltzmann approximation and gain a deeper understanding of the relation between the voltage and the band-gap energy. As explained above, the solar cell output power is the product of current and voltage. First, let us determine the current I by using an approximation. In the model that excludes non-radiative recombination ( fc = 1), the number of electrons that can be extracted as current I from the solar cell per unit time, I/e, is the rate of electronhole generation by the black-body radiation from the Sun, Fs, subtracted by the rate of radiative recombination inside the solar cell, Fc(V ). This relation is shown in Eq. (5.21). I e = Fs Fc(V ) (5.21) 68 5 The Conversion Efﬁciency of a Solar Cell as Determined  By expanding Eq. (5.21) into several speciﬁc terms using the Boltzmann approxi- mation and analyzing the contents of the equation, we shall clarify the terms that are responsible for the reduction of the solar cell voltage to values below the band-gap voltage (Eg/e). Let us begin with rewriting Fs and Fc(V ) as more explicit equations. As also mentioned in Chap. 4, Fs can be evaluated by substituting Eq. (4.3) into Eq. (4.4). Because we want to verify the difference between the band-gap voltage and the voltage at the maximum output power condition, we reformulate the above relation as Eg e Vmax = Eg e Tc Ts + kTc e ln 2π π fω (5.33) 5.3 Losses in Solar Cells 71 The ﬁrst term on the right-hand side of Eq. (5.33) represents the voltage loss that occurs as a result of a ﬁnite solar cell temperature larger than zero. This type of loss is called Carnot loss which increases as Tc/Ts increases. If the Sun and solar cell temperatures are equal, no work can be extracted. The second term represents the voltage loss that occurs as a result of the differences between the solid angle of the sunlight incident on the solar cell and that of the light emitted by the solar cell. This type of loss is called Boltzmann loss. For the ideal solar cell, we assumed that a voltage equivalent to the band gap can be extracted, but the Vmax that accounts for the detailed balance between absorption and emission is smaller than the band-gap energy. In the following, we shall consider the physical meanings of the Carnot and Boltzmann losses derived above, which constitute the origins of this voltage drop. In thermodynamics, there exists a law that states that the efﬁciency in a process exchangingenergybetweenahigh-andalow-temperatureheatreservoircannotreach 100% if the lower temperature is different from absolute zero. This law is called the second law of thermodynamics. Similarly, when we treat the Sun as the hot reservoir and the solar cell itself as the cold reservoir, this law of thermodynamics can also be applied to the case of the solar cell system, which implies that unavoidable losses exist. Let us clarify how the loss induced by the nonzero temperature of the solar cell, Tc, can be mathematically represented within the framework of thermodynamics. Consider the energy transfer between the hot reservoir with temperature Thigh and the cold reservoir with temperature Tlow. Assume a so-called Carnot cycle, the cyclic reversible process drawn in Fig. 5.12, for a photon gas system. Each point in this graph represents a state of the gas, deﬁned by volume and pressure. The transition A B is an isothermal expansion which maintains the high temperature Thigh of the gas. Because the pressure of the photon gas depends not on the volume, the pressure is constant during the transition A B. The transition B C expands the gas under a thermally isolated condition (adiabatic expansion), which lowers the temperature to Tlow. Via the isothermal compression C D and the adiabatic compression D A, the gas returns to the state A. Let us determine the work done by one complete loop of this Carnot cycle. If we deﬁne U as the internal energy of the present system, Q as the amount of heat added to the system, and W as the work done on the outside of the system, the relation of these properties can be represented as Eq. (5.34) according to the ﬁrst law of thermodynamics. U = Q W (5.34) By deﬁning QAB as the amount of heat added to the system during the transition A B and UAB as the change in the inner energy, the work WAB performed during the transition A B can be written as Eq. (5.35). WAB = QAB UAB (5.35) 72 5 The Conversion Efﬁciency of a Solar Cell as Determined  Fig. 5.12 Carnot cycle of the photon gas Volume Pressure Isothermal Isothermal Adiabatic Adiabatic A B D C Thigh Tlow The U of a certain system is determined by the change in the temperature. Since A B is an isothermal transition, UAB is zero. Therefore, we have WAB = QAB (5.36) Because we need to express QAB as a function of the temperature, we introduce the entropy S. S is the change in the entropy and deﬁned by Eq. (5.37). S Q T (5.37) If we express the S during the transition A B as SB SA and use Eq. (5.37), QAB can be written as Eq. (8.38). QAB = Thigh(SB SA) (5.38) By substituting Eq. (5.38) into Eq. (5.36), WAB becomes WAB = Thigh(SB SA) (5.39) With this Eq. (5.39), we are able to describe the work done during A B. WBC is given by Eq. (5.40), which can be obtained by considering that the transition B  C occurs under a thermally isolated condition and thus the heat added to the system is zero. WBC = UBC (5.40) WCD and WDA can be obtained in the same way. 5.3 Losses in Solar Cells 73 WCD = Tlow(SA SB) (5.41) WDA = UDA (5.42) The work WCarnot done during one complete loop of the Carnot cycle is the sum of the works described by Eqs. (5.39)(5.42). The temperature changes for the tran- sitions B C and D A are Thigh Tlow and Tlow Thigh, respectively, and thus have the same absolute magnitude but their signs are opposite. As a result, the expression for WCarnot can be derived as shown in Eq. (5.43), which includes the fact that UBC and UDA exactly cancel each other. WCarnot = Thigh(SB SA) UBC + Tlow(SA SB) UDA = Thigh(SB SA) + Tlow(SA SB) = (Thigh Tlow)(SB SA) (5.43) Theefﬁciencyof acompletecycleof theCarnot process is calledCarnot efﬁciency, ηCarnot, and is deﬁned as the ratio of WCarnot to the amount of heat QAB added to the system. ηCarnot = WCarnot QAB =  Thigh Tlow  (SB SA) Thigh(SB SA) = 1 Tlow Thigh (5.44) Equation (5.44) shows that total input (the heat QAB) during one cycle of the Carnot process cannot be completely converted into output (the work WCarnot deliv- ered to the surrounding); the output suffers a loss corresponding to the input multi- plied by Tlow/Thigh. In the same manner, the solar cell can also not exceed the Carnot efﬁciency, because the solar cell transports energy to a cold heat reservoir with nonzero temperature (the solar cell with temperature Tc). The model in this section is the model that considers the detailed balance between absorption and emission without non-radiative recombination. Therefore, the losses in the current are only those resulting from radiative recombination. Consequently, we can consider that the loss induced by the nonzero solar cell temperature inﬂuences the voltage only. As explained in Chap. 3, in the ideal solar cell without losses due to non-radiative recombination and the FF, the extractable voltage is the voltage corresponding to the band gap, Eg/e. If we now add the conclusions from the interpretation in terms of thermodynamics, we have a voltage loss that is exactly the ideal voltage Eg/e times Tc/Ts. Hence, the solar cell performance loss due to the ﬁnite nonzero Tc, PCarnot, can be written as shown in Eq. (5.45). 74 5 The Conversion Efﬁciency of a Solar Cell as Determined  PCarnot = I (Vmax) Eg e Tc T s (5.45) This corresponds to the ﬁrst term in Eq. (5.33), the Carnot loss. Next, we shall consider the Boltzmann loss from the viewpoint of statistical ther- modynamics. As preparation for the considerations on the Boltzmann loss, we ﬁrst discuss the relation between the entropy and the losses. The entropy is used as an indicator for the randomness of a macroscopic state (probability for a certain state to appear statistically). Furthermore, the energy con- servation law can be formulated as Eq. (5.46) by using the input energy Ein. Ein = W + Q (5.46) From Eq. (5.37), we ﬁnd that if the temperature of the system T is invariable, an increase in S leads to an increase in Q. In other words, the increase in S means that energy is lost as heat. Since the increase in the entropy is accompanied by an increase in the losses, we need to clarify how the solar cell characteristics change with the entropy. In statistical thermodynamics, the entropy S is deﬁned by Eq. (5.47), which assumes w as the number of states for the particles in the system (that is, number of possible particle conﬁgurations that lead to the same macroscopic properties). S = k ln w (5.47) The sunlight is incident on the solar cell with an incident solid angle π fω as shown in Fig. 5.13. On the other hand, the solar cell itself emits light (generated by radiative recombination) into the emission solid angle of 2π (see Sect. 4.1). Sun Solar cell Solar cell Fig. 5.13 Incident solid angle and the emission solid angle are strongly different in case of solar cells without lenses for the concentration of sunlight 5.3 Losses in Solar Cells 75 The w for the photons contained in a speciﬁc solid angle changes according to the solid angle. In case of a solar cell without lenses for the concentration of sunlight, the emission solid angle is larger than the incident solid angle, and therefore, the w for the photons emitted by the solar cell is larger. From Eq. (5.47), we ﬁnd that S increases upon an increase in w. The change in the entropy that occurs as a result of the different solid angles for incidence and emission can be expressed as shown in Eq. (5.48). S = k ln 2π k ln π fω = k ln 2π π fω (5.48) From Eq. (5.37), we understand that the energy equal to the product of S and the solar cell temperature Tc is lost as heat. As in the case of the Carnot loss, the present model does not consider any current losses except those due to radiative recombination. Hence, the loss described by Eq. (5.48) inﬂuences the voltage only. The loss with respect to the solar cell output power that is induced by the difference between the entropies of the incident and emission solid angles, PBoltzmann, can be written as shown below. PBoltzmann = I (Vmax)kTc e ln 2π π fω (5.49) This performance loss corresponds to the second term in Eq. (5.33), the Boltzmann loss. The above clariﬁes that, in addition to the Carnot loss, also the Boltzmann loss reduces the voltage. Figure 5.14 plots the maximum output power Pmax and the relative contribu- tion of each of the ﬁve loss mechanisms (transmission, thermalization, Boltzmann, Carnot, radiative recombination) with respect to the total input power for various band-gap energies. (5.45) and (5.49), respectively. The energy lost per carrier involved in radiative recombination is the energy difference from the valence band maximum to the conduction band minimum, i.e., Eg. In terms of voltage, this energy corresponds to Eg/e. The rate for radiative recombination is Fc(V ) as provided by Eq. (4.16). The loss in the current due to radiative recombi- nation under the maximum output power condition can be expressed as eFc(Vmax). Therefore, the non-radiative recombination losses under the maximum output con- dition can be written as Eq. (5.52). For reference, the relationships used to draw Fig. 5.14 are given below in order. For Fig. 5.15, ﬁrst consider the ﬂux of incident photons that is obtained by integration over the range from a certain energy (shown on the x axis) to inﬁnite energy. (5.53). e  Fs (5.53) 5.3 Losses in Solar Cells 77 In case of the currentvoltage characteristics within the framework of the detailed balance theory, the output power of the solar cell reaches the maximum at Eg = 1.34 eV (Fig. 5.9). The photons with energy larger than the band-gap energy are absorbed, but the excess energy will result in thermalization losses. Furthermore, photons with energy less than 1.34 eV cannot be absorbed, and thus result in trans- mission losses. Figure 5.16 visualizes the thermalization and transmission losses. The rectangular area obtained by excluding the thermalization and transmission losses in Fig. 5.16 is the output of the solar cell for the completely ideal case with Eg = 1.34 eV, which was discussed in Chap. 3. Because the vertical axis of Fig. 5.16 is the incident photon ﬂux times the elementary charge, the height of the rectangular area obtained by excluding the thermalization and transmission losses represents the extractable current of the solar cell in the model for the nominal conversion efﬁciency shown in Fig. 5.2, which does not account for carrier recombination. In this section, we consider the detailed balance between absorption and emis- sion excluding non-radiative recombination. Therefore, the causes for the cur- rent reduction are transmission and radiative recombination only. Accordingly, the Fig. 5.15 Total incident photon ﬂux obtained by integration from a certain photon energy (shown on the x axis) to , multiplied by the elementary charge Fig. 5.16 Visualization of the thermalization and transmission losses for a single-junction solar cell with Eg = 1.34 eV 78 5 The Conversion Efﬁciency of a Solar Cell as Determined  charge-carrier ﬂux (I/e) is the photon ﬂux Fs absorbed by the solar cell subtracted by the radiative recombination rate Fc(Vmax). If we insert the value of Vmax obtained for Eg = 1.34 eV into Eqs. (4.10) and (4.18), we have eFc(Vmax) = 1.26103 C/cm2. The graph including the radiative recombination losses is shown in Fig. 5.17. Since the presently considered model does not account for the non-radiative tran- sitions, the current can only be reduced by transmission and radiative recombination. Therefore, the Carnot and the Boltzmann losses are not responsible for the current reduction, but responsible for the voltage reduction. Finally, let us determine the extent of the voltage drop that is caused by the Carnot loss and the Boltzmann loss. According to Eq. (5.45), the Carnot loss can be represented as I (Vmax)  (EgTc)/(eTs) and thus the voltage drop caused by the Carnot loss can be determined by dividing Eq. (5.45) by I (Vmax), resulting in  EgTc  /(eTs). Equation (5.54) evaluates  EgTc  /(eTs) using the assumptions Tc = 300 K and Ts = 6000 K. EgTc eTs 0.067 V (5.54) InEq.(5.55),thevoltagelossinducedbytheBoltzmannloss,kTc/e ln(2π/π fω), is evaluated for the same assumptions. kTc e  ln  2π π fω  0.296 V (5.55) Figure 5.18 shows Fig. 5.17 extended by the above results of the Carnot and Boltzmann losses. From Fig. 5.18, we can understand that the Carnot and Boltzmann losses are the origins of the reduction from the band-gap voltage to the actually extractable voltage under the maximum output power condition of the solar cell. Fig. 5.17 Radiative recombination losses in relation to the thermalization and transmission losses for a single-junction solar cell with Eg = 1.34 eV 5.3 Losses in Solar Cells 79 Fig. 5.18 Output power and the breakdown of the losses for a single-junction solar cell with Eg = 1.34 eV The different types of losses in a solar cell are important factors for determining the conversion efﬁciency. The losses induced by transmission and radiative recom- bination lead to a decay in the output current, and the thermalization, Boltzmann, and Carnot losses reduce the output voltage. Due to these hardly avoidable losses in a solar cell, the conversion efﬁciency of a solar cell with a single band gap is limited even if we consider an ideal carrier extraction. This limit is the SQ limit, and to exceed this limit, we have to suppress the losses explained in this section to a certain extent. In Chap. 6, we clarify the conversion efﬁciencies of actual solar cells by using more concrete calculations and present the physics of the technologies that suppress such losses and enable the realization of efﬁciencies beyond higher than the SQ limit. Reference 1. Hirst LC, Ekins-Daukes NJ (2011) Fundamental losses in solar cells. Prog Photovolt Res Appl 9(3):286293 Chapter 6 Actual Calculation of Solar Cell Efﬁciencies Abstract In Chap. 5, we derived the single-junction solar cell conversion efﬁciency within the framework of the detailed balance theory and computed the solar spec- trum by employing Plancks law for black-body radiation. As explained in Sect. 2.2, the solar spectrum that passes the atmosphere differs according to the amount of air passed. This amount can be expressed as air mass (AM). In this chapter, we do not rely on the black-body radiation for the solar spectrum, but employ the actual spectral data for the sunlight that reaches the Earth after passing the atmosphere. With this data, we calculate the conversion efﬁciencies of several different solar cell structures. We ﬁrst recalculate the conversion efﬁciency of the single-junction solar cell in Sect. 6.1 by using the real solar spectrum. In Sect. 6.2, we determine the con- version efﬁciency of a solar cell under concentrated sunlight, which can be obtained, for example, by using a lens. Additionally, in Sects. 6.36.6 we discuss four selected solar cell structures that are recently attracting attention as novel types of solar cells with the ability to overcome the SQ limit. To exceed the SQ limit means that the transmission and thermalization losses, which we declared as being unavoid- able, have to be avoided somehow by using novel approaches. For the reduction of the losses and conversion of the sunlight into electrical power to the best extent possible, we can either increase the wavelength range that is employed by the solar cell for conversion into electrical power, or the opposite, and we can reshape the solar spectrum itself in such a way that it can be efﬁciently converted into electrical power by the solar cell. As examples for the ﬁrst-mentioned approach, we discuss the multi-junction solar cell (which can also be called tandem solar cell) in Sect. 6.3 and the intermediate-band solar cell in Sect. 6.4. Multi-junction solar cells comprise a multilayer structure where each of the stacked layers is a semiconductor junction with different Eg. This method enables capturing the light transmitted through one solar cell by another solar cell in the layer below. Therefore, the light that would usually be lost through transmission can be used for electricity generation, and this allows us to exceed the SQ limit of the single junction. This solar cell architecture has already been practically implemented, and the multi-junction type is the current world record holder in terms of solar cell performance. Unfortunately, the series  Springer Nature Singapore Pte Ltd. 2019 T. Kita et al., Energy Conversion Efﬁciency of Solar Cells, Green Energy and Technology, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-13-9089-0_6 81 82 6 Actual Calculation of Solar Cell Efﬁciencies connection between the junctions constitutes a drawback for the multi-junction solar cells. The tandem solar cells consist of stacked junctions that are serially connected. Therefore, the electricity generation is already completely suppressed if one of the stacked junctions does not operate. For example, a certain spectral part of the light may hardly reach the solar cell due to the scattering and absorption in clouds on a cloudy day. If due to this the electricity generation of any solar cell in the stack drops, the performance of the whole device is affected because the current that ﬂows through the directly affected cell is reduced and becomes the bottleneck of the whole device, even if the solar cells in the other layers work ﬁne. On the other hand, the intermediate-band solar cell has attracted attention as a solar cell architecture that promises high conversion efﬁciencies and can also lift the restrictions imposed by the series connection of the multi-junction tandem solar cells. This architecture reduces the transmission losses by implementing a new band (the so-called intermediate band) within the band gap of the solar cell. Because the direct absorption of the light via a transition between the conduction and the valence bands (a so-called interband transition) occurs in parallel with the stepwise absorption via the intermediate band, the intermediate-band solar cells electricity generation cannot be completely sup- pressed under clouded conditions as was the case for the multi-junction solar cells. In the intermediate-band solar cell, by absorbing a below-gap photon, an electron transits from the valence band to the intermediate band. Upon absorbing another below-gap photon, the electron is further excited into the conduction band. There transitions widely cover the solar spectrum. This two-step excitation (two-step pho- ton up-conversion) process following the absorption of two below-gap photons pro- duces additional photocurrent without degrading the photovoltage. Such two-step photoexcitation is known to occur at heterointerfaces. In Sect. 6.5, we deal with a solar cell containing a heterointerface in the intrinsic layer so-called two-step photon up-conversionsolarcell.Theconversionefﬁciencyofthesolarcellutilizinginterband and intraband transitions strongly depends on the absorptivity of these transitions. Detailed inﬂuences of the absorptivity of photovoltaic materials on the conversion efﬁciency are also discussed. Section 6.6 discusses one example for the reduction of losses by altering the solar spectrum. To reduce the transmission losses, it is nec- essary to convert the light in the long-wavelength region of the solar spectrum into light with shorter wavelengths. Similarly, to reduce losses through thermalization, it is necessary to convert the light in the short-wavelength region of the solar spectrum into light with longer wavelengths. We analyze how much the conversion efﬁciency can be improved by down-converting the short-wavelength light (high photon ener- gies) to long-wavelength light (small photon energies). Besides, the inﬂuences of changing weather conditions on the conversion efﬁciency of various types of solar cells are introduced in detail in Sect. 6.7. Up to Sect. 6.7, we proceed to discuss the solar cell at a constant temperature of 300 K. However, since concentration of sunlight is an important technique to enhance conversion efﬁciencies, the inﬂuence of 6 Actual Calculation of Solar Cell Efﬁciencies 83 the temperature has to be investigated. Due to the strong concentration of sunlight, the temperature of the concentrator solar cell device increases. If the temperature increases, the semiconductor band gap becomes smaller and furthermore, a higher the electronhole recombination rate (stronger black-body radiation) is observed as explained in Sect. 4.2; this causes a decrease in the conversion efﬁciency. In Sect. 6.8, we analyze the change in the single-junction solar cell efﬁciency that occurs upon a change in the solar cell temperature. The solar cell is the photovoltaic device used as a solar energy converter. When indoor light such as white light-emitting diode (LED) is harvested, the optimized energy structure of the photovoltaic materials is different from the results obtained for the sunlight. We touch the energy conversion efﬁciency of indoor photovoltaic cells in Sect. 6.9. In Fig. 6.4, we can conﬁrm that the calculated maximum efﬁciency for AM1.5G exceeds that for AM0. This is due to the scattering and absorption by molecules in the air, which reduces the light intensity of the AM1.5G solar spectrum in the ultraviolet and infrared regions (Fig. 6.1 shows the comparison with the AM0 solar spectrum). The ultraviolet light is usually the origin of thermalization losses, and the infrared light is usually the reason for transmission losses. We can infer that the intensity reduction of these light regions leads to an increased match between the solar spectrum and the solar cell. This is the solar spectrum shaped by nature itself. Owing to the better match, the theoretical conversion efﬁciency for AM1.5G exceeds that for AM0. It should be noted that although the conversion efﬁciency is smaller for AM0, the theoretical output power under AM0 illumination exceeds that under AM1.5 illumination. Curve (a) is the result for illumination with AM0 and curve (b) that for AM1.5G 88 6 Actual Calculation of Solar Cell Efﬁciencies 6.2 Concentrator Solar Cell The solar cell conversion efﬁciencies in the previous section are all for solar cells that are directly exposed to sunlight like under open sky. In this section, we provide details about the operating principle and the conversion efﬁciency of a concentrator solar cell, which is a solar cell that is illuminated by concentrated sunlight. The concentrator solar cell is a solar cell type that aims at an increase of the conversion efﬁciency by collecting sunlight that would usually miss the solar cell surface. By employing lenses or mirrors, this light is focused on the solar cell surface and thus increases the solar power incident on the solar cell. The concentration of sunlight is essential for realizing solar cells with conversion efﬁciencies above 45%, in particular those aiming at 50%. Figure 6.6 shows the basic layout of a concentrator solar cell. Let us investigate how much the single-junction solar cell conversion efﬁciency increases upon concentrating the light. As noted above, the photon ﬂux incident on the solar cell increases by concentrating the sunlight. We shall use P ﬂux to denote the photon ﬂux of the solar black-body radiation spectrum that can be absorbed by the solar cell under concentrated sunlight per unit area and per unit solid angle. If we deﬁnetheconcentrationratioasX, P ﬂux canbewrittenasshowninEq.(6.7),i.e.,asthe product of X and the photon ﬂux Pﬂux deﬁned in Sect. 4.1. The latter is the photon ﬂux that can be absorbed by the single-junction solar cell under unconcentrated sunlight per unit area and per unit solid angle. P ﬂux  νg, Ts  = X Pﬂux  νg, Ts  (6.7) The increase of P ﬂux is accompanied by an increase in the generation rate of electronhole pairs. The generation rate of electronhole pairs for concentration of sunlight is denoted by F s and can be expressed using Eq. (6.7) and the deﬁnition of Fs in Eq. (4.4). The Boltzmann approximation stands when |E Ef| kT in the FermiDirac function, which is used for calculating the density of electrons in the conduction band and holes in the valence band. As we discuss in Chap. 8, Eqs. (8.18) and (8.20) represent the electron distribution function in the conduction band and the hole distribution function in the valence band, respectively. However, under a high sunlight concentration, the quasi-Fermi levels of electrons and holes signiﬁcantly split and approach the edge of the conduction band and the valence band. Then, the assumption |E Ef| kT becomes no longer valid. In addition, in terms of recombination of electronhole pairs, so far we have only considered the spontaneous recombination in solar cells, which is only determined by the density of electrons in the conduction band and holes in the valence band (see Sect. 4.2). Conversely, in case the quasi-Fermi level approaches the band edge, the stimulated recombination should be also taken into account as well as the spontaneous recombination. Using the FermiDirac function without pursuing the Boltzmann approximation and considering the effect of stimulated recombination, the recombination rate of electronhole pairs Fc(V ) is given by using the so-called generalized Planck equation [3]. When the concentration ratio is below approximately 1000, two lines calculated by (a) the Boltzmann approximation and (b) the generalized Planck equation obey almost the same dependence. By concentrating the sunlight on the solar cell surface, the number of electronhole pairs generated by the sunlight increases. The enhanced open-circuit voltage is thus a result of the larger difference between the electron and the hole quasi-Fermi levels, which expresses the electromotive force due to the enhanced generation rate of electronhole pairs. To allow for an extended sensitivity region with respect to the solar spec- trum, it has a structure that contains multiple layers with different Eg, stacked in order from large to small Eg with the largest being at the front side (side of light incidence). Each layer is in fact a single-junction solar cell, also called subcell, and tunnel junctions are employed for the connection between the subcells. This structure absorbs the high-energy photons (short wavelength) in the upper layers, while the low-energy photons (long wavelength) are absorbed in the layers below. Therefore, 6.3 Multi-junction Solar Cell 93 Fig. 6.9 Detailed balance limit of the conversion efﬁciency of a single-junction solar cell under concentrated sunlight obtained from the calculation using the AM data. Curve (a) is the result for concentrated AM0 light and curve (b) that for concentrated AM1.5D light sunlight is absorbed over a broad spectral range, transmission and thermalization losses are suppressed, and electronhole pairs are generated with high efﬁciency. The dual-junction type constitutes the simplest design, but by increasing the num- ber of junctions to three, four, or more junctions, an increased match with the solar spectrum is possible, which ultimately leads to efﬁciency improvement. On the other hand, there are still technical issues to be solved. Usually, the stacked layer structure is prepared by a multilayer crystal growth. Consequently, the multi- junction solar cell becomes rather thick. Unfortunately, a semiconductor with a dif- ferent band gap also implies a different lattice constant (roughly speaking, a crystal with a large lattice constant has a small Eg). The stacking of multiple semiconductor layers with different Eg therefore implies a stacking of semiconductor crystals with different lattice constants. Due to the resulting lattice mismatch, a large strain is built up near the heterointerface between two subcells (this region is also referred to as heterojunction) and it scales with the layer thickness. In the epitaxial growth, i.e., the layer-by-layer growth of ﬂat surfaces, both crystal lattices are connected properly. However, if the strain exceeds a certain threshold, the epitaxial growth will become impossible and accumulated strain energy is released by forming crystal dislocations. Such crystal dislocations are detrimental, because they constitute recombination center for carriers which reduces the quasi-Fermi-level separation. Consequently, a high-quality heterojunction requires a combination of semiconductors with small lattice mismatch. However, as mentioned above, there is a speciﬁc relation between the band gap and the lattice constant. Therefore, only a few materials systems are actually suited for the implementation in multi-junction solar cells. The common triple-junction solar cells employ either InGaP/GaAs/Ge or InGaP/GaAs/InGaAs for the top cell, middle cell, and bottom cell, respectively. These compound materi- als unfortunately use costly metals and require an expensive crystal growth method. Therefore, these compound semiconductor multi-junction solar cells are utilized in space applications, rather than in domestic electricity generation. However, recently, emerging techniques such as metamorphic epitaxial growth, epitaxial lift off, and 94 6 Actual Calculation of Solar Cell Efﬁciencies wafer-bonding have been accelerating the development of the multi-junction solar cells. We shall now calculate the conversion efﬁciency for the concrete examples of the dual-junction and triple-junction solar cells. I total = I c1 = I c2 (6.27) Furthermore, the output voltage of the tandem solar cell device is the sum of the voltages for each cell, V total = V c1 + V c2 (6.28) For the actual calculation of the conversion efﬁciency, we have to determine the current I total and voltage V total that maximize the output power and simultaneously satisfy Eqs. The results for concentrated sunlight reach a maximum conver- sion efﬁciency of 59.9%, which is obtained at Ec1 = 1.44 eV, Ec2 = 0.70 eV. These calculation results largely exceed the conversion efﬁciencies evaluated in the above discussion on the single-junction solar cell; the tandem solar cell exceeds the SQ limit both with and without concentration (44.3 and 30.5%, respectively). The- oretically, the conversion efﬁciency increases further if we increase the number of junctions. However, as mentioned above, strain energy will accumulate upon growth of stacked semiconductor layers with different crystal lattice constants. If too many layers are grown, the strain energy exceeds the limit and crystal dislocations form. Therefore, the growth of stacked high-quality crystals is actually very difﬁcult. At present, the highest solar cell conversion efﬁciency is realized by a multi-junction solar cell. 98 6 Actual Calculation of Solar Cell Efﬁciencies 6.4 Intermediate-Band Solar Cell The intermediate-band solar cell (shown in Fig. 6.14) has a theoretical conversion efﬁciency that exceeds the SQ limit and represents an approach that is different from the tandem solar cell concept. Here, an artiﬁcial intermediate band is formed between the valence band and the conduction band. The intermediate-band solar cell design enables transitions from the conduction band to the intermediate band and from the intermediate band to the conduction band. Therefore, it can absorb photons that are lost through transmission in the conventional single pn junction. The intermediate-band solar cell is one of the popular solar cell architectures and was investigated from the viewpoint of detailed balance in the work Increasing the Efﬁciency of Ideal Solar Cells by Photon Induced Transitions at Intermediate Levels [4] by Antonio Luque and Antonio Marti of the Polytechnic University of Madrid, Spain. This work showed that the maximum theoretical conversion efﬁciency of this solar cell type is 63.1%. The intermediate-band solar cell has attracted attention because of its high conversion efﬁciency combined with the fact of being a solar cell type that can lift the restrictions imposed by the series connection in the multi- junction solar cells. In the tandem solar cells, pn junctions with different Eg are stacked in series. Therefore, the electricity generation is already strongly suppressed if one of the junctions in the stack is affected by altered illumination conditions like a reductionofacertainspectralpartofthelightonacloudydaybecauseofthescattering and absorption by the clouds. In this case, the performance of the whole device is affected because the current that ﬂows through the directly affected cell is reduced and becomes the bottleneck of the whole device, even if the other subcells work ﬁne. In contrast, the intermediate-band solar cell enables both a direct absorption of light via interband transitions and a stepwise absorption via the intermediate band. Sincebothabsorptionprocessesproceedinparallel,theintermediate-bandsolarcells electricity generation cannot be completely suppressed under clouded conditions as was the case for the multi-junction solar cells. We determine the conversion efﬁciency of this intermediate-band solar cell by adopting the scheme that was used to determine the efﬁciency limit satisfying the detailed balance principle. Fig. 6.14 Schematic of the intermediate-band solar cell. Because the conventional single-junction solar cell has only interband transitions between the conduction band and the valence band, photons with energies below Eg cannot be absorbed. On the other hand, by inserting an artiﬁcial intermediate bandbetweenthevalenceandtheconductionbands,photonswithenergiesbelowEg canbeabsorbed 6.4 Intermediate-Band Solar Cell 99 Because an additional band has to be considered, we need to distinguish different interband transition types in the equations. Therefore, we introduce the superscript VC to indicate transitions between the valence band and the conduction band, VI for those between the valence band and the intermediate band, and IC for those between the intermediate band and the conduction band. Using Eqs. (6.42), (6.45), and (6.46), the intermediate-band solar cell can be rep- resented as an equivalent circuit consisting of the three solar cells shown in Fig. 6.17. The VC cell is the solar cell with a band-gap energy EC EV, the VI cell is that with a band-gap energy EI EV, and the IC cell is the solar cell with a band-gap energy EC EI. We proceed with the calculation by considering these three independent solar cells under electronhole pair generations that are given with Eqs. (6.31)(6.33) and (6.36)(6.41). For the actual calculation of the conversion efﬁciency, we have to determine the current I total and the voltage V VC whose product (corresponding to the output power) is maximized and simultaneously satisfy Eqs. (6.42) and (6.46), respectively. The calculation results for the intermediate-band solar cell conversion efﬁciencies obtained using the above procedure are shown with the contour plots in Figs. 6.18 and 6.19. This data shows that for concentrator solar cells, a larger improvement of the efﬁciency can be obtained with a smaller band gap. These three solar cells have to obey the restrictions implied by this equivalent circuit 104 6 Actual Calculation of Solar Cell Efﬁciencies Fig. 6.18 Conversion efﬁciency of the intermediate-band solar cell (AM1.5G, 1 sun). A maximum conversion efﬁciency of 49.4% is reached for a band-gap energy of 2.43 eV between the conduction band and the valence band, and an energy gap of 1.49 eV between the valence band maximum and the intermediate-band quasi-Fermi level Fig. 6.19 Conversion efﬁciency of the intermediate-band solar cell (AM1.5D, maximum concentration). A maximum conversion efﬁciency of 67.6% is reached for a band-gap energy of 1.91 eV between the conduction band and the valence band, and an energy gap of 1.22 eV between the valence band maximum and the intermediate-band quasi-Fermi level representative methods that are considered presently, e.g., utilization of quantum structures with nanoscale sizes and approaches based on impurities. Structures that possess quantum mechanically discretized energy levels are called quantum structures. Usually, this discretization appears as a result of conﬁning electrons or holes spatially within structures that possess extensions on the order of nanometers in at least one direction. The quantum structures with small sizes on the nanoscale are also called nanostructures. We can classify them into quantum wells with a two-dimensional degree of freedom, quantum wires with a one-dimensional degree of freedom, and quantum dots without any degree of freedom. The quantum wells are widely used in devices like semiconductor lasers and photodetectors. The intermediate-band solar cell concept requires optical transitions from the intermedi- ate band to the conduction band. It is important to note that normally incident light cannot induce optical transitions from the intermediate band formed by a quantum 6.4 Intermediate-Band Solar Cell 105 well to the conduction band since such an absorption is forbidden according to the so-called optical selection rule. Furthermore, even under oblique incidence of the light, Snells law implies that the light inside the solar cell will be refracted toward the normal direction because the refractive index of the material used for the solar cell is larger than that of air. Fortunately, the selection rule is relaxed in case of the quan- tum dot structure, and therefore the optical transition from the intermediate level formed by the quantum dot to the conduction band is possible. However, another problem arises for the quantum dot structure. Since it possesses a conﬁnement in all three directions, the energy level of each quantum dot is independent (and even varies slightly due to imperfect growth). Therefore, a band cannot be established at low quantum dot densities. Consequently, the neighboring quantum dots are brought closer together to form a band (this concept is explained in Chap. 8). The resulting structure is the so-called quantum dot superlattice [5]. InAs is frequently used as material for quantum dots, since its crystal growth can be well controlled. On the other hand, representative materials that employ impurity levels for the intermediate band are, for example, Si doped with indium (In) [6], GaNAs formed by doping of GaAs with nitrogen (N) [7], or ZnTeO prepared by doping ZnTe with oxygen (O) [8]. Because these materials systems contain impurities whose crys- tallographic and electronic properties are strongly different from those of the host semiconductor, they are termed highly mismatched alloys. All of them employ the energy level that is formed in the band gap of the host semiconductor by the impurity as the intermediate band. 6.5 Two-Step Photon Up-Conversion Solar Cell In the intermediate-band solar cell, two-step photon up-conversion process following the absorption of two below-gap photons can widely cover the solar spectrum and produce additional photocurrent without degrading the photovoltage. Such two-step photoexcitation is known to occur at heterointerfaces. The concept of the two-step photon up-conversion solar cell [9] is schematically shown in Fig. 6.20. The two- step photon up-conversion solar cell is a single-junction solar cell consisting of a wide-gap semiconductor and a narrow-gap semiconductor. Sunlight shines on the n-layer (left-hand side of Fig. 6.20). High-energy photons are absorbed in the wide- gap semiconductor, and generated electrons and holes drift in opposite directions and reach the n-type wide-gap semiconductor and the p-type narrow-gap semiconductor, respectively. On the other hand, below-gap photons of the wide-gap semiconductor pass through the wide-gap semiconductor without absorption and excite the narrow- gap semiconductor. The photogenerated electrons in the narrow-gap semiconductor drift toward the wide-gap semiconductor/ narrow-gap semiconductor heterointerface andareobstructedthere,whilephotogeneratedholesreachthep-layer.Theobstructed electrons at the heterointerface are prevented from recombining with holes because they are separated in the narrow-gap semiconductor. The dense accumulation of electrons at the heterointerface enables efﬁcient intraband optical absorption, and 106 6 Actual Calculation of Solar Cell Efﬁciencies Fig. 6.20 Schematic band lineup of the two-step up-conversion solar cell electrons excited at the conduction band offset of heterointerface are collected at the n-type wide-gap semiconductor. We deal with similar calculation framework and procedure to that of the intermediate-band solar cell except for the valence band offset at the heterointer- face. The relationship between current and voltage of the wide-gap semiconductor, I WGS and V WGS, can be written by using the generation rate of electronhole pair by the Sun FWGS s , the generation rate of electronhole pair under the thermal equi- librium FWGS c0 , and the recombination rate of electronhole pair under the sunlight illumination FWGS c  V WGS . In Fig. 6.22, the slopes of VOC for the two-step photon up-conversion solar cell are slightly steeper than that of the single-junction solar cell at lower sunlight concentrations, which arises from the voltage boost effect V C caused by the two-step photon up-conversion occurring at the heterointerface. With the increase in solar concentration, V C increases and ﬁnally reaches E C. As shown in Fig. 6.22, once V C saturates, the slope of VOC becomes small. At lower concentrations, the increases in V NGS and V C contribute to the increase in VOC. Conversely, at higher concentrations, only the increase in V NGS drives the increase in VOC, resulting in the shallow slope. In this calculation, ENGS is optimized to maximize the efﬁciency at each EWGS. When EWGS is small, the calculated efﬁciency approaches to that of the single-junction solar cell, because the band discontinuity of E C becomes small. The efﬁciency of the two-step photon up-conversion solar cell increases with EWGS. Then, the calculated efﬁciency curve reaches the maximum. With the increase in E C, the optimum EWGS exhibiting the maximum increases and its peak efﬁciency decreases. As mentioned at the beginning of this section, the generation rate at the heteroin- terface is generally weak compared with that of bulk material in the two-step photon up-conversion solar cell. On the other hand, the generation rate via the intermediate band in the intermediate-band solar cell is also generally weak because the quantum structures or highly mismatched alloys have lower density of states. Curve (a) is the result for the intermediate-band solar cell, curve (b) that for the two-step photon up-conversion solar cell for E C-to-E V ratio of 1:0, and curve (c) the result for the two-step photon up-conversion solar cell for E C-to-E V ratio of 3:2 114 6 Actual Calculation of Solar Cell Efﬁciencies 6.6 Solar Cells with Spectral Converters In order to improve the solar cell conversion efﬁciency, we have to make best use of the sunlight, which broadly distributes in the range from the ultraviolet to the infrared. The tandem solar cell and the intermediate-band solar cell analyzed in Sects. 6.3 and 6.4 enabled an improvement of the conversion efﬁciency by a redesign of the solar cell architecture in accordance with the solar spectrum. In that sense, the solar cells with spectral converters do the opposite. Instead of redesigning the solar cell architecture, they allow us to improve the conversion efﬁciency by reshaping the solar spectrum and match it with the solar cells speciﬁc requirement. We can classify the solar cells with spectral converters into two types, that is, those that reduce the transmission losses by converting several low-energy photons into a high-energy photon via up-conversion, and those that reduce the thermaliza- tion losses by converting a high-energy photon into several low-energy photons via down-conversion. The conversion is performed by some external device and not by the solar cell itself. The intermediate-band solar cell actually uses the up-conversion process realized by its internal structure; i.e., it absorbs two photons with frequencies νVI and νIC to generate an electron with frequency νVC = νg via the intermediate band. In this section, we calculate for the conversion efﬁciency that can be realized by a solar cell with a spectral converter that employs down-conversion. Consider a single-junction solar cell with a down-conversion layer in front of the cell as shown in Fig. 6.29. For the solar spectrum, we shall use AM1.5G without concentration of sunlight. Further, assume that the band-gap energy of the down- conversion layer is 2Eg with Eg being the band-gap energy of the single-junction solar cell. The photons among the incident sunlight with energy above 2Eg are absorbed by the down-conversion layer, and the photons with energy below 2Eg are transmitted by the down-conversion layer and reach the solar cell. The photons absorbed in the down-conversion layer undergo a wavelength conversion with energy conversion efﬁciency ηDC, and we assume that all photons that have been converted to longer wavelengths fall onto the solar cell. In the simple geometrical arrangement shown in Fig. 6.29, it is difﬁcult to realize such a perfect illumination of the solar cell that includes all converted photons, because the up-conversion layer equally emits photons into both directions, toward the solar cell and toward the Sun. For the sake of simplicity, we shall consider that there exists some technique that allows us to focus all photons that were converted to longer wavelengths onto the solar cell. In case of ηDC = 100%, nothing special occurs since the n photons with energy above 2Eg that are incident on the down-conversion layer simply loose a part of the energy and exactly n photons are emitted from the down-conversion layer as photons with energy Eg, which enter the solar cell. In case that ηDC equals 200%, each photon is split into two photons with half energy (Eg). If n photons with energy above 2Eg enter the down-conversion layer, the resulting number of photons emitted from the down-conversion layer becomes 2n. Since these are absorbed by the solar cell, the energy that would have been lost by thermalization in the semiconductor can now be used more effectively and result in a larger current. If a signiﬁcant number of 6.6 Solar Cells with Spectral Converters 115 Fig. 6.29 A schematic for a solar cell with a spectral converter that employs down-conversion photons with energy above 3Eg are incident on a conversion layer with ηDC = 300%, a further pronounced increase in the efﬁciency can be expected. In any case, if all photons emitted by the down-conversion layer can be absorbed by the single-junction solar cell, we can expect a positive effect as long as ηDC is above 100%. Intensive investigations are being performed to realize such a down-conversion using rare earth ions [10]. The key element in the idea of reshaping the solar spectrum using down-conversion is to increase the number of photons incident on the solar cell by exploiting the photon excess energy. Let us proceed with the derivation of the conversion efﬁciency for the device in Fig. 6.29.
