For the sake of completeness, we note that λEg is the wavelength corresponding to the band-gap energy, N(λ) is the incident photon ﬂux per unit area and unit wavelength, and ηDC is the energy conversion efﬁciency of the down-conversion layer. From Fig. 6.30, we ﬁnd that the conversion efﬁciency reaches 40.5% for a solar cell with Eg = 0.94 eV. This is a conversion efﬁciency increase of about 8% compared to the maximum theoretical conversion efﬁciency of 32.7% for the single-junction solar cell analyzed in Sect. 6.1. The output voltage of the solar cell with the spectral converter stays the same, but due to the down-conversion of the high-energy photons the current yield is increased, which enhances the conversion efﬁciency. According to the above result, a semiconductor with a band gap near 0.94 eV is most appropriate for this type of solar cell. Hence, Si with Eg 1.1 eV is suited for implementation in solar cells with spectral converters that employ down-conversion. Intheabove,weconsideredtheeffectiveutilizationoftheexcessenergyofphotons by photon splitting. In the same way, we can also imagine that a photon with excess energy generates more than one electronhole pair upon being absorbed inside the solar cell. Through recent investigations, it became evident that this phenomenon can efﬁciently occur in semiconductor nanostructures [11]. 118 6 Actual Calculation of Solar Cell Efﬁciencies 6.7 Inﬂuence of the Weather In the discussions so far, we considered that the Sun shines brightly; i.e., there is not a single cloud that interferes with the rays falling from space onto the Earths ground. However, not all days are clear, and when the days of the year pass we encounter cloudy and rainy weather, or even snow. In order to design a solar cell structure that meets the requirements imposed by the weather on a speciﬁc site where the solar cell will be installed, we have to clarify the inﬂuence of the weather on the solar cells conversion efﬁciency and the output power. On a cloudy or rainy day, the solar cells generated amount of electricity decreases because the intensity of the sunlight is reduced. In this section, we calculate the conversion efﬁciency and the output power that are obtained on a cloudy day. By comparing them with the calculation results for AM1.5 which were elaborated in the previous sections, we shall investigate how the solar cell characteristics depend on the weather condition. On a cloudy day, the amount of light reaching the Earths ground is reduced because the light from the Sun is partially blocked by clouds. An example for a solar spectrum on a cloudy day is shown in Fig. 6.31. The AM1.5G spectrum is also plotted for comparison. This irradiance data shown here is obtained from the spectrum modeler SPCTRAL2 [12] provided by the Electricity, Resources, and Building Systems Integration Center of the National Renewable Energy Laboratory (NREL),USA.AlthoughSPCTRAL2isamodelerthatevaluatesthesolarspectrum reaching the Earths ground by using parameters for the atmospheric condition, both the atmosphere and the clouds are an accumulation of small particles. In other words, the inﬂuence of the clouds on the solar spectrum is similar to that of the atmosphere. Therefore, in this section we approximate the cloud condition by an atmosphere that was calculated by SPCTRAL2 for this purpose. Considering a spectrum shown in Fig. 6.31, the integrated irradiance within 03000 nm is 58 W/m2 which is about 17 times smaller than the corresponding value for AM1.5G; i.e., compared to AM1.5G, the total available power of the sunlight on a cloudy day is reduced by 94%. In order to compare the relative shapes of the solar spectrum on a cloudy day and the AM1.5G spectrum, we rescale the cloudy day spectrum by a factor of 17 to obtain the same maximum irradiance and show the result in Fig. 6.32. We ﬁnd that the cloudy day spectrum exhibits a relative decrease on the long- wavelength (low-energy) side when compared to AM1.5G. Let us analyze how the conversion efﬁciency and output power of a solar cell change in case of illumination with such a solar spectrum. In the following discussion, we compare the calculation results for the solar cells analyzed so far, i.e., the single- junction solar cell, the multi-junction tandem solar cell, and the intermediate-band solar cell. The calculations are performed for 1 sun illumination (X = 1). (1) Single-junction solar cell The conversion efﬁciency of the single-junction solar cell is shown in Fig. 6.33. Com- pared to the result for AM1.5G, the conversion efﬁciency during cloudy weather is smaller. The maximum conversion efﬁciency is 31% at Eg = 1.39 eV. The efﬁciency 6.7 Inﬂuence of the Weather 119 Fig. 6.31 Curve (a) is an example for a solar spectrum on a cloudy day, and curve (b) represents the AM1.5G spectrum. The total energy of the sunlight on a cloudy day is about one-seventeenth of the value for AM1.5G Fig. 6.32 Comparison of the relative spectral shapes. Here, the solar spectrum for the cloudy day and the AM1.5G spectrum shown in Fig. 6.31 have been normalized with respect to the maximum irradiance. On a cloudy day, the relative contribution of sunlight in the long-wavelength region will be slightly reduced in the long-wavelength region (8001300 nm) decrease is especially signiﬁcant on the low-energy side, and the band-gap energy for the maximum conversion efﬁciency is shifted slightly. Additionally, since the total power of the sunlight on a cloudy day is about one-seventeenth of that under clear sky, the amount of generated electricity drops drastically. A detailed discus- sion on the large change in the amount of generated electricity is provided later. Here, we ﬁrst focus on the fact that the conversion efﬁciency decrease due to the clouds is marginal. This strongly resembles the difference between the conversion efﬁciencies under AM0 and AM1.5G. The discussion in Sect. 6.1 revealed that the theoretical conversion efﬁciency for AM1.5G exceeds that of AM0 as a result of the reduced light intensity in the ultraviolet and infrared regions of the AM1.5G solar spectrum (due to the scattering and absorption by molecules in the atmosphere). The ultraviolet light is usually the origin of the thermalization losses in solar cells, and the infrared light is usually the origin of transmission losses in solar cells. In other 120 6 Actual Calculation of Solar Cell Efﬁciencies Fig. 6.33 Conversion efﬁciency of a single-junction solar cell under the solar spectrum for cloudy weather without concentration of light. During cloudy weather, the conversion efﬁciency maximum is obtained for a larger band gap. The conversion efﬁciency is less than that obtained under AM1.5G; a maximum of 31% is reached at a band-gap energy of 1.39 eV words, the match between the solar spectrum and the solar cell increased owing to the reduction of ultraviolet and infrared light intensities. Also in case of the cloudy weather, we observe a signiﬁcant reduction of the near-infrared light intensity in the solar spectrum. Although an improvement in the efﬁciency might have been expected due to the reduction of absorption losses, the relatively broad reduction of the intensity in the region of the optimum band gap results in an overall small drop of the conversion efﬁciency. From Fig. 6.33, we ﬁnd that the band-gap energy for the maximum conversion efﬁciency during cloudy weather is larger than that for clear sky. Because the relative spectral contribution of sunlight in the near-infrared region decreased, the relative losses through thermalization would increase if we employ the optimum Eg for AM1.5G illumination. Consequently, a larger band-gap energy is more suited to maintain a large conversion efﬁciency during cloudy weather because the thermalization losses can be reduced. (2) Multi-junction solar cells Now let us investigate the inﬂuence of the cloudy day spectrum on the dual-junction and triple-junction solar cells. Figure 6.34 plots the calculation result for the conversion efﬁciency of a dual- junction solar cell under the solar spectrum during cloudy weather without concen- tration of sunlight. The horizontal axis is the band-gap energy of the top cell, and the vertical axis is that of the bottom cell. The conversion efﬁciency reaches its maxi- mum of 42.3% for the band-gap combination Ec1 = 1.60 eV and Ec2 = 0.94 eV. We note that the band-gap energy of the top cell optimized for the cloudy day spec- trum is larger than the optimum for AM1.5G (Fig. 6.10; the maximum conversion efﬁciency under AM1.5G illumination without concentration of sunlight is 45.7% for the band-gap combination Ec1 = 1.63 eV and Ec2 = 0.96 eV). Figure 6.35 shows the calculation result of the conversion efﬁciency of a triple- junction solar cell obtained under the cloudy day solar spectrum without concen- tration of sunlight. We assumed the same band gap for the bottom cell as for the 6.7 Inﬂuence of the Weather 121 Fig. 6.34 Conversion efﬁciency of a dual-junction solar cell obtained for unconcentrated sunlight (X = 1) and by employing the spectrum during cloudy weather. At the point Ec1 = 1.60 eV and Ec2 = 0.94 eV, the conversion efﬁciency reaches its maximum of 42.3% calculation in Fig. 6.12 (Ge with Ec3 = 0.66 eV) and evaluated the conversion efﬁciencies as a function of the band-gap values for the top and middle cells. The horizontal axis is the band-gap energy of the top cell, and the vertical axis is that of the middle cell. As shown in Fig. 6.12, the maximum conversion efﬁciency for AM1.5G illumination without concentration of sunlight is 50.4% for the band-gap combination Ec1 = 1.75 eV, Ec2 = 1.17 eV, and Ec3 = 0.66 eV. The band-gap energies of both top and middle cells for maximum conversion efﬁciency during a cloudy day are Ec1 = 1.77 eV and Ec2 = 1.20 eV (Fig. 6.35). Here, the maxi- mum conversion efﬁciency becomes 46.5%. Both optimum band-gap energies for the cloudy day spectrum are larger than those for the clear sky spectrum. The reason is the same as that for the single-junction solar cell; i.e., since the relative contribution of the near-infrared light in the solar spectrum on a cloudy day decreased, a larger band-gap energy is more suited to suppress the thermalization losses. (3) Intermediate-band solar cell In Fig. 6.36, we show the conversion efﬁciency during cloudy weather for the intermediate-band solar cell under unconcentrated sunlight. Compared to the case of AM1.5G illumination in Sect. 6.4, the maximum conversion efﬁciency during cloudy weather is lower. Furthermore, the band-gap energy for the maximum con- version efﬁciency during cloudy weather shifts to higher energies as expected. The change in the optimum band-gap energy with respect to the value obtained for clear sky is less than the changes that have been conﬁrmed for the other solar cell archi- tectures. The maximum conversion efﬁciency is 44.8% for a band-gap energy of 2.46 eV (between the conduction band and the valence band), and a gap energy of 1.53 eV between the valence band maximum and the intermediate-band quasi-Fermi level. The smaller loss can be explained by the design of the intermediate-band solar cell, which mixes parallel and serial elements. In such a case, the relative reduction of the near-infrared region in the solar spectrum leads to a relative decrease in the current of the serially connected element. 122 6 Actual Calculation of Solar Cell Efﬁciencies Fig. 6.35 Conversion efﬁciency of a triple-junction solar cell obtained for unconcentrated AM1.5G sunlight (X = 1) and by employing the solar spectrum during cloudy weather. For the bottom cell material, we assumed Ge with a band-gap energy of Ec3 = 0.66 eV. At the point Ec1 = 1.77 eV and Ec2 = 1.20 eV, the conversion efﬁciency reaches its maximum of 46.5% Fig. 6.36 Conversion efﬁciency of an intermediate-band solar cell obtained for unconcentrated AM1.5G sunlight (X = 1) with the solar spectrum during cloudy weather. The maximum conversion efﬁciency is 44.8%, which is realized for a band-gap energy of 2.46 eV and a gap energy of 1.53 eV between the valence band maximum and the intermediate-band quasi-Fermi level So far, we discussed one speciﬁc example of a solar spectrum on a cloudy day and considered the inﬂuence of weather changes on the solar cell performance from the viewpoint of the optimized band-gap energies. The composition of the solar spectrum changes due to the ﬁlter effect of the clouds, and accordingly the conversion efﬁciencies decrease slightly. But more important than this efﬁciency decrease is the decrease in the output power. Since the total power of the sunlight on a cloudy day is much less than the total power under clear sky, the reduction in the amount of generated electricity cannot be neglected. Regarding the above results, one important point has to be emphasized. The actual value of the conversion efﬁciency is inﬂuenced 6.7 Inﬂuence of the Weather 123 by the solar spectrum. Consequently, depending on the speciﬁc weather condition considered, slightly different results may be obtained. In the discussion below, we assume a solar cell that is optimized for the solar spectrum obtained under clear sky conditions. We then clarify the inﬂuence of the cloudy weather by calculating the output powers and the conversion efﬁciencies upon illumination with different solar spectra for cloudy weather. Before the actual calculation results are presented, we have to provide a more detailed explanation regarding the cloudy day solar spectra that are used in the cal- culations. At the beginning of this section, we mentioned that the solar spectrum during cloudy weather exhibits a signiﬁcant intensity reduction when compared to AM1.5G. This reduction is due to the scattering and absorption by the clouds, but the degree of reduction differs according to the composition and thickness of the clouds. The main components of a cloud are water droplets and tiny dust particles. The water droplets absorb a part of the direct incident light, and the dust scatters the sunlight. The strength of these effects depends on the wavelength, and they alter the shape of the solar spectrum. In the following, we cover the solar spectra that are obtained by changing these parameters within the range dictated by usual cloudy weather. First, we conﬁrm the change in the spectral shape. In order to imagine the situation in simple terms, hereafter we use the term cloud thickness to refer to the amount of dust and the term water content to refer to the amount of water droplets. An increase in the amount of dust is equivalent to an increase of the cloud thick- ness. The light incident on the cloud is strongly scattered, and thus the light intensity decays as it passes through the cloud. The total intensity reduction of the transmitted light is determined by the path length of light passing through the cloud and the so- called turbidity coefﬁcient, which is a function that depends on the amount of dust. The distance travelled by the light as it passes through the cloud is set to unity for light that is normally incident on a horizontally extending cloud. The distance increases in accordance with the incident angle. In other words, we have the same behavior as for the AM deﬁned in Chap. 2. The turbidity coefﬁcient is a coefﬁcient that describes how much the light intensity decreases per unit length travelled through the cloud. The value in case of a path length equal to unity constitutes the reference value for the calculations. The light intensity of light at wavelength λ that reaches the Earths ground surface after passing the cloud, Iout, is represented by Eq. (6.66) [13]. Iout(λ) = Iin(λ)exp{τ(λ)  L} (6.66) Iin is the initial light intensity before scattering in the cloud occurs, L is the distance travelled by the light through the cloud, and τ(λ) is the turbidity coefﬁcient for L = 1. As shown in Eq. (6.67), the turbidity coefﬁcient τ(λ) can be expressed by using the turbidity at a certain reference wavelength, βn, and an exponent αn which depends on the dust particle size. τ(λ) = βn  λαn (6.67) 124 6 Actual Calculation of Solar Cell Efﬁciencies This turbidity βn is deﬁned as the cloud thickness. The value of αn changes with the particle size and thus inﬂuences the shape of the solar spectrum. However, the present calculations use a ﬁxed value of αn = 1.14, which is the same value as that for the AM1.5G atmosphere. For an accurate theoretical description of the scattering, the size difference between the particles in the atmosphere and the particles in the clouds has to be accounted for, but here we omit this detail. The calculations below are performed for values of βn ranging from 0 to 30. This range represents the standard values for a cloudy day and can be determined from the actual observation data [14]. The amount of water is treated as outlined below. If the amount of water in the cloud increases, the light passing through the cloud is absorbed stronger and decays faster. The amount of water is equivalent to the precipitation observed on the Earths ground surface if the entire water in the cloud has fallen down as rain. Therefore, it has a unit of a length, cm. For the calculation in this section, we employ spectra that include values for the amount of water within the range from 0 to 10 cm. First, let us conﬁrm the characteristic changes that occur in the spectral shape of the sunlight upon changing the cloud thickness or the amount of water. Figure 6.37 shows how the solar spectrum changes when we ﬁx the amount of water to the standard value for cloudy weather (3 cm) and vary the cloud thickness only. As the clouds become thicker, the solar spectrum is stronger affected by the scattering via the dust particles. In particular, a large decrease is observed on the short-wavelength side (i.e., at high energies). We can conﬁrm that the total power of the sunlight decreases drastically as βn increases. To investigate the dependence of the solar spectrum on the other parameter, we ﬁx the cloud thickness to the standard value of 25 and vary the amount of water (Fig. 6.38). As the amount of water increases, the solar spectrum slowly decreases in the near-infrared regime. There are several deep indentations in the solar spectrum, and most of them correspond to the absorption bands of water (H2O). Consequently, Fig. 6.37 Relation between the solar spectrum and the cloud thickness. As the thickness of the clouds increases, the total power of the solar spectrum decreases while the change at the short-wavelength side is initially more pronounced than in the long-wavelength region 6.7 Inﬂuence of the Weather 125 Fig. 6.38 Relation between the spectral shape of sunlight and the amount of water in the clouds. The intensity reduction induced by the water droplets is prominent in a certain wavelength range of the infrared regime. The decay of the light as it passes through the cloud is more signiﬁcant for a larger amount of water, and therefore the indentations in the spectrum become deeper Fig. 6.39 Relation between the output power and the cloud thickness. As the cloud thickness increases, all solar cell types exhibit a strong decrease in the output power the inﬂuence of the water droplets becomes more signiﬁcant for a larger amount of water. However, although a change in the spectral shape can be perceived, the total reduction of the sunlight power stays on an insigniﬁcant level. We shall now actually examine the changes in the output power and the conversion efﬁciency of solar cells that are exposed to solar spectra for different cloud conditions. First, we consider how the amount of generated electricity and the conversion efﬁciency change in case of a ﬁxed amount of water and varying cloud thickness. We assume the standard value of 3 cm for the amount of water and change the cloud thickness in the range from 0 to 30. The calculation is performed for the single- junction solar cell, the intermediate-band solar cell, the triple-junction solar cell, and the dual-junction solar cell. For the band-gap energies of these solar cells, we shall employ the values that provide maximum conversion efﬁciency for AM1.5G illumination without concentration of sunlight. The calculation results for the output power and the conversion efﬁciency are shown in Figs. 6.39 and 6.40, respectively. 126 6 Actual Calculation of Solar Cell Efﬁciencies Fig. 6.40 Relation between the conversion efﬁciency and the cloud thickness. The trend of each solar cell type is different As the clouds become thicker, the energy entering the solar cell decreases, and the output power decreases with almost the same rate (Fig. 6.39). On the other hand, the change in the conversion efﬁciency (Figs. 6.33, 6.34, 6.35 and 6.36) is less pronounced than that observed for the output power. This is because the efﬁciency is a value that expresses the ratio of output power to incident light power. In other words, even if the amount of generated electricity (the numerator) decreases strongly on a cloudy day, since this reduction corresponds to the reduction of the incident light energy (i.e., the denominator), the conversion efﬁciency (the ratio between them) does not change much. Furthermore, it is important to note that each solar cell exhibits a different dependence on the cloud thickness (Fig. 6.31). In case of the multi-junction solar cells, the efﬁciencies initially increase as the clouds become thicker, and then decrease gently. The results for the intermediate-band solar cell and the single-junction solar cell predict an initial decrease and then an intermediate gentle increase which is ﬁnally followed by another decrease. All curves exhibit a non-monotone behavior. In the following, we analyze the changes in the output power and conversion efﬁciency for a ﬁxed cloud thickness and a varying amount of water. The amount of water changes with season and location, but is on the order of 0.5 cm for clear sky and 10 cm for heavy rain. Here, we perform calculations for values up to 10 cm. For each solar cell type, we shall employ those band-gap energies that result in the maximum conversion efﬁciency for AM1.5G illumination without concentration of sunlight. The calculation results for the output power and the conversion efﬁciency are shown in Figs. 6.41 and 6.42, respectively. As explained above, while the amount of water alters the spectrum of the Sun in the near-infrared region, the inﬂuence on the total power is not severe. A similar effect is observed for both the amount of generated electricity and the conversion efﬁciency; that is, they exhibit no signiﬁcant reduction upon increasing the amount of water. Further, the degree of the reduction in the conversion efﬁciency of the multi-junction solar cell is large when compared to the reduction observed for the other solar cell 6.7 Inﬂuence of the Weather 127 Fig. 6.41 Relation between the output power and the amount of water in the clouds. The increase in the amount of water is accompanied by a slight decrease in the output power. The observed decrease is small when compared to that for the variation of the cloud thicknesses. Further, the decrease observed for the multi-junction tandem solar cells and the intermediate-band solar cell is more signiﬁcant than that of the single-junction solar cell Fig. 6.42 Relation between the conversion efﬁciency and the amount of water in the clouds. The intermediate-band architecture shows almost no decrease, but the multi-junction tandem solar cells exhibit a relatively steep decrease. The conversion efﬁciency of the single-junction solar cell increases as the amount of water increases types. In other words, the multi-junction solar cell is sensitive to the change in the spectral shape of the sunlight. In that respect, the multi-junction solar cell character- istics are easily inﬂuenced. The conversion efﬁciency of the intermediate-band solar cell exhibits almost no change and thus is hardly inﬂuenced by the amount of water. In contrast, the conversion efﬁciency of the single-junction solar cell increases upon increasing the amount of water because the match between the solar cell and the resulting solar spectrum becomes better. 128 6 Actual Calculation of Solar Cell Efﬁciencies 6.8 Inﬂuence of the Temperature InSect.6.2,weshowedthatthetheoreticalconversionefﬁciencylimitofanysolarcell increases signiﬁcantly if we employ concentrated sunlight for excitation. However, as is the case for focusing light by a magnifying glass, the solar cell temperature will increase immediately if sunlight is focused on the device because more light with energy in excess to the band-gap energy is absorbed. If a semiconductor is illuminated by light, electrons are excited from the valence band to the conduction band. In general, electrons are excited to a state above the conduction band minimum. The electrons reach the stable ground state at the band edge via energy relaxation, that is, by emitting the energy in excess to the band gap via phonons. Due to the emission of these phonons, the lattice temperature of the crystal increases. So far, we discussed solar cells at a temperature of T c = 300 K (and 0 K in Chap. 3), but the temperature of an actual solar can exceed this value signiﬁcantly. Consequently, we have to specify how the conversion efﬁciency is inﬂuenced by such an increase in the solar cell temperature. The conclusion that can be drawn from the discussion below is that the conversion efﬁciency decreases for increasing solar cell temperatures, and this constitutes an important issue for concentrator solar cells. It is known that the band-gap energy Eg of a semiconductor usually decreases as the temperature increases. This behavior can be well explained by the semiempirical Varshni equation [15] shown in Eq. (6.68). Eg(T ) = E(0) αT 2 T + β (6.68) Here, T is the temperature of the semiconductor, and α and β are experimen- tally determined constants. The temperature dependence of the Eg of the frequently employed solar cell materials silicon (Si), germanium (Ge), and gallium arsenide (GaAs) is given by Eqs. (6.69)(6.71) [16], respectively. Si: Eg(T ) = 1.17 4.37  104  T 2 T + 636 (6.69) GaAs: Eg(T ) = 1.519 5.405  104  T 2 T + 204 (6.70) Ge: Eg(T ) = 0.74 4.77  104  T 2 T + 235 (6.71) By plotting these equations in the range of 0500 K, we obtain the curves shown in Fig. 6.43. We ﬁnd that the band-gap energy monotonously decreases as the temperature increases. This is a result of the temperature-dependent extension of the lattice and change in the energy band that is formed by the electronlattice interactions. How- ever, since a discussion of these effects is not in the scope of this book, we omit a detailed explanation. The important point is that the temperature dependence of Eg 6.8 Inﬂuence of the Temperature 129 Fig. 6.43 Temperature dependence of the band-gap energies of GaAs, Si, and Ge becomes stronger for higher temperatures. At around 300 K, which is near room tem- perature, the changes in the band-gap energies of Si, Ge, and GaAs for a temperature increase of 1 C are 0.24, 0.39, and 0.45 meV, respectively. Let us evaluate the conversion efﬁciencies for different solar cell temperatures by employing the temperature dependence of the band gap. In Fig. 6.44, we show the detailed balance limit of the conversion efﬁciency for Si, Ge, and GaAs single-junction solar cells for AM1.5G illumination without concentration of sunlight. As the temperature increases, the conversion efﬁciencies of the Si, Ge, and GaAs single-junction solar cells decrease without exception. Furthermore, we can estimate the expected conversion efﬁciency if we select a certain semiconductor material and can predict the operating temperature of the solar cell. We note that depending on the temperature, the solar cell with the highest conversion efﬁciency may change as is the case for Si and GaAs, which cross at 308 K. The extent of the conversion efﬁciency change for a certain temperature difference depends on the band gap of the semiconductor. In Fig. 6.45, we show band-gap energy dependence of the detailed balance limit of the conversion efﬁciencies at three different temperatures (100, 300, and 500 K). Fig. 6.44 Theoretical temperature dependence of the detailed balance limit of the conversion efﬁciency for AM1.5G illumination without concentration of sunlight Fig. 6.45 Detailed balance limit of the conversion efﬁciencies (for AM1.5G, X = 1) at 100, 300, and 500 K 132 6 Actual Calculation of Solar Cell Efﬁciencies The band-gap energies of the representative semiconductors Si, Ge, and GaAs at 300 K are indicated with the broken lines. To verify the inﬂuence of the temper- ature change on the conversion efﬁciency, we compare the difference between the conversion efﬁciencies for 300 and 500 K in Fig. 6.46. The band-gap energies of Si, Ge, and GaAs at room temperature are 1.12, 0.67, and 1.43 eV, respectively. The largest conversion efﬁciency decrease in Fig. 6.46 is observed near the band-gap energy of Ge (Eg = 0.67 eV); the efﬁciency decreases by 12% at 0.70 eV. The reduction in the conversion efﬁciency becomes smaller as Eg increases. Finally, let us separately consider the contributions of the temperature-induced efﬁciency loss in terms of the band-gap change and the carrier density change. From Fig. 6.45, we can conﬁrm that the conversion efﬁciency decreases for band-gap energies smaller than 0.67 eV (which is the Eg of Ge). On the other hand, for semi- conductors with band-gap energies above about 1.2 eV such as GaAs, the conversion efﬁciency decreases upon increasing temperature. When we look at the conversion efﬁciency curve at 300 Kin Fig. 6.45, we might say that in case of GaAs, the efﬁciency should increase for higher temperature since the band gap becomes smaller. How- ever, even for GaAs with its large band gap, the conversion efﬁciency decreases upon increasingtemperatures.Thismeansthattheinﬂuenceofthetemperaturedependence of the carrier density is stronger than the improvement in the conversion efﬁciency due to the band-gap energy reduction. As explained in Sect. 4.2, the radiative and the non-radiative recombination rates are proportional to the product of electron and hole densities. The concentrations of electrons and holes increase upon raising the temperature, and as a result, the electronhole recombination becomes signiﬁ- cantly strong, which is accompanied by a decrease in the conversion efﬁciency. This contribution is the most important factor in evaluating the changes of the solar cell characteristics. The partial compensation between the band-gap decrease for higher Fig. 6.46 Difference between the conversion efﬁciencies for 300 and 500 K as shown in Fig. 6.45 6.8 Inﬂuence of the Temperature 133 temperatures and the inﬂuence of the carrier density increase is the reason for the relatively gentle temperature dependence of the GaAs conversion efﬁciency shown in Fig. 6.44. 6.9 Indoor Photovoltaic Cell In this chapter, we have calculated the conversion efﬁciency for various types of solar cells. Here, we consider photovoltaic cells efﬁciently harvesting indoor light. Indoor photovoltaic cells are already used in our daily life for wristwatches, calculators, various wireless devices, and stuff. After the invention of blue light-emitting diode (LED), white LEDs have been widely spreading for indoor light. In this section, we focus on indoor photovoltaic cells harvesting white LED light. Typical emission spectra of cool white LED and warm white LED are shown in Fig. 6.47 along with the AM1.5G spectrum. The white color is obtained by yellow phosphor excited by a blue LED. Thereby, the spectrum dominantly shows peaks in the blue and yellow regions; the sharp peak appearing around 450 nm in Fig. 6.47 corresponds to the blue emission from a nitride semiconductor-based LED, and the broad yellow band around 600 nm comes from the phosphor. Let us calculate the conversion efﬁciency using the spectra shown in Fig. 6.47. It should be noted that this spectrum is not absolute spectral irradiance but relative spectral irradiance. In order to calculate the conversion efﬁciency, absolute spectral irradiance is necessary. We convert relative spectral irradiance to absolute spectral irradiance. Here, we introduce a new term, illuminance, which is a quantity indicating brightness humans feel. The illuminance is deﬁned as a luminous ﬂux per one square meter, and the unit is lm/m2 or lux, where lm (lumen) is the unit of luminous ﬂux. A luminous ﬂux is deﬁned as the intensity of light that can be perceived by humans. Fig. 6.47 Relative spectral irradiance. Curve (a) is the data for AM1.5G, curve (b) that for a typical cool white LED, and curve (c) that for a typical warm white LED 134 6 Actual Calculation of Solar Cell Efﬁciencies Fig. 6.48 Photopic luminous efﬁciency curve What is the difference between the luminous ﬂux and the irradiation intensity? The irradiation intensity is a product of the photon ﬂux per unit area and the energy of photons. On the other hand, the illuminance and the luminous ﬂux are calibrated by light sensitivity for the human eye. The human eye has sensitivity in a range of 400700 nm. The Commission Internationale de lEclairage (CIE) establishes the photopic luminous efﬁciency function as a standard light sensitivity of human eye [17]. Photopic luminous efﬁciency function is shown in Fig. 6.48. This function peaks 683 lm/W at 555 nm and expands in the range of 400700 nm. Pﬂux, absolute(λ) is obtained by Fig. 6.48 and Eq. (6.72). Under the illumination of the white LED, it is interesting that the conversion efﬁciency exceeds 50% because the white LED contains less light components causing transmission and thermalization losses. ISO 8995-1:2002 (CIE S 008/E:2001) Chapter 7 Application Limits for the Ideal Conditions Abstract The solar cells covered in the previous chapters are idealized solar cells, which allowed us to evaluate the efﬁciency limits under various conditions. Of course, besides the conditions employed so far (like the transmission of photons with Ephoton  Eg), there exist plenty of other factors that lead to losses in the amount of generated electricity and thus are important for the actual development of solar cells. Due to these losses, the conversion efﬁciency of an actually fabricated solar cell is smaller than the theoretical efﬁciency limit. For example, if we use silicon (Si) for the solar cell absorber material, the detailed balance limit of the conversion efﬁciency is about 30%. This value can be obtained from Fig. 5.10 in Chap. 5 by looking up the efﬁ- ciency for the curve (b) at 1.1 eV, which is the band gap of Si. On the other hand, when we look up the present values for actual Si pn junction solar cells, we ﬁnd that the single crystal type has reached a maximum of 26.3% and the multi-crystalline type has reached a maximum of 22.3%, both being well below the theoretical efﬁciency limit[1]. In Chap. 3, we discussed the trade-off relation between the transmission and thermalization losses in the single-junction solar cell and derived the conversion efﬁciency limit that is imposed by these two types of losses. In considerations on the solar cell performance, such trade-off relations exist for various parameters. As another example for a trade-off relation, in the following sections, we introduce the semiconductor light absorption characteristics, which differ for each material. With this new concept, we discuss the solar cell conversion efﬁciency from a viewpoint that is different from the detailed balance model elaborated in the previous chapters. The trade-off presented in the following sections is a trade-off between the thickness of the semiconductor that is required for sufﬁcient light absorption and the distance that can be travelled by the generated electrons and holes. The latter property is essential for the efﬁcient extraction of carriers at the electrodes. It is straightforward that a thicker semiconductor can absorb more light. On the other hand, if the semiconduc- tor is too thick, the distance that has to be travelled by the photoexcited electrons and holes also becomes longer. If the path to be travelled is long, there is a high probability for the electron to collide with an obstacle before its electrical energy can  Springer Nature Singapore Pte Ltd. 2019 T. Kita et al., Energy Conversion Efﬁciency of Solar Cells, Green Energy and Technology, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-13-9089-0_7 139 140 7 Application Limits for the Ideal Conditions be used. Then the energy is unfortunately lost in spite of the efforts to generate it. A thicker semiconductor is not necessarily better; we have to consider the optimum thickness for efﬁcient extraction of the electrical energy. After discussing the light absorption characteristics of semiconductors in Sect. 7.1, we explain the transport of the generated electrons and holes inside the semiconductor in Sect. 7.2. In the last section, we consider two speciﬁc semiconductor materials, Si and GaAs, as examples and discuss the optimum semiconductor thickness in a solar cell. Keywords Absorption coefﬁcient  Minority carrier diffusion  Photocurrent density 7.1 Consideration of the Absorption Coefﬁcient The calculations performed up to Chap. 6 assume that all photons with energy above the semiconductor band-gap energy are absorbed, which omits any consideration of the thickness of the semiconductor absorber layer in the solar cell. However, a certain thickness of the semiconductor is required to actually absorb the light. The absorption coefﬁcient determines how much light can be absorbed by a material with a certain thickness. Let us consider the case of monochromatic light with wavelength λ and photon ﬂux 0 being incident on a material with thickness d as shown in Fig. 7.1. Assume that there is no reﬂection of light at the materials surface. When the material then absorbs the photon ﬂux , the remaining photon ﬂux 0  exits the back surface of the material. It is known that the logarithm of the ratio between the photon ﬂux 0 incident on the front surface and the photon ﬂux 0 exiting at the back surface is proportional to the thickness of the material. This connection is the law of attenuation of light by absorption in a material and is called the LambertBeer law. Equation (7.1) represents the LambertBeer law with α as the proportionality constant. Here, α is a positive number and deﬁned with respect to the natural loga- rithm (the logarithm that employs Napiers constant e as a base), which is useful for the following calculation. Fig. 7.1 Relation between the incident photon ﬂux, the absorbed photon ﬂux, and the transmitted photon ﬂux. In this ﬁgure, we neglect the reﬂection of the incident light 7.1 Consideration of the Absorption Coefﬁcient 141 Because we consider that the material absorbs a photon ﬂux corresponding to , the transmitted photon ﬂux is strictly smaller than the incident photon ﬂux. When we focus on the ratio of incident light to transmitted light inside the logarithm, we ﬁnd that it is less than unity and therefore the logarithm becomes negative. ln 0  0 d ln 0  0 = αd (7.1) The photon ﬂux that is absorbed by the material, , constitutes the important number for the solar cell. This is because each photon in the absorbed photon ﬂux leads to a photoexcitation of an electron and hole as explained in Chap. 1. Let us reformulate Eq. (7.1) and solve for . Since the LambertBeer law uses a logarithmic representation, let us ﬁrst revert it to the conventional linear representation. 0  0 = exp(αd) By rearranging this equation, we obtain the equation that expresses the photon ﬂux  as a function of the thickness d.  = 0{1 exp(αd)} (7.2) The α which is used as a proportionality constant is called the light absorption coefﬁcient. Its unit is the inverse of a length. As the absorption coefﬁcient becomes larger, more light is absorbed by the material, i.e.,  becomes larger. Furthermore, the absorption coefﬁcient is a value speciﬁc to the material and also depends on the wavelength of the incident light. To gain an accurate picture of the semiconductor absorption coefﬁcients, we use some literature values [2]. First, we consider the case of monocrystalline Si. As the reader might know, Si is todays most common semiconductor for solar cells. According to the literature, the absorption coefﬁcient of monocrystalline Si is about 1.8  104 cm1 for light with a wavelength of 500 nm. Let us substitute this value for α into Eq. (7.2) and calculate how much light can be absorbed. For the sake of simplicity, we assume that the thickness of the Si single crystal, d, is the inverse of the above value 1.8  104 cm1 (i.e., d = 0.56 µm). The exponent on the right-hand side of Eq. (7.2) then becomes αd = 1. With Napiers constant e = 2.72, the term in the parenthesis of the right- hand side can be evaluated,  1 eαd = 0.63. In other words, the thickness that is required to absorb about 63% of the incident photon ﬂux is equal to the inverse of the absorption coefﬁcient. Additionally, the material depth that results in an absorption of about 63% of the incident light is called the penetration depth of light (here, 0.56 µm). 142 7 Application Limits for the Ideal Conditions In the following, we consider the light absorption for the same material but use a different wavelength of the incident light. The absorption coefﬁcient of monocrys- talline Si with respect to light with a wavelength of 800 nm is about 1.0 103 cm1. When we perform the same calculation as for the light at 500 nm, we ﬁnd that a thickness of 10 µm is required to absorb 63% of the incident light with a wavelength of 800 nm. However, in case of the Si single crystal with a thickness of 0.56 µm, only a small amount of about 5% of the incident light at 800 nm can be absorbed. This means that, although a thickness of 0.56 µm allows to absorb more than half of the light at 500 nm, 95% are transmitted in case of 800 nm light. Next, let us consider a GaAs single crystal. As mentioned in Chap. 6, GaAs is a material that has the potential to realize the highest conversion efﬁciency within the single-junction scheme explained in the SQ theory. With respect to light with a wavelength of 500 nm, the GaAs absorption coefﬁcient is about 1.1  105 cm1. Therefore, the GaAs single crystal thickness that is required to absorb 63% of the light at 500 nm is only 0.09 µm. This absorption behavior for different materials and wavelengths is illustrated in Fig. 7.2. Additionally, in Fig. 7.3, we plot the wavelength dependence of the absorp- tion coefﬁcients of Si and GaAs. It can be clearly seen that the absorption coefﬁcient for each material exhibits a different wavelength dispersion characteristic. Because the extent of the light absorption directly inﬂuences the conversion efﬁciency, it is evident that the consideration of the light absorption coefﬁcient is extremely impor- tant for the prediction of the solar cell characteristics. The absorbed light ﬂux  explained above corresponds to the total amount of light that is absorbed while the light passes through the volume of the material, that is, from the front surface to the back surface. The distance from the location of the electronhole pair generation to the depletion layer region will become important Fig. 7.2 Different behavior of the light absorption in Si and GaAs single crystals with same thick- ness (0.56 µm) 7.1 Consideration of the Absorption Coefﬁcient 143 Fig. 7.3 Relation between the wavelength of the incident photon and the absorption coefﬁcient. The absorption spectrum reﬂects the unique wavelength dispersion characteristics of the material Fig. 7.4 Method for deriving the absorbed photon ﬂux in a cross-sectional volume at depth x for the detailed discussion of the diffusion of the minority carriers in the following Sect. 7.2. For this discussion, we now derive the photon ﬂux that is absorbed in a cross-sectional volume (thickness dx) at the depth x as measured from the surface of the material. In Fig. 7.4, we show an illustration of the derivation method. The photon ﬂux that is absorbed within this cross-sectional volume is obtained by calculating the difference between the photon ﬂux that is absorbed within a distance x and the photon ﬂux that is absorbed within a distance x + dx. The inﬁnitesimal photon ﬂux absorbed in the cross section at depth x is the result for dx approaching 0. If we employ Eq. (7.2), which expresses the penetration-depth dependence of the photon ﬂux for a certain material, the following derivative of  is obtained: lim dx0  dx = lim dx0 0  1 exp{α(x + dx)} 0{1 exp(αx)} dx (7.3) 144 7 Application Limits for the Ideal Conditions Using the above, the photon ﬂux absorbed in the cross-sectional volume with inﬁnitesimal thickness located at depth x can be represented as shown in Eq. (7.4). Because this absorbed photon ﬂux depends on the depth x, we use the notation d(x). d(x) = 0  αexp(αx) (7.4) Here, 0 represents the incident photon ﬂux at a speciﬁc wavelength. In the present section, we employ the AM1.5 spectrum (curve (b) in Fig. 6.1) as the input power of the solar cell, which is the same condition as in Sect. 6.7. For each wavelength λ, the photon ﬂux 0 of the sunlight (that reaches the Earths ground surface) has a different value. Therefore, in the calculations below, we shall use the notation 0(λ) for the incident photon ﬂux. Similarly, in order to express that the absorption coefﬁcient α also depends on the wavelength, we use the notation α (λ). We can now precisely state that the absorbed photon ﬂux is a function of the wavelength λ and the depth x. d(x, λ) = 0(λ)  α(λ)  exp(α(λ)x) Finally, we determine the density of photoexcited electrons and holes that are generatedatdepthx byabsorptionofincidentlightwithwavelength λ.Ifwedeﬁnethe number of electronhole pairs that are excited by a single photon during absorption of light in the semiconductor as tc (see Sect. 4.1), the density of the photoexcited electronhole pairs, g(x, λ), can be written as shown in Eq. (7.5). g(x, λ) = tc  0(λ)  α(λ)  exp(α(λ)x) (7.5) The ratio tc is also called quantum efﬁciency. In case of tc = 1, each absorbed photon results in photoexcitation of one electronhole pair. In the following discus- sion, we employ the same conditions as in Sect. 4.1 and consider that tc is unity. 7.2 The Minority Carrier Diffusion In the previous section, we derived the depth proﬁle of the photogenerated elec- tronhole pair density, g(x, λ). Next, we need to understand how many of these electronhole pairs contribute to the electricity generation of the solar cell. Even if electrons are generated by photoexcitation, they may collide with a hole on their way to the place where they can be utilized, that is, the electrode. In other words, there is a possibility that the electronhole pairs disappear before they contribute to the elec- tricity generation. The present section elucidates the details about the electronhole pairs that have not been extinguished and thus can be effectively utilized. 7.2 The Minority Carrier Diffusion 145 To begin with, we shall investigate the structure that is required for the separation and transport of the photogenerated carriers. Below, we specify the solar cell structure that is employed in this section. The n-layer is at the side where the light enters the pn junction (front), and the p-layer is at the back as shown in Fig. 7.5. We assume that the n-layer is sufﬁciently thin to enable a signiﬁcant excitation of the p-layer volume by incident light. Regarding the thickness of the p-layer, we assume it being larger than that of the n-layer. This structure is commonly adopted in monocrystalline Si solar cells. The details are given in Sect. 7.3. We note that Fig. 7.5 omits the electrodes at the front side and back side, as well as other elements like the anti-reﬂection coating. Now let us analyze how the photogenerated electrons and holes move in this structure. As described by Eq. (7.5), the density of the photoexcited electronhole pairs varies with the depth because the density of the photoexcited electronhole pairs, g(x, λ), is a function of the depth x. Therefore, there is a density gradient in the neutral regions of both the p- and n-layers. When we consider electrons and holes as particles, we have to be aware that there is a force that tries to make the particle density homogeneous within the medium. This phenomenon is called diffusion, which is a random movement of particles in all the possible directions. Consequently, the electrons in the neutral region of the p-layer as well as the holes in the neutral region of the n-layer diffuse also toward the side of the depletion region. The p-layer contains many holes which are induced by the acceptors, and the n-layer contains many excess electrons which originate from the donors. Therefore, the photoexcited electrons in the p-layer as well as the photoexcited holes in the n-layer are called minority carriers. If these minority carriers reach the depletion region via diffusion as shown with arrow ➀in Fig. 1.11, they can contribute to the electricity generated by the solar cell. However, there is also the possibility that they are annihilated by recombination as shown with arrow ➁in Fig. 1.11. To consider the diffusion of the minority carriers including the abovementioned annihilation, we employ the continuity equation of the form provided in Eq. (7.6). Fig. 7.5 Structure of the semiconductor pn junction considered in this chapter 146 7 Application Limits for the Ideal Conditions Dh d2 pN dx2 pN pN0 τh = 0 (7.6) The continuity equation is frequently treated in textbooks concerning semicon- ductor engineering [3] and is also covered in detail in Chap. 8. Equation (7.6) is the equation for the case of photoexcited holes in the neutral region of the n-layer (i.e., the region without electric ﬁeld) in a steady-state condition. It discusses the photoex- cited hole density in terms of the difference between total hole density (pN) and the hole density in dark (pN0). The ﬁrst term on the left-hand side represents the increase of the total hole density pN as a result of the overall diffusion toward the cross section at depth x within a certain time. The second term describes the hole density reduction via recombination of photoexcited holes (the photoexcited hole density is pN pN0) in the cross-sectional volume with inﬁnitesimal thickness located at depth x within a certain time. In a situation with continuous light illumination, the solar cell is in a steady-state condition and generates a constant output power. Consequently, the hole current that passes the cross section at depth x within a certain time is constant, and the right-hand side of the equation, which describes the temporal change in the hole density at depth x, is zero. Dh is called the diffusion constant of the holes and describes the ease of diffusion, that is, a larger Dh implies a better transport. The subscript h means hole, and De is used to express the diffusion constant of the electron, which is used later. τh is the recombination life time of the photoexcited hole, which describes that after a certain hole population has been generated by photoexcitation, and the hole popu- lation is reduced to 36.8% (=1/e) of the initial population within the time τh due to electronhole recombination. Our target is to discuss the depth dependence of the photoexcited carriers including effects of diffusion and recombination, and therefore, we reformulate Eq. (7.6). Because pN is the hole density in the n-layer under light illumination and pN0 describes the hole density in the n-layer without light illumi- nation, the difference pN pN0 is the density of the photoexcited holes. Since the density of the photoexcited holes changes with the depth x, we express this density as a function of x. pN(x) = pN(x) pN0 (7.7) By substituting Eq. (7.7) into Eq. (7.6), we obtain Eq. (7.8). Dh d2pN(x) dx2 pN(x) τh = 0 (7.8) Equation (7.8) is a differential equation. Because pN0 is independent of x, the derivative of pN(x) with respect to x results in the same value as the derivative of pN(x) with respect to x. By slightly transforming Eq. (7.8), we can derive the solution shown in Eq. (7.9). 7.2 The Minority Carrier Diffusion 147 d2pN(x) dx2 = 1 Dhτh  pN(x) pN(x) = Aexp  x Dhτh  + Bexp   x Dhτh  (7.9) Let us evaluate the integration constants A and B, which are introduced during the integration. Consider the case of an inﬁnite depth x . The recombination life time τh has a ﬁnite value, and therefore, the photoexcited holes will recombine somewhere in the n-layer. Because the photoexcited hole cannot diffuse from the front surface (on the side of light incidence) to the inﬁnite far back surface, s can be evaluated as shown in Eq. (7.10). lim xpN(x) = lim xAexp  x Dhτh  = 0 A = 0 (7.10) Next, we consider the case for the depth approaching zero, i.e., the front surface. The density of photoexcited holes at the materials surface can be obtained from Eq. (7.5). lim x0 pN(x) = B = lim x0 g(x, λ) (7.11) In Eq. (7.11), we obtained B for the case of x approaching zero. In case of an arbitrary but ﬁnite depth x, the value of B becomes g(x, λ). Under continuous light illumination, the density of holes that is established via photoexcitation including effects of diffusion and recombination can be represented as a function of the depth x as shown in Eq. (7.12). pN(x) = g(x, λ)exp   x Dhτh  = g(x, λ)exp  x Lh  (7.12) Here, we replaced the square root of the product between the hole diffusion con- stant and the recombination life time, Dhτh, by Lh. Lh deﬁnes the distance that can be travelled by a hole via diffusion until it recombines and is called diffusion length. In Fig. 7.6, we show the different types of distances that are related to the hole diffusion in the n-layer. 148 7 Application Limits for the Ideal Conditions Fig. 7.6 Different types of distances that are related to the hole diffusion in the n-layer Using the surface of the n-layer (the front surface) as the base, we deﬁne x as the distance from the front surface (depth). The thickness of the n-layer is set to dN, and the width of the depletion region of the pn junction is W. For the sake of simplicity, we assume that the thickness W of the depletion region is sufﬁciently small compared to the thickness of the n-layer and the diffusion lengths of the pho- togenerated electrons and holes. Additionally, we neglect the carrier annihilation inside the depletion region, because the donors and acceptors in this region provide no electrons and holes. Therefore, collisions between photogenerated carriers and carriers of opposite charge hardly occur. Hence, we treat the width of the depletion region in this chapter as negligible (W = 0). To determine the density of the photoexcited holes in the n-layer that reach the pn junction (i.e., the region around the interface between n- and p-layer) by diffusion, we consider the electronhole density generated by light at point x, g(x, λ) and the transport from the point x to the center of the pn junction at dN, which results in the exponential factor derived above. pN(x) = g(x, λ)exp  dN x Lh  (7.13) By integrating the hole density shown in Eq. (7.13) from 0 to dN (corresponding to the total region of the n-layer), we can determine the hole ﬂux that ﬂows from the n-layer side toward the pn junction and passes the interface. If we multiply this ﬂux by the elementary charge e, we obtain the current density jh(λ) that is generated in the n-layer and ﬂows through the pn junction interface toward the p-layer. jh(λ) = e dN 0 pN(x) dx = e dN 0 g(x, λ)exp  dN x Lh  dx 7.2 The Minority Carrier Diffusion 149 = e 0α Lh αLh 1  exp  dN Lh  exp(αdN) (7.14) In the following, let us consider the diffusion of the minority carriers in the p-layer (that is, the electrons). In Fig. 7.7, we provide the different types of distances that are related to the electron diffusion in the p-layer. Le represents the diffusion length of the electron. We consider that the thickness of the p-layer, dP, is sufﬁciently large compared to the thickness of the n-layer, dN. The density of photoexcited electrons in the p-layer that do not recombine and reach the region of the pn junction by diffusion can be obtained by integrating the excess electron density over the whole p-layer region (from the depth dN of the pn junction to the depth dP + dN). Because we assumed that the p-layer is sufﬁciently thick compared to the n-layer, we can now use the approximation dP + dN = dP to simplify the calculation. In order to discuss the case of the p-layer, we rewrite Eq. (7.12), which is given in terms of the hole density, to obtain an equation for the electron density. Because the depth x considered here lies inside the p-layer, x is larger than the layer thickness dN for the n-layer. Hence, the distance from the location of the pn junction is given by x dN. nP(x) = g(x, λ)exp  x dN Le  (7.15) We can obtain the electron ﬂux that ﬂows from the p-layer side toward the pn junction interface and passes it by integrating Eq. (7.15) over the range from dN to dP. If we multiply this ﬂux by the electrical charge e, we obtain the current density je(λ) that is generated in the p-layer and ﬂows through the pn junction interface toward the n-layer. je(λ) = e dP dN nP(x) dx = e dP dN g(x, λ)exp  x dN Le  dx Fig. 7.7 Different types of distances that are related to the electron diffusion in the p-layer 150 7 Application Limits for the Ideal Conditions = e 0α Le αLe + 1  exp(αdN) exp  αdP dP dN Le  (7.16) je(λ) is positive for the direction along the electron ﬂow. To discuss the photocurrent density jsh(λ) that is generated in the solar cell per illuminated area, we have to combine Eqs. (7.14) and (7.16) by aligning the current directions, which leads to Eq. (7.17). jsh(λ) = jh(λ) je(λ) (7.17) Equation (7.17) describes the photocurrent density jsh(λ) that is generated by incident light at a certain wavelength λ. The above results provide the relationships between light absorption and the layer thicknesses of the n- and p-layer. Finally, we integrate jsh(λ) over the whole wavelength regime to obtain the photocurrent density Jsh generated by the total incident light. Jsh =   0 jsh(λ) dλ (7.18) In the next section, we investigate the details of the trade-off between light absorp- tion and the thickness of the n-layer. Let us see how the thickness of the n-layer or its absorption coefﬁcient affects the photocurrent density Jsh. 7.3 Photocurrent Densities Calculated for Different Materials Under Consideration of the Layer Thickness By employing the equations derived in Sect. 7.2, we now perform calculations for two concrete examples of solar cell materials, silicon (Si) and gallium arsenide (GaAs), and discuss how the absorption coefﬁcient and other factors inﬂuence solar cell applications. The room temperature band-gap energies of Si and GaAs are 1.12 and 1.43 eV, respectively, which correspond to photon wavelengths of 1110 and 867 nm according to Eq. (2.4). For the calculations of the single-junction solar cells in the previous chapters, we used the assumption that the semiconductor absorber layer of the solar cell completely absorbs the light with energy above the band-gap energy, while it cannot absorb any light with smaller energy. However, the degree of light absorption by an actual semiconductor depends on the wavelength as shown in Fig. 7.3. Below, we provide the calculation results that implement Eqs. (7.17) and (7.18) including the actual absorption coefﬁcients and diffusion lengths of each material. The calculations employ the AM1.5G spectrum for the incident light. These results clarify how the thickness dN of the n-layer affects the value of the short-circuit current. 7.3 Photocurrent Densities Calculated for Different Materials  151 (1) The Si single-junction solar cell. The diffusion length of the minority carrier is different for each semiconductor and is inﬂuenced by various factors such as the donor and acceptor doping concentration, the density of other impurities, or also the number of bonding defects in the semicon- ductor crystal. In the present case, we shall use Lh = 10 µm for the diffusion length of the holes in the n-layer and Le = 0.5 µm for the diffusion length of the electrons in the p-layer. These are common values for Si single crystals. The thickness of the p-layer is ﬁxed to dP = 300 µm. Figure 7.8 shows the photocurrent that is obtained for different thicknesses of the n-layer (dN) as a result of the photoexcitation of the electrons and holes in the solar cell under short-circuit condition. The horizontal axis is dN, and the vertical axis shows the photocurrent density Jsh which is obtained from Eq. (7.18). Jsh reaches its maximum for an n-layer thickness of about 4 µm. This means that the optimum n-layer thickness becomes 4 µm as a result of the trade-off relationship between the layer thickness required to absorb the light and the layer thickness that allows transport of electrons and holes via diffusion, which was brieﬂy mentioned in the beginning of this chapter. Let us analyze the effect of the layer thickness by focusing on the photocurrent densities that are generated in the n- and p-layer. In Fig. 7.9, we show the rela- tion between the incident photon ﬂux 0(λ) (gray data), the current density jh(λ) generated in the n-layer (red data), and the current density | je(λ)| generated in the p-layer (black data). Figure 7.9ac show in order the results for the n-layer thick- nesses 1.0, 4.0, and 9.0 µm. We ﬁnd that, as the n-layer becomes thicker, the peaks of the photocurrent densities generated in both the n- and p-layer shift toward the long-wavelength region. The penetration depth for short-wavelength light in Si is relatively small, e.g., on the order of 0.1 µm for the light at 400 nm. On the contrary, as the penetration depth for the light at 800 nm is on the order of 10 µm, the penetration depth of long-wavelength light is relatively large. In case of an n-layer thickness of 1.0 µm, short-wavelength light may be absorbed sufﬁciently in the n-layer, but the long- wavelength light is hardly absorbed. Therefore, the current density jh(λ) that is shown in Fig. 7.9a exhibits a peak at the short-wavelength side. In contrast, for Fig. 7.8 Relation between the thickness of the n-layer and the photocurrent density given in Eq. (7.18) for a Si single-junction solar cell 152 7 Application Limits for the Ideal Conditions (a) Result for an n-layer thickness of 1.0 µm. (b) Result for an n-layer thickness of 4.0 µm. (c) Result for an n-layer thickness of 9.0 µm. Fig. 7.9 Relation between the n-layer thickness and the photocurrent densities given in Eqs. (7.14) and (7.16) for a Si single-junction solar cell thick n-layers such as shown in Fig. 7.9c, the hole density that is generated in the n-layer increases signiﬁcantly because the long-wavelength light can be absorbed sufﬁciently. However, since the n-layer is thick, the holes that are excited near the surface of the n-layer by short-wavelength light have a higher possibility to collide and recombine with electrons during their diffusion toward the pn junction interface. Therefore, the holes generated by short-wavelength light hardly diffuse to the pn 7.3 Photocurrent Densities Calculated for Different Materials  153 junction in case of a thick n-layer, and the peak position of the hole current density jh(λ) shifts toward the long-wavelength region. In case of the Si solar cell that is considered here, the diffusion length of the holes inside the n-layer is twenty times that of the electrons in the p-layer. Therefore, the jh(λ) for the optimum n-layer thickness is larger than the corresponding | je(λ)|. (2) The GaAs single-junction solar cell. Here, we shall assume a diffusion length of Lh = 3.5 µm for the holes in the n- layer and a diffusion length of Le = 1.2 µm for the electrons in the p-layer. We vary the thickness dN for the n-layer and the thickness dP for the p-layer is ﬁxed to 300 µm, which is the same condition as used in the case for Si. The photocurrent that establishes due to the photoexcitation of the electrons and holes in the single-junction solar cell under short-circuit condition is shown in Fig. 7.10. Here, we use the same representation as in Fig. 7.8, namely the horizontal axis represents dN and the vertical axis represents the photocurrent density that can be obtained from Eq. (7.18), Jsh. The Jsh of GaAs is maximized for a dN around 0.3 µm. Compared to the optimum n-layer thickness of the Si solar cell (4 µm), this is about one tenth of the thickness. As explained in Figs. 7.2 and 7.3, GaAs is a material that absorbs visible light stronger than Si. Since GaAs can absorb the same amount of light by a thinner layer, a smaller optimum dN is obtained. As in the case for the Si solar cell, we analyze the effect of the layer thickness from the viewpoint of the photocurrent densities generated in the n- and p-layer. Figure 7.11 shows the relation between the incident photon ﬂux 0(λ), the current density jh(λ) generated in the n-layer, and the current density | je(λ)| generated in the p-layer. Figure 7.11ac show in order the results for the n-layer thicknesses 0.1, 0.3, and 0.9 µm. For a thicker n-layer, the peak wavelengths of the photocurrent densities generated in both n- and p-layer shift toward the long-wavelength region, which is the same tendency as observed for the Si solar cell. In the present GaAs solar cell, the diffusion length of the holes inside the n-layer is about three times longer than that of the electrons inside the p-layer. Therefore, jh(λ) at the optimum n-layer thickness is larger than the corresponding | je(λ)|, which is the same result as for the Si solar cell. However, compared to the difference between jh(λ) and | je(λ)| observed in the Si solar cell, the difference of jh(λ) | je(λ)| in the GaAs solar cell is smaller. Fig. 7.10 Relation between the n-layer thickness and the photocurrent density given in Eq. (7.18) for the GaAs single-junction solar cell 154 7 Application Limits for the Ideal Conditions (a) Result for an n-layer thickness of 0.1 µm. (b) Result for an n-layer thickness of 0.3 µm. (c) Result for an n-layer thickness of 0.9 µm. Fig. 7.11 Relation between the n-layer thickness and photocurrent densities given in Eqs. (7.14) and (7.16) for a GaAs single-junction solar cell From the above calculation results, we can understand the trade-off relationship betweenthesemiconductorthicknessrequiredtoabsorblightandthedistancethathas to be travelled by the generated electrons and holes via diffusion. The relationships between the largest photocurrent density Jsh, the corresponding n-layer thickness (which is the optimum n-layer thickness), the materials absorption coefﬁcient, and the diffusion length of the minority carrier are summarized in the following two statements: 7.3 Photocurrent Densities Calculated for Different Materials  155  As the absorption coefﬁcient of the material becomes larger, the optimum n-layer becomes thinner.  The relative intensities of the photocurrents | je| and jh that constitute the Jsh at the optimum layer thickness depend on the diffusion lengths, and the photocurrent carried by the carrier with the longer diffusion length has a larger contribution. Let us compare the photocurrent considered in the previous chapters with the photocurrent that accounts for the trade-off between absorption and diffusion length, which was introduced in this chapter. Figure 7.12a is the comparison for Si, and Fig. 7.12b is that for GaAs. For both Si and GaAs, the photocurrent density given by Eq. (7.17) is smaller than the photocurrent density employed up to Chap. 6. In the present chapter, we introduced the semiconductors light absorption coefﬁcient as one example of the factors that can lower the performance of solar cells. This coefﬁcient differs for every material and is an important factor besides the transmission and thermalization losses, which were introduced in Chap. 3. Besides these factors, there exist various origins that lead to losses in solar cells such as the reﬂection loss mentioned in Chap. 3, the limited minority carrier diffusion length explained in Sect. 7.2, or also the recombination losses that occur at the semiconductor surface and interface due Fig. 7.12 Comparison between the photocurrent density employed up to Chap. 6 and the photocurrent density deﬁned by Eq. (7.17) for different absorber materials (Si and GaAs). a Comparison between the photon ﬂux absorbed by thick Si (gray data) and the current extracted via the diffusion process in Si (black data). b Comparison between the photon ﬂux absorbed by thick GaAs (gray data) and the current extracted via the diffusion process in GaAs (black data) 156 7 Application Limits for the Ideal Conditions to a discontinuity in the crystal. However, the restrictions imposed by the trade-off mentionedinthischapter,i.e.,thatbetweenthelayerthicknessrequiredforabsorption and the diffusion-limited transport, can be relaxed by fabricating materials with larger diffusion lengths. By solving such issues step by step, the conversion efﬁciencies of the actual solar cells can approach their theoretical conversion efﬁciencies. This chapter is not intended to deny the theoretical efﬁciency limit. With this chapter, we wanted to show the reader that a detailed analysis of the different types of losses in solar cells provides strategies to compensate them and reach higher conversion efﬁciencies. We pray that the development of highly efﬁcient solar cells serves humanity and changes our future for the better. References 1. McGraw-Hill, New York Chapter 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors Abstract The solar cells that are discussed in this book are based on semiconduc- tors. The degree of difﬁculty in the explanation of the properties of semiconductors greatly depends on the starting point of the explanations. In this book, we start with the fact that a semiconductor is formed by a valence band which is completely ﬁlled with electrons and an empty conduction band, both bands being separated by a characteristic energy gap. First, we shall investigate the behavior of the bands in the representative semiconductor silicon (Si) to clarify the band-gap concept. Then, we provide explanations on the intrinsic semiconductors which contain no impu- rities, and the extrinsic semiconductors wherein impurity atoms with a different number of valence electrons are added. Within the extrinsic semiconductors, we distinguish between the n-type semiconductor with excess electrons that are deliv- ered to the conduction band and the p-type semiconductor with the so-called holes (i.e., missing electrons) that are delivered to the valence band. Regarding these two classes of semiconductors with different electronic conditions, we clarify the details about the density of electrons and holes, their distributions, and the so-called Fermi level (which is the energy level corresponding to an electron occupation probability of 50%). We also consider the transport of electrons and holes inside the semicon- ductor. Finally, we derive the characteristics of the pn junction, which connects an n-type semiconductor with a p-type semiconductor and is commonly used for solar cell devices. The present chapter provides detailed explanations from the viewpoint of the semiconductor electronics, which makes it accessible with the knowledge equivalent to that of a third-year undergraduate student. With respect to the structure of the present book, not all details mentioned in this chapter are required for the understanding of the other chapters. In other words, the calculation of the energy conversion efﬁciency of a solar cell, which is the target of the present book, is based on a relatively independent approach. The reader may omit this chapter, or regard it as a guide to ﬁnd concrete ideas for improving the conversion efﬁciencies of semi- conductor solar cells. In order to enable an independent study of this chapter on semiconductors, we again deﬁne all variables of the previous chapters.
