Kita et al., Energy Conversion Efﬁciency of Solar Cells, Green Energy and Technology, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-13-9089-0_8 157 158 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors 8.1 The Semiconductor Band Gap In the above, we brieﬂy mentioned that a semiconductor is formed by a valence band which is completely ﬁlled with electrons and an empty conduction band, and both bands are separated by a characteristic energy gap. However, this is a rather ambiguous deﬁnition. The most important point in this chapter is the degree of the ﬁlling of the bands with electrons, and this will be speciﬁed in the following. First, we shall conﬁrm the outline of the semiconductor band structure. The electron orbitals around the atom core are arranged in shells which are, in order from low to high energies, the K shell, the L shell, the M shell, and further shells. In terms of the main quantum number n as deﬁned by quantum mechanics, these shells correspond to 1, 2, 3, and so on. Actually, each shell contains a different number of electron orbitals, which are deﬁned by the azimuth quantum number. The number of electrons that ﬁt in a certain shell is deﬁned by quantum numbers. For larger shells with higher energy, the number of electrons that ﬁt into them increases, i.e., 2 for the K shell, 8 for the L shell, and 18 for the M shell. The number of electrons that can be accommodated in a certain shell is determined by the following set of rules. Firstly, the azimuth quantum number l is an integer number and starts from 0. These numbers represent states which are named, in order, s orbital, p orbital, d orbital, f orbital, and so on. Secondly, the azimuth quantum number l assumes values between 0 and n 1. Finally, each orbital (which is deﬁned by l) can contain up to 2(2l + 1) electrons. Silicon (Si) is the most frequently used semiconductor. The atomic number of Si is 14, and thus, it has 14 electrons. The K shell is ﬁlled with 2 electrons, and the L shell is ﬁlled with 8 electrons, which means that they are completely ﬁlled. The M shell can contain 18 electrons, but there are only 4 electrons left for distribution in the orbitals of the M shell since the other occupy the K and L shells. These 4 electrons that ﬁt in the most outer shell are called valence electrons and have very important functions with respect to the semiconductor properties. When we investigate the electrons in the M shell of Si in more detail, we ﬁnd that 2 electrons are in the s orbital and 2 in the p orbital. This is for the case of a single Si atom. However, if the Si atoms come close to each other during the crystallization, their interaction becomes possible and the s and p orbitals mix. This sp hybridization results in four equivalent orbitals (hybrid orbitals). Due to the Pauli exclusion principle, these 4 electrons are distributed equivalently in the hybridized orbitals. The orbitals of the electrons equally extend along the four directions toward the vertices of a regular tetrahedron with the atom core being in the center of the tetrahedron. This hybrid orbital is stable if completely ﬁlled by a total of 8 electrons (2 electrons from the s orbital and 6 electrons from the p orbital). In other words, 2 electrons are in each of the four hybrid orbitals elongated along the tetrahedral directions. The 4 electrons are also responsible for the physics that connect the single Si atoms. The regular tetrahedral structure as shown in Fig. 8.1 represents the most balanced placement of the electrons at the outmost part of the Si atoms. This structure is the same as that of 8.1 The Semiconductor Band Gap 159 Fig. 8.1 Crystal structure of Si (left panel) and the shared electrons (right panel). The right panel depicts the tetrahedral bonds in a planar scheme diamond and thus also called diamond structure. Diamond is made up of carbon (C) which belongs to the same group IV elements as Si. The Si atoms form bonds by sharing one of the four valance electrons with each of thefournearestneighbors.Thebondsthatareformedinsuchawayarecalledcovalent bonds. The charge of the inner core without the 4 electrons used for the bonds is +4e (e = 1.61019 C). Therefore, the negative charge of the electrons located between the atoms attracts the neighboring atoms via Coulomb interaction. The reader may wonder why the covalent electrons are located between the atoms. The Coulomb potential (energy that depends on the relative location) reaches its maximum in the area between the atoms, and thus, the kinetic energy of the electrons is minimized in this area. Because the total energy of the electrons is constant, the probability for being located in that area is largest. This is a rather rough model, but sufﬁcient for the present purpose. 160 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors Fig. 8.2 Coulomb potential and electronic band structure formed by the connected Si atoms The Coulomb potential between the atoms can be imagined as shown in Fig. 8.2. It shows six Si atoms that are aligned in a row and have formed bonds. The source of the Coulomb potential between the atoms is of course the Coulomb force F, i.e., the force that induces the attraction between positive and negative charges. The Coulomb force is inversely proportional to the squared of the distance r between the charges, i.e., F 1/r2. On the other hand, since the Coulomb force is the negative of the ﬁrst derivative of the Coulomb potential EP, we have F = EP and can assume the vacuum level EVAC for r , resulting in EP EVAC 1/r. By adding these six potentials for the six different atom locations, we obtain the black solid curve in Fig. 8.2 and at the crystal surfaces at both sides the EP changes according to EVAC 1/r. The bands formed by the electrons in the core with main quantum numbers n = 1, 2 are localized at the Si atoms, but the 4 electrons that accompany each Si core in the outmost shell with n = 3 can escape the conﬁnement by the Coulomb potential. Thus, they are not localized at a single Si atom, but extend to the neighboring atoms and contribute to the bonds via the interaction explained above. The band gap opens due to the interaction between the electron orbitals and forms a valence band that is completely ﬁlled with electrons and a conduction band without electrons. Here, we will not further elaborate these interactions. The energy difference that represents the band gap, Eg, depends on the semicon- ductor material. In Table 8.1, we compare the Eg of semiconductors that are com- monly employed in solar cells. Since the band-gap energy Eg is extremely small, it is expressed in units of electronvolt (eV), which is equivalent to the energy of 1.6  1019 J obtained by accelerating one electron using 1 V. Regarding the relation between the wavelength of light and its energy, the wavelength range of visible light (400800 nm) corresponds to an energy range of about 3.11.6 eV as explained in Sect. 2.1. If we include the near-infrared light with even smaller energies, this energy range of light almost covers the band gaps of the main semiconductors. We might say that this fortunate constellation has enabled the realization of various optical devices based on semiconductors such as the solar cells. 8.1 The Semiconductor Band Gap 161 Table 8.1 Band-gap energies of several important semiconductors Semiconductor Eg Ge 0.67 eV Si 1.12 eV InP 1.35 eV GaAs 1.43 eV GaN 3.39 eV Fig. 8.3 Absorption of light that enters Si (band-gap energy 1.12 eV) We shall now consider the situation of light falling on a semiconductor with such a band gap. In Fig. 8.3, we show the process of light absorption in a semiconductor for both illumination by light with energy above Eg (short-wavelength light) and illumination by light with energy below Eg (long-wavelength light). Here, EC is the energy of the lower edge of the conduction band, and EV is the upper edge of the valence band. The band-gap energy Eg is equal to the difference EC EV. If external light with sufﬁciently large energy is incident on the semiconductor, an electron in the valence band will be excited and is elevated into the conduction band. To be more precise, within the semiconductors there are the so-called direct band-to- band transition semiconductors and the indirect band-to-band transition semiconduc- tors. If the band structure is drawn in the wavevector space, the former type exhibits transitions between conduction band and valence band along the same wavevector 162 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors and latter not. For example, InP, GaAs, and GaN are direct band-to-band transition semiconductors, but Si and also Ge are indirect band-to-band transition semiconduc- tors. Because the wavenumber of the initial and ﬁnal states in optical transitions of indirect band-gap semiconductors is different, these optical transitions require the help of phonons. Therefore, the intensities of their optical transitions such as absorp- tion are weaker than those of the direct transition types, and the consequences for the solar cell were explained in Chap. 7. Additionally, we note that the symmetry of the electron orbitals in the initial and ﬁnal states has to be different in order to complete the optical transition between the bands. This is a very important factor in the design of advanced solar cells that employ semiconductor quantum structures. Incontrast,iftheenergyoftheincidentlightissmallerthanthatofthesemiconduc- tor band gap, the light is not absorbed by the semiconductor and will be transmitted without being used. The color of the Sun as seen through a semiconductor with a large band gap is the color of the light that passed the semiconductor without being absorbed. In Fig. 8.3, we consider the illumination of Si (Eg = 1.12 eV) using light with different energies (different wavelengths). Incident light with an energy of 1 eV (i.e., a wavelength of 1.24 μm) is not absorbed and simply passes the semiconductor because the energy of the incident light is smaller than Eg. This is called transmis- sion. On the other hand, upon illumination with light at 1.2 eV (i.e., a wavelength of 1.03 μm), electrons are generated in the conduction band by exciting electrons from the valence band. These electrons are responsible for electrical conduction in the semiconductor. The transmission of the sunlight with energy smaller than the band-gap energy of the semiconductor is equivalent to a loss for the solar cell. 8.2 The Intrinsic Semiconductor A semiconductor that contains neither impurity atoms with a number of valence electrons different from that of the host crystal nor such crystal defects is called intrinsic semiconductor. Therefore, the intrinsic semiconductor crystal itself ini- tially contains no free electrons and also no missing electrons (i.e., holes) in excess. An electron in the conduction band can be generated via excitation from the valence band using light or heat. The hole in the valence band is the missing electron induced by this excitation process. Hence, the number of electrons in the conduction band is always equal to the number of holes in the valence band. Under the condition of absent light illumination (dark condition) and simultaneous absolute zero tem- perature (0 K1), any possible excitation is prevented. In this situation, there are no electrons in the conduction band, and the valence band is completely ﬁlled with elec- trons. If we deﬁne n0 as the electron density in the conduction band under thermal equilibrium and use p0 as the corresponding hole density in the valence band, we can express the abovementioned condition of equality as 1The unit of the absolute temperature is pronounced Kelvin. 273 K corresponds to 0 C. 8.2 The Intrinsic Semiconductor 163 n0 = p0 = ni (8.1) Here, ni is intrinsic carrier density, which is equal to both the electron density and the hole density in the intrinsic semiconductor under thermal equilibrium. The carrier density at a certain ﬁnite temperature is discussed in detail in the next section. Figure 8.4 illustrates the excitation of an electron in real space (upper panel) and the corresponding situation of the excitation from the valence band to the conduction band in the band diagram (lower panel). If, for example, light with energy above the band gap enters the semiconductor, a part of the electrons that completely ﬁll the valence band is excited to the conduction band. The excited electrons can move freely inside the semiconductor crystal. However, since there is an electron deﬁcit in the valence band (i.e., holes exist in the valence band), an electron that is freely moving around may once again encounter a hole with a certain probability. If they meet, the electron ﬁlls the hole. This process is called recombination. When we trace this process in the band structure (the reverse direction of the blue arrow in Fig. 8.4), we ﬁnd that the electron in the conduction band loses energy as it approaches the hole which energetically lies below. This is called energy relaxation. In this energy relaxation process, the excess energy with amount equal to the band-gap energy is removed from the electronhole pair by the emission of light or heat. The process that emits light is called radiative recombination (or also luminescent recombination), and the process that emits heat is called non-radiative recombination. The number of electrons that are generated in the conduction band via excitation is proportional to the number of quantized states of the incident light (photons). In the ideal case where each single photon generates one electron and one hole, the so-called quantum efﬁciency equals 100%. However, under real-world conditions, 100% is not reached. Let us consider how many photons are actually contained in a certain light beam. For example, the sunlight intensity reaching the Earths ground surface is about 100 mW per cm2. The unit of the light intensity is Watt (W), which is equivalent to the energy transported by the light per second. The unit of the energy is Joule (J). In other words, illumination by light with a power density of 100 mW per cm2 meansthatanenergyof100mJenterstheareaof1cm2 everysecond.Becausethe sunlight contains many different wavelengths as a result of the black-body radiation, an accurate calculation of the number of photons requires a consideration of the solar spectrum. Since our aim is to get an idea of the photons, here we consider that the light with a power of 100 mJ/(s cm2) only consists of monochromatic green light at about 500 nm. If we use 2.5 eV for the photon energy, the number of photons contained in the light with a power of 100 mW that enters the area of 1 cm2 within a second is 100  103 J 2.5 eV   1.6  1019 J/eV  = 2.5  1017 photons (8.2) In other words, illumination with such a light generates a similar high number of electrons and holes in the semiconductor. 164 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors Fig. 8.4 Excitation of the electron from the valence band to the conduction band represented in the band diagram (lower panel) and visualization of the corresponding excitation of the electron in real space (upper panel) 8.3 The Extrinsic Semiconductor Semiconductors that contain a small number of atoms (impurities) with a different number of valence electrons are called extrinsic semiconductors. They are some- times also termed impurity semiconductors. Impurity atoms that provide electrons in excess are called donors, and semiconductors containing these are called n-type semiconductors (see Fig. 8.5a). On the other hand, impurity atoms that induce elec- tron deﬁcits (which is equivalent with providing holes in excess) are called accep- tors, and semiconductors containing these are called p-type semiconductors (see 8.3 The Extrinsic Semiconductor 165 Fig. 8.5 a The n-type semiconductor and b the p-type semiconductor Fig. 8.5b). In an extrinsic semiconductor, we have n0 = p0. In particular, for an n-type semiconductor n0  p0 holds, and for a p-type semiconductor n0  p0. Consider, for example, Si which is a semiconductor belonging to group IV. If we add a group-V element like phosphorus (P), each P atom contributes one excess electron because it has ﬁve valence electrons in the outmost shell. Hence we obtain n0  p0. On the other hand, if we add a group-III element like boron (B), which has only 3 valence electrons in the outmost shell, each B atom results in one missing valence electron. Since this is equivalent to contribution of one hole, n0 becomes smaller than p0. These two examples are shown in Fig. 8.5. In case of the n-type semiconductor Si including P, one electron out of the ﬁve valence electrons of P cannot be delivered to any of the bonds, and thus, it is left over. If the temperature is sufﬁciently low, this excess electron is attracted by the Coulomb force of the P+ ion (the P atom with a missing electron). However, if the 166 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors heat energy exceeds the Coulomb potential of the P+ at a certain elevated tempera- ture, the attracting force is not sufﬁcient anymore to trap the electron at the P+ ion and the electron can diffuse freely. This situation is explained later in more detail. The difference with respect to the intrinsic semiconductor is that the impurity semi- conductor requires no hole to generate free electrons, and thus, there is no hole that might induce a recombination. In contrast, for the case of the p-type semiconductor Si including B, the impurity atomhasonlythreevalenceelectrons.Therefore,oneofthefourbondsofthediamond structure lacks an electron, which constitutes the hole. Similar to the case of the n- type semiconductor, if the temperature is low enough the hole is attracted by the B ion. However, also here the hole will move away from the Bion if the temperature sufﬁciently increases. The subtle difference to the n-type semiconductor is that a new hole forms in a neighboring bond by ﬁlling the location of the present hole with the neighbors valence electron. The continuous repetition of this process represents the transport of the hole in the valence band in real space. 8.4 Energy Levels of Impurities and Carrier Generation At very low temperatures, the donor of the n-type semiconductor is accompanied by an excess electron due to the Coulomb attraction. In this case, the donor is electrically neutral. However, the donor ionizes if the temperature sufﬁciently increases because the electron gains thermal energy and moves freely away from the location of the donor. The acceptor in the p-type semiconductor behaves similar. In the following, we shall investigate the temperatures at which the donors and acceptors ionize and the carriers can start to move around freely. The ionization is determined by the relation between the magnitudes of the thermal energy and the energetic depth of the impurity energy level. First, consider the n-type semiconductor. In terms of ionization, the donor can be interpreted as a monovalent cation together with an electron and thus behaves like the hydrogen atom with atomic number 1. To proceed, we analyze the interaction between the electron with electric charge e and the donor ion with electric charge +e via the Coulomb potential. The energy levels of the electron that are obtained using Eq. (8.10) for the addition of a P+ ion into a Si crystal are shown in Fig. 8.7. However, this value is larger than the experimentally obtained value of 0.045 eV. As shown in the above, the hydrogen model is sufﬁciently accurate for the qualitative understanding of the formation of the energy levels of the donors. The ground state energy level ED of the donor is located at shallow energies below the conduction band minimum, which enables thermal activation (excitation) of electrons even at temperatures on the order of 300 K (27 C, room temperature). The temperature dependence of the carrier distribution inside the band is discussed in detail in the next section. After excitation from the donor ground state to the conduction band, the electrons are not conﬁned anymore and can move freely in the conduction band as shown in Fig. 8.8. These thermally activated electrons contribute to the electrical conductivity. Fig. 8.8 Generation of free electrons (which are not conﬁned to donors) via thermal excitation. The energy levels are given by the following equation: En = EV + m ee4 32π2ε2  1 n2ℏ2 (8.11) The energy levels that are formed by the acceptor within the band gap are shown in Fig. 8.9. Similar to the case of the donors, the ground state energy level EA of the acceptor is slightly above the valence band maximum, and thus can even be excited at room temperature. After thermal activation from the acceptors, the holes are not conﬁned anymore and can move freely in the valence band as shown in Fig. 8.9. These holes contribute to the electrical conductivity of the semiconductor. Here, we note that the current is carried by the negatively charged electrons. Each single electron carries a charge of 1.6  1019 C. Since the current is the charge transported per unit time, the number of moving electrons corresponds to the current intensity. If we now deﬁne J as the current per unit area, the current density can be expressed with the electron density n and the average electron velocity veas shown in Eq. (8.12). J = enve (8.12) Further, vecan be expressed with the velocity of each electron vei and the volume V, ve= 1 V  i vei 8.4 Energy Levels of Impurities and Carrier Generation 171 In other words, if a band is completely ﬁlled with electrons, each electron with vei has a corresponding electron with vei,4 and thus, ve= 0. Consequently, J = 0 and a current cannot be transported in this band. Additionally, there are no electrons in the conduction band of an intrinsic semiconductor at low temperatures in a dark environment, and thus, the conduction band also transports no current. However, in case of an n-type semiconductor, current ﬂows at ﬁnite temperatures because free electrons exist in the conduction band after excitation from the donor levels. Now, we shall consider the situation in a p-type semiconductor. At ﬁnite temper- atures, electrons are excited from the valence band to the acceptor levels. This leaves a missing electron behind in the valence band, which is the hole. Since there has to be an electron in the valence band with exactly the opposite velocity as that of the missing electron, a current ﬂows due to this electron. The velocity of the electron that has lost its pairing partner due to the hole generation is represented as vuei. Thus, the current J in the valence band becomes J = e V  i vuei (8.13) If we deﬁne vhi as the velocity of the hole, we can write  i vuei =  i (vei vhi) and obtain J = e V  i vuei = e V  i (vei vhi) = e V  i vei   i vhi  = e V  i vhi = epvh (8.14) Here, p is the hole density and vh is the average velocity of the holes. In other words, the current in a p-type semiconductor is carried by the charge +e of the hole. 4To prove this, we have to provide details on the relation between the wavenumber K and the energy E. The energy of a particle propagates with the velocity of the center of mass, which is called group velocity and deﬁned as 1 ℏ dE dK . Because the energy dispersion E(K) is symmetric with respect to K, each vei has a corresponding vei. To avoid any misunderstandings, this textbook omits statements regarding the energy dispersion in the momentum space. 172 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors 8.5 The Carrier Distribution Within a Band So far, we treated the whole conduction band (and the whole valence band) as a single entity. We did not mention anything about a possible change in the density of states, which can be energetically occupied by the electrons. However, the density of states actually depends on the energy. If we assume that electrons inside the semiconductor can move like they can propagate in vacuum, the density of states for electrons in the conduction band is given by the following equation: De(E) = 1 2π2 2m e ℏ2 3/2 E EC (8.15) The derivation of this equation for the density of states is not a difﬁcult task, but requires the energy dispersion curve of the free electron in terms of the momentum space, which is omitted in this book. The density of states for the electron is zero at EC (the energy at the bottom of the conduction band) and increases for higher energies in proportion to the square root of the energy increase relative to EC. Similarly, the density of states for the holes in the valence band is given by the following equation: Dh(E) = 1 2π2 2m h ℏ2 3/2 EV E (8.16) These energy dependences of the density of states for both carrier types are shown in Fig. 8.10. The band gap is the energy region between the upper edge of the valence band and the lower edge of the conduction band. The density of states of the electrons increases as their energy increases and separates from the lower edge of the conduction band, while the density of states of the holes increases as their energy separates from the upper edge of the conduction band toward the inside of the valence band. The carrier density can be obtained through multiplying the density of states by the carrier distribution function. The electron distribution function is given by the following equation according to the FermiDirac statistics: fe(E) = 1 1 + exp  EEf kT  (8.17) Here, Ef is the Fermi level, T is the absolute temperature, and k is the so-called the Boltzmann constant (1.38  1023 J/K). In Fig. 8.11, we show the fe(E) calculated using Eq. (8.17) for different temperatures. As proven later, the Fermi level in an intrinsic semiconductor is located almost at the center of the band gap. Therefore, Fig. 8.11 depicts the case of an intrinsic semiconductor. At 0 K, the occupation probability of energies below the Fermi level is unity for the electron and no electrons exist above the Fermi level. The Fermi level corresponds to that 8.5 The Carrier Distribution Within a Band 173 Fig. 8.10 Density of states for the electrons in the conduction band and the density of states for the holes in the valence band energy where the occupation probability becomes exactly 1/2. An important feature is that the shape of the distribution function near the Fermi level becomes smoother as the temperature increases. In terms of the present ﬁgure, temperatures as high as T 2 or T 3 are sufﬁcient for a ﬁnite occupation of the conduction band states with electrons. This nonzero distribution in the conduction band represents the thermal excitation of electrons. We note that the thermal energy is given by kT, which is about 26 meV at room temperature (300 K). On the other hand, for large energies above EC, the denominator E Ef in Eq. (8.17) is on the order of 1 eV. Therefore, the approximation E Ef kT is valid. Consequently, the electron distribution function inside the conduction band can be approximated as shown in Eq. (8.18). fe(E) exp  E Ef kT  (8.18) This is the so-called Boltzmann distribution (Boltzmann approximation). Because the probability for a state being not occupied by an electron equals the probability for occupation with a hole, the distribution function of the hole can be expressed as 174 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors Fig. 8.11 Electron distribution function at different temperatures fh(E) = 1 fe(E) = 1  1 1 + exp  EEf kT  = 1 1 + exp  EfE kT  (8.19) Similar to the case for the electrons, the valence band is occupied by a ﬁnite number of holes at elevated temperatures. Because the denominator of Eq. (8.19), Ef E, is in the order of eV for energies below EV (the top of the valence band) that are sufﬁciently separated from the Fermi level, the approximation Ef E kT is valid. Their product is given by Eq. (8.9) tend to overlap. If the orbitals overlap, the discretized impurity levels form a band that has a signiﬁcant energy width. Any extrinsic semiconductor in such a condition is called degenerated semiconductor. In such a degenerated semiconductor, the Fermi level enters the band because the impurity band and the band of the (intrinsic) semiconductor overlap, resulting in a smaller effective band gap. This book does not cover degenerated semiconductors with such impu- rity levels. We only consider the non-degenerated semiconductors whose Fermi levels lie in the band gap. 8.6 The Fermi Level 177 This means that the product of the electron and hole densities in the semiconductor is determined by the effective masses (via the effective density of states), the band gap of the material, and the temperature only. Equation (8.28) is a law that restricts the relative densities of electrons and holes and is called mass action law. Let us determine, for example, the intrinsic carrier density ni for Si at room temperature. The band gap at room temperature is 1.124 eV, the effective mass of the electron is m e = 1.09m0, and the effective mass of the hole is m h = 0.54m0. By inserting these values into Eq. (8.28), we obtain ni = NCNV exp  Eg 2kT  =  2.89  1019 1.01  1019 exp   1.124 2  0.02586  = 6.23  109 cm3 (8.29) Similarly, we ﬁnd ni = 2.0  106 cm3 for GaAs, and ni = 2.7  1013 cm3 for Ge. Next, let us determine the energy Ei of the Fermi level in an intrinsic semicon- ductor. By dividing Eq. (8.23) by Eq. (8.24) and using n0 = p0, we can derive Eq. (8.30). NC NV = exp  EC Ei kT  exp  Ei EV kT  = exp  EC + EV 2Ei kT  (8.30) The Ei determined from this equation is given below. Ei = EC + EV 2 + kT 2 ln NV NC = EC + EV 2 + 3 4kT ln m h me  (8.31) The ﬁrst term on the right-hand side of Eq. (8.31), (EC + EV)/2, directly evi- dences that Ei is located near the center of the band gap. However, not exactly at the center as it is offset by the amount of the second term. For example, in case of Si, the second term is 1.05 meV, which is extremely small compared to the band gap. Consequently, it is usually not problematic to consider that the Fermi level of an intrinsic semiconductor is located at the center of the band gap. In an extrinsic semiconductor, which contains additional impurities, the position of the Fermi level is signiﬁcantly different from the band-gap center. Therefore, the thermal activation of the carriers from the impurities to the bands should become easier. Let us derive the equation that describes this behavior. First, we express the effective density of states in Eqs. (8.23) and (8.24) in terms of the intrinsic carrier density ni. NC = ni exp  EC Ei kT  (8.32) NV = ni exp  Ei EV kT  (8.33) 178 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors By substituting these equations into Eqs. This implies n0  ni, and therefore, we have E f  Ei; that is, the Fermi level shifts from the band-gap center toward the conduction band. The electron density increases as the Fermi level approaches the conduction band minimum. In contrast, the hole density obeys Eq. (8.37), and thus, the above shift of the Fermi level is accompanied by an exponential decrease in the hole density. As an example, we shall determine the Fermi level and the electron and hole densities at room temperature for Si with an acceptor doping concentration of 1016 cm3. If we assume that all added acceptors are activated and the holes are ejected to the valence band (which is possible at 300 K), the hole density becomes exactly the acceptor density, i.e., p0 = 1.0  1016 cm3. Using n0 = n2 i /p0, we obtain n0 = 3.9  104 cm3. From Eq. (8.24), we obtain Ef EV = kT ln(p0/NV ) = 0.18 eV, and therefore, the Fermi level is located about 0.18 eV above the valence band maximum. 8.7 Temperature Dependence of the Carrier Density In the discussions above, we considered that under thermal equilibrium, all donors and acceptors are ionized and the number of supplied carriers equals the number of added impurities. However, as discussed in Sect. 8.4, the Coulomb force between the carrier and the impurity becomes dominant if the temperature is sufﬁciently low, and then, the impurity behaves neutral. On the other hand, at extremely high temperatures, 8.7 Temperature Dependence of the Carrier Density 179 the excitation exceeds by far that from the impurity levels and direct excitation from the valence band to the conduction band occurs. In the present section, we investigate how the carrier density changes in such conditions with different semiconductor temperatures. In particular, we thoroughly discuss the transition to the intrinsic semiconductor as the temperature rises above that temperature where all impurities ionize. The total charge that arises from the negative and positive charges in a semicon- ductor containing ionized donors and acceptors has to be zero (i.e., the semiconductor itself is neutral). If we assume that all impurities have been ionized, the density of the added impurities and the density of the ionized impurities are equal, and the following condition regarding the electrical neutrality is fulﬁlled. p0 + ND = n0 + NA (8.38) Here, n0 is the electron density, p0 is the hole density, ND is the density of the positively ionized donors, and NA is the density of the negatively ionized acceptors. On the left-hand side of Eq. (8.38), we have the positive charges, and on the right-hand side, we have the negative charges. Using p0 = n2 i /n0, we obtain n2 i n0 n0 + ND NA = 0 n2 0 n0(ND NA) n2 i = 0 (8.39) This is a quadratic equation for n0, and the solution is as follows: n0 = ND NA 2 +  ND NA 2 2 + n2 i (8.40) In case of an n-type semiconductor, we have ND NA, and thus, Eq. (8.40) can be approximated as Eq. (8.41). n0 = ND 2 +  ND 2 2 + n2 i (8.41) The calculation result for the speciﬁc case of n-type Si is shown in Fig. 8.13. For relatively low temperatures around room temperature (300 K), there is no temperature dependence since all electrons that cannot be used for the bonds have already been removed from the donors and the free electron density equals the donor density. For higher temperatures, the behavior of the intrinsic semiconductor can be observed. Here, the electron density starts to increase because electrons are directly excited from the valence band to the conduction band. The broken curve represents the temperature dependence of the electron density ni in the intrinsic semiconductor. 180 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors Fig. 8.13 Temperature dependence of the electron density in the conduction band of n-type Si for different donor concentrations. As the temperature increases, the temperature dependence starts to resemble that of an intrinsic semiconductor Fig. 8.14 Temperature dependence of the Fermi level in n-type Si. As the temperature increases, the Fermi level separates more and more from the conduction band edge and approaches the Fermi level of the intrinsic semiconductor, Ei, near the center of the band gap Upon the modiﬁcation of the electron density that occurs for increasing tempera- tures, the Fermi level obviously changes as well. Using Eq. (8.23), we can calculate the position of the Fermi level relative to the lower edge of the conduction band according to Eq. (8.42). Ef EC = kT ln  n0 NC  (8.42) The calculated temperature dependence of the Fermi level in n-type Si is shown in Fig. 8.14. For increasing temperatures, the Fermi level separates more and more from the conduction band minimum and asymptotically approaches the Fermi level of the intrinsic semiconductor, Ei, which lies almost at the center of the band gap. To conclude this section, we provide an explanation on the carrier densities at extreme low temperatures. Here, the attracting Coulomb potential from the ionized 8.7 Temperature Dependence of the Carrier Density 181 impurity prevails over the thermal energy, and thus, the impurity stays neutral with- out ejecting the electron. This temperature regime is the regime with incomplete ionization where the carriers freeze out. In this temperature regime, Eq. (8.41) is not valid because it was derived using the assumption that all impurities are ion- ized. Although the carrier density in this temperature regime can also be analyzed in detail, we omit a detailed derivation because the statistics of the electrons conﬁned to the impurities would require considerations that are rather complex. Therefore, we restrict the discussion to qualitative statements. In case of Si, complete ionization of impurities such as phosphorous cannot occur for temperatures below 200 K. Such low temperatures belong to a temperature regime that needs not be considered for devices such as common solar cells. As the temper- ature decreases, the number of impurities that cannot maintain the ionized condition increases more and more, and the carrier density signiﬁcantly drops below the den- sity of the impurities added to the semiconductor. Here, the Fermi level is located in the region between EC and ED instead of being located close to either EC or ED. For reference, below we provide the theoretical results for the temperature dependence of the electron density and the Fermi level in an n-type semiconductor in the freeze-out range with incomplete ionization. n =  NCND 2 exp  EC ED 2kT  (8.43) Ef = EC + ED 2 + kT 2 ln  ND 2NC  (8.44) 8.8 The Currents in a Semiconductor: Drift Current and Diffusion Current In the above sections, we clariﬁed that electron and hole densities become larger if impurities are added to the semiconductor and the temperature is increased. The movementofthesecarriersthroughthesemiconductorvolumeconstitutesthecurrent. The carrier movements can be classiﬁed into the transport of the negatively charged electron and the positively charged hole under an electric ﬁeld, and the transport by carrier diffusion which intends to cancel any spatial inhomogeneity in the electron and hole densities. First, we shall consider the carrier transport via the electric ﬁeld. Imagine a semi- conductor that is connected to a battery as shown in Fig. 8.15. The right edge of the semiconductor is connected to the positive electrode of the battery and the left edge to the negative electrode. Assume that there is no potential barrier at the inter- face between the wire and the semiconductor, which enables a smooth movement of the carriers. This is called Ohmic contact. Because the electric ﬁeld is a vector that is directed from plus to minus, it is directed from right to left as shown in the ﬁgure by the red arrow. The negatively charged electrons are attracted by the positive 182 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors Fig. 8.15 Inside the semiconductor, the current is carried by electrons and holes. The current that runs through the conducting wire is carried by electrons electrode and therefore are transported toward the right side (i.e., opposite to the direction of the electric ﬁeld). In contrast, the positively charged holes are attracted by the negative electrode and move along the direction of the electric ﬁeld toward the left side. Because the deﬁnition of the current is the amount of charge transported per unit time, the ﬂow direction of the negatively charged electrons is opposite to the current direction. The electrons that have left the semiconductor at the right edge of the semiconductor ﬂow through the conducting wire (clockwise in the present case). However, the holes cannot run through the conducting wire. The holes in the semiconductor recombine with the electrons that enter the semiconductor from the wire (at the left edge in the ﬁgure), and thus, the hole is neutralized by the incoming electrons. All holes that have moved toward the left edge are ﬁlled with electrons and vanish. In other words, the current inside the semiconductor is carried by the electrons and holes, and the conducting wire carries the same current by means of electrons only. The current that is generated by such an electric ﬁeld-induced carrier transport is called drift current. The charge that passes the unit area per unit time as a result of the electric ﬁeld, that is, the drift current density J(drift), is given by the following equation: J(drift) = envde + epvdh (8.45) Here, we expressed the current in terms of n and p, which are the electron and hole densities in the steady-state condition. Further, vde and vdh are the drift velocities of the electron and the hole, respectively. If we denote the electric ﬁeld by E, we can express these velocities as μeE and μhE, that is, as multiplication of E by the corresponding carrier mobility. The mobility is a material property that describes how fast of a carrier can be transported under a given electric ﬁeld. By substituting these terms into Eq. (8.45), we obtain J(drift) =  enμn + epμp  E = σ E (8.46) which allows us to deﬁne the conductivity σ. The carrier mobility is reduced by scat- tering as a result of collisions between the conducting carrier and ionized impurities or also phonons, which are quantized lattice vibrations that depend on the crystal temperature. The mobility is proportional to the average time between two collisions 8.8 The Currents in a Semiconductor: Drift Current and Diffusion Current 183 and inversely proportional to the effective mass. In case of Si, Ge, and even GaAs, the electron mobility is larger than the hole mobility. Further, by increasing the impu- rity density, the average time between two subsequent collisions with impurity ions becomes shorter and thus the mobility decreases. The electrons in an n-type semi- conductor are called majority carriers, and the holes are the minority carriers. In contrast, in the p-type semiconductor, the hole constitutes the majority carrier and the electron is the minority carrier. We note that the contribution of the scattering via the impurity ions or phonons depends on the temperature. Upon increase of the temperature, the thermal motion of the carrier is intensiﬁed and its average velocity increases. Due to this, the scattering via the impurity ions decreases initially, but at higher temperatures the number of phonons increases and the resulting phonon scattering leads to a signiﬁcant decrease in the mobility. In the following, we analyze the other carrier transport mechanism, that is, dif- fusion. Diffusion is the transport of carriers from locations with high densities to locations with low densities. The resulting current is called diffusion current. The diffusion current is a current that is generated by the spatial density distribution and the thermal energy of the carriers only. We note that this current is signiﬁcantly dif- ferent from the drift current which is the carrier ﬂow induced by an electric ﬁeld. The current resulting from the density gradient of the carriers is a mechanism that is very important for the carrier transport in cases like solar cells, where the light incident on the semiconductor surface creates high carrier densities that are localized near the surface, or also the carrier transport in a pn junction diode under forward bias. Let us consider the minority carrier diffusion in a semiconductor slab that is illuminated by light from the left side as shown in Fig. 8.16. Assume that the light illuminates a p-type semiconductor. The ﬁgure illustrates that the electron density becomes smaller for larger x. We shall determine the charge that passes the cross section S at x0 per unit area and unit time, that is, the electron diffusion current density Je(diff). If we denote the mean free path of the electrons in Fig. 8.16 Minority carrier diffusion in a semiconductor 184 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors the semiconductor as l, and deﬁne t as the average time between two collisions, we obtain Je(diff) = e nL 2 Sl nR 2 Sl /St = e(nL nR)l 2t e l 2t dn dx l = e l2 2t dn dx = eDe dn dx (8.47) Here, nL and nR are the average electron densities within the regions deﬁned by the mean free path l to the left and right of x0. In this ﬁgure, the carriers ﬂow from the left side with high density to the right side with low density. When we consider that the carriers in an inﬁnitesimal small area diffuse homogeneously, we ﬁnd that half of the carriers diffuse to the left and half to the right. This applies to both the region on the left side of x0 and that on the right side and is the reason for the terms nL/2 and nR/2 in the ﬁrst line of the equation. Further, we deﬁned De = l2/2t as the diffusion constant of the electrons. We can also derive the hole diffusion current by the same approach. Jh(diff) = eDh dp dx (8.48) The Einstein relation provided below connects the diffusion constant and the mobility. De μe = kT q (8.49) Dh μh = kT q (8.50) Because the total electron current density Je as well as the total hole current density Jh is expressed as the sum of drift and diffusion current densities of the particular carrier type, we obtain the following equations: (Total electron current density) Je = Je(drift) + Je(diff) = enμeE + eDe dn dx (8.51) (Total hole current density) Jh = Jh(drift) + Jh(diff) = epμhE eDh dp dx (8.52) Consequently, the total current density J in the semiconductor is given by the sum of these equations as shown below. J =  Je(drift) + Je(diff)  +  Jh(drift) + Jh(diff)  8.8 The Currents in a Semiconductor: Drift Current and Diffusion Current 185 =  enμeE + eDe dn dx  +  epμhE eDh dp dx  (8.53) We note that in case of carrier injection, carriers may accumulate inside the semi- conductor and carrier annihilation through recombination can also occur. Therefore, the number of carriers entering the semiconductor is not necessarily equal the num- ber of carriers ﬂowing out. To analyze the temporal evolution of the carrier density distribution, we have to employ the continuity equation, which accounts for the carrier generation and recombination. By using the electron generation rate (the number of generation events per unit time) Ge, the electron recombination rate (the number of annihilation events per unit time) Re, the hole generation rate Gh, and the hole recombination rate Rh, we can write the continuity equations for the electron and hole as shown in Eqs. (8.54) and (8.55), respectively. n t = 1 e Je x + (Ge Re) (8.54) p t = 1 e Jh x + (Gh Rh) (8.55) Let us analyze the continuity equation for the case of electrons being the minority carriers. The electron density n can be divided into the density under thermal equi- librium, n0, and the excess carrier density n, and thus can be represented as shown in Eq. (8.56). n = n0 + n (8.56) By substituting Eq. (8.56) into Eq. (8.54), we obtain n t = n0 t + n t = n t (8.57) Here, we employed n0/t = 0, i.e., the fact that the density under thermal equilibrium is independent of the time. Before we compare Eq. (8.57) with Eq. (8.54), we have to specify the details of the electron generation and recombination processes. For the electron generation process, we consider the generation rate via thermal excitation, Gth,andtheopticalgenerationratevialightabsorption, Gopt.Additionally, we deﬁne τe as the minority carrier life time due to recombination. Using these deﬁnitions, we can write Ge = Gth + Gopt (8.58) Re = n τe = n0 τe + n τe (8.59) 186 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors By substituting Eqs. (8.58) and (8.59) into Eq. In this case, the bracket in the above equation has to be zero and the relation Gth = n0/τe holds. By substituting this into Eq. (8.60), we obtain Eq. (8.61) as the continuity equation for the electron. n t = n t = 1 e Je x +  Gopt n τe  (8.61) Similarly, if we consider the holes as minority carriers, we can derive the following continuity equation for the holes: p t = p t = 1 e Jh x +  Gopt p τh  (8.62) Now let us actually determine the diffusion length of the minority carriers in a semiconductor by employing the equations derived above. Consider the semicon- ductor slab shown in Fig. 8.16. We assume a p-type semiconductor and calculate the diffusion of the electron (minority carrier) under the steady-state condition. As above, light illuminates the left side of the semiconductor slab and generates elec- trons. For the sake of simplicity, assume that all light is absorbed at the surface and electrons are generated at the surface only. Because the light is completely absorbed at the surface, there is no generation inside the semiconductor, i.e., Gopt(x  0) = 0. Additionally, since no electric ﬁeld is applied the drift current is zero; Je(drift) = 0. Therefore, we need to consider the diffusion current Je(diff) only. In the steady-state condition, we have n/t = 0, and thus we obtain Eq. (8.63) from Eq. (8.61). dJe(diff) dx = en τe (8.63) From Eq. (8.63), we obtain Eq. (8.65). eDe d2n dx2 = en τe (8.65) 8.8 The Currents in a Semiconductor: Drift Current and Diffusion Current 187 Before we solve this second-order differential equation, we rearrange it as shown below. d2n dx2 = n Deτe (8.66) This equation describes the distribution of the excess carrier density n as a function of x. By using the boundary conditions n = n(0) at x = 0 and n = 0 for x , we obtain the following solution: n(x) = n(0) exp   x Deτe  (8.67) Here, Deτe is the so-called electron diffusion length, which is denoted as Le. Equation (8.67) describes that the excess electron density decreases exponentially as the distance from the light illuminated front surface increases (corresponding to larger values of x). The electron diffusion length Le monotonously decreases for larger impurity densities since the latter directly inﬂuences τe. For example, in a high-quality p-type Si crystal with a doping density of 1017 cm3, the diffusion length of the electron (which is the minority carrier) is about 100 μm. 8.9 The Quasi-Fermi Level In the above sections, we conﬁrmed that the Fermi level, which was introduced during the discussion of the thermal equilibrium, cannot be deﬁned anymore if excess carriers are generated by processes like photoexcitation. In such a non-equilibrium condition, we have to consider separate Fermi levels for the electrons and the holes. These are the so-called quasi-Fermi levels. There is no requirement to introduce any new concept for the deﬁnition of the quasi-Fermi level. Theenergylevel that servedas basefor boththeelectrons andholes in the semiconductor under thermal equilibrium is now simply deﬁned separately. For convenience, we repeat the deﬁnitions for the carrier densities under thermal equilibrium condition: n0 = NC exp  EC Ef kT  = ni exp  Ef Ei kT  (8.34) p0 = NV exp  Ef EV kT  = ni exp  Ei Ef kT  (8.35) If we deﬁne Efe and Efh as the quasi-Fermi levels of the electrons and the holes, respectively, the electron density n and the hole density p under non-equilibrium condition can be represented as shown in Eqs. (8.68) and (8.69). 188 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors n = NC exp  EC Efe kT  = ni exp  Efe Ei kT  (8.68) p = NV exp  Efh EV kT  = ni exp  Ei Efh kT  (8.69) The carrier densities can be expressed as the sum of the densities under thermal equilibrium and the excess carrier densities, i.e., n = n0+n and p = p0+p. The non-equilibrium electron and hole densities (n and p) presented above are exactly those introduced in Eqs. (4.11) and (4.12) in Chap. 4 with the subtle difference that the temperature T was that of the solar cell, T c. The quasi-Fermi levels can be straightforwardly derived using the above Eqs. (8.68) and (8.69). Efe = Ei + kT ln n ni (8.70) Efh = Ei kT ln p ni (8.71) From these equations, we ﬁnd that an increase in the densities of the excess carriers is accompanied by a shift of the quasi-Fermi levels. As the electron density increases, the quasi-Fermi level of the electron steadily approaches the conduction band. In contrast, the quasi-Fermi level of the hole approaches the valence band for increasing hole densities. In this way, the quasi-Fermi levels of electron and hole shift in opposite directions, and their energy difference becomes larger as the carrier densities increase. To understand the behavior of the shift of the quasi-Fermi levels, we shall analyze the changes in the quasi-Fermi levels that are induced by the generation of electrons and holes via photoexcitation. Consider once more the semiconductor slab shown in Fig. 8.16. Assume again a p-type semiconductor and that light illuminates the left edge of the semiconductor slab, generating electrons and holes. In the same manner as in the previous example, we shall assume that all light is absorbed at the surface and carriers are only generated at the surface. This situation is shown in Fig. 8.17. The minority carrier (electron) density decays exponentially for increasing depth, obeying Eq. (8.67). At the semiconductor surface where the photogeneration of carriers occurs, the excess electron density n and the excess hole density p are equal. Because the diffusion constant of the electrons, which are the minority carriers, is different from that of the majority carriers (holes), the depth dependence of p differs from that of n indicated in the ﬁgure. The photogenerated carriers cannot reach the regions deep inside the semiconductor via diffusion, and therefore, these regions exhibit a carrier distribution that corresponds to the electron and hole densities under thermal equilibrium condition, n0 and p0. In this case, the quasi- Fermi levels of electrons and holes can be deﬁned by the same value. Because the excess electron and excess hole densities are larger closer to the front surface, the quasi-Fermi levels exhibit a shift and separate as described by Eqs. (8.70) and (8.71). Because the presently considered material is a p-type semiconductor, we have p0 n0. Therefore, the contribution of the excess carriers to the total carrier density 8.9 The Quasi-Fermi Level 189 Fig. 8.17 Shifts of the quasi-Fermi levels of electrons and holes inside a p-type semiconductor under illumination with light is smaller for the holes. Accordingly, the shift in the hole quasi-Fermi level is also smaller than that for the electrons. To understand the physical meaning of the Fermi energy, it is worth to consider the free energy of electrons and holes in a semiconductor from the viewpoint of the thermodynamics and statistical physics. We deal with the entropy of electrons in a crystal. The entropy of a single electron is given by Eq. (8.72). S = 1 n  k ln w (8.72) Here, w is the number of states for the electron and is given by the FermiDirac statistics which is represented by the electron density n and the effective density of states for the electrons NC, when n NC, in Eq. (8.73). w = NC! (NC n)!n! (8.73) Using Stirlings formula given by ln n! = n ln n n, Eq. (8.73) can be expressed as shown below. ln w = NC ln NC NC (NC n) ln(NC n) + (NC n) n ln n + n = NC ln  NC NC n  + n ln  NC n n  n ln  NC n  , because of NC n NC (8.74) 190 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors By substituting Eq. (8.74) into Eq. (8.72), we obtain Eq. (8.75). S = k ln  NC n  (8.75) According to thermodynamics, the Helmholtz free energy of the electron Fe in a semiconductor is derived as Fe = EC kT ln  NC n  (8.76) Here, EC is the internal energy of the electron. On the other hand, since the electron density n is given by Eq. (8.68), the Fermi energy of the electron is expressed by the following equation. Efe = EC kT ln  NC n  (8.77) By comparing Eqs. (8.76) and (8.77), it is elucidated that Fe = Efe. That is to say that the Fermi energy corresponds to the free energy. In the same way, the free energy of hole is given by Fh = EV kT ln  NV n  (8.78) Thus, Fh = Efh. Under the thermal equilibrium condition, Fe = Fh because the sign of the free energy and the internal energy for the hole with positive charge are opposite to those for the electron with negative charge. Thereby, Efe = Efh in the electron energy. Conversely, non-equilibrium condition causes a splitting of the quasi-Fermi levels of electrons and holes. The split energy is the chemical potential which is deﬁned as the rate of change of a free energy of a thermodynamic system. 8.10 The pn Junction We ﬁnally ﬁnished the preparations for the discussion of the pn junction. The pn junction is the most basic structure that provides functionality for the implementation of semiconductors in devices. It is no exaggeration to say that this structure supports the whole semiconductor electronics industry. In the present section, we explain in detail what happens when a p-type semiconductor is connected to an n-type semi- conductor. The Fermi levels of the p-type and the n-type semiconductor under thermal equi- librium condition differ as described in Eqs. (8.36) and (8.37), respectively. We have already explained that the Fermi level at a ﬁnite temperature corresponds to the 8.10 The pn Junction 191 Fig. 8.18 Semiconductor pn junction under thermal equilibrium condition energy with an occupation probability of 1/2. From this, it can be concluded that even when the p-type and n-type semiconductors are connected, the Fermi level that extends over the whole junction has to adopt the same energy (this conclusion holds for the steady-state thermal equilibrium condition without current). This situation is depicted in Fig. 8.18, which contains information that is extremely important for the following discussion. Because the initially different Fermi levels of the p-type and n-type semiconduc- tors have to align at the same energy upon connection, the band in the vicinity of the connecting interface (i.e., the junction interface) has to shift. The potential step that is formed upon connection is called diffusion potential, which is denoted by Vb in the ﬁgure. This potential step is formed because the carrier diffusion that occurs in the vicinity of the junction interface results in a difference of the electrical potential. The energy is the electrical potential multiplied by the elementary charge e. The fact that a potential difference has formed at the junction interface is equivalent to the existence of an electric ﬁeld vector that is directed from the n-type semiconductor toward the p-type semiconductor. We note that we draw band diagrams in such a way that the upward direction corresponds to the direction of increasing energy. This has its root in Fig. 8.2, where we tried to draw the potential with respect to the elec- 192 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors tron. Consequently, when we explain the hole using the same ﬁgure, the direction of increasing energy is reversed; that is, a larger energy of the hole corresponds to a lower electron energy. This is the reason why the holes are always distributed around the upper edge of the valence band, which is the state with the lowest possible energy. The regions excluding the vicinity of the junction interface (where the potential gradient is nonzero) are called neutral regions because the spatially distributed number of ionized impurities equals the number of thermally activated free carriers; that is, these regions are electrically neutral. On the other hand, in the region near the junction interface there are no free carriers as a result of recombination. Therefore, the remaining impurity ions constitute a spatially distributed charge (a so-called space charge). These positive and negative charges to the left and right of the interface are balanced. The charge neutrality condition that is satisﬁed by the region with nonzero potential gradient can be written as eNDwN eNAwP = 0 (8.79) byusing ND fortheimpuritydensityinthen-typesemiconductor, NA,fortheimpurity density in the p-type semiconductor, and wN and wP. for the corresponding partial widths of that region in the n- and p-type semiconductors, respectively. As a result, the width of the region with nonzero potential gradient decreases for higher impurity densities. Figure 8.18 shows a diagram for a higher impurity density in the p-type semiconductor.InChap.7,wementionedthattheextensionofthisso-calleddepletion layer is extremely important for the solar cell characteristics, because it is directly related to the collection efﬁciency of the photogenerated carriers. The fact that under the thermal equilibrium condition, the carriers can stay in the steady-state condition (shown in Fig. 8.18) without any effective transport, means that the electrons in the n-type semiconductor and the holes in the p-type semicon- ductor cannot overcome the potential barrier of the depletion layer and thus cannot move to the other side. To explain this, we have to consider that the total current is the sum of the drift current and the diffusion current. In the situation shown in Fig. 8.18, the diffusion current, which induced by the carrier density gradient, perfectly can- cels the drift current induced by the internal electric ﬁeld at the junction interface. Consequently, a current starts to ﬂow if the potential barrier of the depletion layer is lowered by an external force. This can be realized by applying a negative voltage to the electrode of the n-type semiconductor and a positive voltage to the electrode of the p-type semiconductor, which is the forward bias condition. Vice versa, applying a positive voltage to the n-type semiconductor and a negative voltage to the p-type semiconductor is called reverse bias. In the latter case, the potential barrier increases further and the carrier transport stops completely due to the large impeding potential barrier. If light is incident and excess carriers are generated, these diffuse toward the pn junction interface, cross it, and are transported to the opposite region. Besides generating current, the charge transport also changes the electrical potential. Hence, electric power is generated. This is the operating principle of the solar cell. 8.10 The pn Junction 193 With the above, we sketched the physics of the pn junction roughly. Below we shall further specify the concrete behavior of the junction. First, we determine the actual value of the potential barrier in the depletion layer, Vb. We deﬁne EP as the energy difference between the Fermi level in the p-type semiconductor, Ef, and the energy of the valence band maximum, EV. Further, we deﬁne EN as the energy difference between the Fermi level Ef in the n-type semiconductor and the energy EC of the lower edge of the conduction band. Using the above deﬁnitions, we can evaluate eVb by subtracting the sum of EP and EN from the band-gap energy Eg; that is, eVb is given by Eq. (8.80). eVb = Eg (EN + EP) (8.80) By using Eqs. (8.23) and (8.24) for the electron and hole densities under thermal equilibrium condition, we obtain the following relations for the temperature regime where the impurities are completely ionized: EN = EC Ef = kT ln NC nN0 = kT ln NC ND (8.81) EP = Ef EV = kT ln NV pP0 = kT ln NV NA (8.82) The subscript 0 was introduced earlier to indicate a carrier density under the thermal equilibrium condition. Here, we added the subscripts N and P in addition to the 0, in order to distinguish the carrier densities in the n- and p-layers. In other words, nN0 denotes the electron density in the n-type semiconductor under thermal equilibrium condition, and nP0 (used below) denotes the electron density in the p- type semiconductor under thermal equilibrium condition. By substituting the above Eqs. (8.81) and (8.82) into Eq. (8.80), eVb can be represented as eVb = Eg (EN + EP) = Eg kT  ln NC ND + ln NV NA  = Eg kT ln NCNV NDNA (8.83) From this equation, we understand that the energy barrier of the diffusion potential approaches the band-gap energy if the values of ND and NA get close to NC and NV, respectively. To proceed, we employ n2 i = NCNV exp  Eg kT  (8.84) and obtain 194 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors Eg = kT ln NCNV n2 i (8.85) By substituting this result into Eq. (8.83), we arrive at the following equation: eVb = kT ln NDNA n2 i (8.86) Next, we derive concrete expressions for the potential gradient at the junction interface, the change in the electric ﬁeld, and the depletion layer width. The potential gradient of the pn junction in Fig. 8.18 was drawn with a curvature that actually has an important meaning. This curve can be obtained by solving the Poisson equation, which relates the electrical potential V (x) with the charge density QV. The Poisson equation reads d2V (x) dx2 = QV ε (8.87) Here, ε is the dielectric constant of the material, which is the dielectric constant of vacuum, ε0, multiplied by the relative permittivity εr; ε = εrε0. By using the boundary conditions V (xN) = 0 and V (xP) = Vb, the solutions of Eq. (8.87) for the electric potential V (x) in the n- and p-type semiconductor regions become as shown in the following: V (x) V (xN) = eND 2εrε0 (x xN)2 xN x x j (8.88) V (xP) V (x) = eNA 2ε (xP x)2 x j x xP (8.89) In such a way, the potential gradient in the region near the junction interface can be expressed with a quadratic function. In Fig. 8.19, we clarify the difference in the behavior of the potential gradient in the p- and n-type sides by using separate colors. Because the potential energy of the electron is eV (x), the gradient of the band in the most upper diagram of Fig. 8.19 is reversed with respect to the electric potential V (x). If we employ the condition NDwN = NAwP for charge neutrality together with Eqs. Further, the electric ﬁeld reaches its maximum at the junction interface x = xj. This behavior is also shown in Fig. 8.19. 8.11 CurrentVoltage Characteristics of a pn Junction We have already mentioned that the current ﬂowing in a semiconductor is the sum of drift current and diffusion current. In a pn junction under thermal equilibrium condition, these currents perfectly cancel each other. If forward bias is applied, the potential barrier at the junction interface is lowered. Then, the electrons ﬂow from the n-type region into the p-type region while holes ﬂow in the reverse direction, from the p-type region into the n-type region. In this way, the diffusion constitutes the dominant carrier transport in a pn junction, and therefore, the drift current of the minority carriers is not considered. Using Eq. (8.66), we can describe the excess carrier diffusion from the n-type region into the p-type region by Eq. (8.99). d2n dx2 = n L2e (8.99) Here, Le is the diffusion length of the electron. We shall assume that the positive direction of x is along the direction of the current ﬂow. By solving the above equation using the boundary conditions n = 0 for x and n = n(xP) at x = xP, we can derive the electron density nP(x) in the p-type semiconductor region. nP(x) = nP(xP) exp  xP x Le  (8.100) Hence, the electron diffusion current is given by Eq. (8.101). The excess electron density that diffuses inside the p-type semi- conductor is nP(xP) at x = xP, but inside the p-type semiconductor the density exponentially decays with a characteristic length scale Le (the electron diffusion length) as the distance from the junction interface further increases. Similarly, the excess hole density that diffuses inside the n-type semiconductor is pN(xN) at x = xN. Also, this carrier density exponentially decays (inside the n-type semicon- ductor) with a characteristic length scale Lh (the hole diffusion length) as the distance from the junction interface increases (see Fig. 8.20). Since the total current in a pn junction under forward bias condition is the diffusion current entering the transition region between xN and xP (we assume zero recombination inside the depletion layer), we obtain the following from the above results: J = Je(diff)(xP) + Jh(diff)(xN) = e  DenP0 Le + DhnN0 Lh  exp eVa kT  1  = J0  exp eVa kT  1  (8.119) For the last equality in Eq. Equation (8.119) is known as the pn junction diode equation. In Fig. 8.21, we show the calculation result based on this equation. As explained in the outline of the pn junction, an applied forward bias leads to a reduction in the potential barrier of the depletion layer. Consequently, the electrons ﬂow from the n-type region into the p-type region (via diffusion), and on the other side, the holes ﬂow from the p-type region into the n-type region (via diffusion). By this mechanism, the current can quite abruptly increase like water that has been initially stored in a dam and then is released. J0 is the so-called dark saturation current, which can be rewritten as shown in Eq. (8.120). Because it is proportional to the intrinsic carrier density n2 i , it actually depends on the temperature. J0 = e  DenP0 Le + DhnN0 Lh  = en2 i  De τe 1 NA +  Dh τh 1 ND  8.11 CurrentVoltage Characteristics of a pn Junction 201 = e  De τe 1 NA +  Dh τh 1 ND  NCNVexp  Eg kT  (8.120) In the reverse bias regime, a leak current corresponding to J0 ﬂows through the pn junction. If reverse bias is applied, the potential barrier increases and the transport of the carriers across the junction interface as outlined above will be inhib- ited. Therefore, under ideal conditions, a constant value of J0 is maintained for a wide range of reverse bias voltages. However, if an excessively high reverse bias is applied, a tunnel current starts to ﬂow at the depletion layer. Furthermore, the resulting large potential drop induces the carrier multiplication phenomenon, which leads to a signiﬁcant increase of the reverse current. Besides the diffusion and tunneling currents discussed above, the generationre- combination current speciﬁed below is also present at the junction interface under forward bias condition. The transition of an electron in the valence band into the con- duction band constitutes the generation process and results in a free electron. The recombination process refers to the recombination of the electron in the conduction band with a hole in the valence band. In the generationrecombination process, the electrons in the conduction band and the holes in the valence band move simulta- neously. Therefore, the effective potential barrier for the generationrecombination process is half of that for the diffusion current. The current density that accounts for this generationrecombination current is given by Eq. (8.121). J = Jdiff + JGR = J0  exp eVa kT  1  + JGR0  exp  eVa 2kT  1  = JS  exp  eVa nkT  1  (8.121) In the last line of Eq. (8.121), we introduced a new factor n to collect the terms for thediffusioncurrentandthegenerationrecombinationcurrent.This n istheso-called diode quality factor, which indicates the quality of the diode, that is, how close the actual diode characteristics are to those of the ideal diode described by Eq. (8.119). n assumes values between 1 and 2, and a value close to 1 means that diffusion current is dominant. If it is closer to 2, the generationrecombination current is dominant. This generationrecombination current is an important mechanism that inﬂuences the current characteristics of semiconductor devices with different materials such as the multi-junction solar cell presented in Chap. 6. The characteristics of the generationrecombination current appear under low forward bias voltages, and as the bias voltage is increased the diffusion current becomes dominant, i.e., n approaches 1. However, upon the generation of intense currentsbyfurtherincreasedvoltages,thebiasvoltageisalsopartiallyappliedtoother regions beside the depletion region. Since the contribution of the series resistance 202 8 Fundamentals of Semiconductors piles up in this case, the slope of the currentvoltage characteristic decreases in accordance with Ohms law. If sunlight is incident on a pn junction, excess carriers are generated which then diffuse, and this results in the generation of current and voltage. We have already mentioned several times that this is the operating principle of the solar cell. In the concluding remarks on the pn junction, we have to point out one important detail regarding the solar cell. The FermiDirac distribution expresses the occupation prob- ability of the electron under thermal equilibrium condition. We mentioned that the Fermi level is the energy level with occupation probability 1/2. However, under the non-equilibrium condition that occurs upon the generation of excess electrons and holes by incident sunlight, we have to consider separate Fermi levels for the electrons and the holes. These quasi-Fermi levels have to align with the Fermi levels in the metallic electrodes, which are attached to the pn junction on both sides. This means that the extractable voltage is equal to the difference in the quasi-Fermi levels of electrons and holes. This difference in the quasi-Fermi levels of electron and hole is usually smaller than the band-gap energy. In the completely ideal case introduced in the course of discussing the SQ theory, we assumed that the voltage for elec- tricity generation is equal to the band gap. But in order to obtain characteristics that are more realistic, these quasi-Fermi levels were introduced in the detailed balance model.
